A Neo-Leitesian, Psycho-Cultural Analysis of Chinese Movies
Mie Augier & Jerry Guo
This is a draft - for discussion with AWM and AM - do not circulate etc
"The ways in which movies pattern the expression of impulses such as
those of hostility which are strongly interfered with in real life can yield
many clues to an understanding of the culture in which the movies are
produced. There are many different ways of interfering with the all-out
hostility, which would make social life impossible. These ways of
repressing or rechanneling hostility will depend on the image of what
would happen if hostility were fully unleashed. Movies show us what
some of these images are" (Wolfenstein & Leites, 1950, p. 306).

0. ABSTRACT AND EXECUTIVE SUMMARY
"The scope of Nathan's accomplishments includes not only the work on the Soviet Union
...
but also his work toward the end of. his life on theological trends on the last 150 years
and the elaboration of some conjectures concerning the life of Michelangelo. Early on, one
of the more interesting and rewarding of Nathan's products was his book with Martha
Wolfenstein on the movies. This delightful work, more accessible than most of Nathan's
writings, had for me a double reward. The first was its intellectually fascinating
comparative analysis of the themes and the contrasting archetypical characters in ...
movies. Second, because of his analytical structure that one now carried around in one's
head, even poor movies were made interesting"
- Andrew Marshall, 1988, p. 29-30
This is the preliminary result of the ongoing research and analysis, trying to apply some
of the ideas and frameworks in Nathan Leites' approach to understanding other cultures,
countries, and organizations - in this case, focusing on some themes and trends in
Chinese films. An 'application' of Leites"ideas could have focused on trying to apply one
of his works on the Soviet-style approach to the Chinese context, but Marshall suggested
early on to focus first on the topic of movies - to see how the categories and themes
discussed in Leites' movie analysis do (or do not) fit Chinese films (a quick summary
illustration is in the table below), and try and find some preliminary patterns and how
they may reflect issues in Chinese society. At first, 100 modern Chinese films were
watched; summaries are included. This present report is an attempt to extend this analysis
to also include a) older Chinese films and:b) references to modern western ones, as
suggested during discussions with Marshall, to try and understand and illustrate the
continuing relevance of Leites' work and ideas.
.
oversight over the film process from script-writing to the final cut. Examining themes that
exist - and themes that do not exist - can help us understand messages the Chinese
government want the people to see. At the very least, all themes reflected in films are
neutral to the interests of the Party or the government, while at best they reflect the ideals
of what the Chinese government hope for the people to believe.
Sample
We examine 121 Chinese films from 1935-2012; covering the whole span of the Chinese
film industry. Films were selected for their cultural and historical significance (in the case
of early films, as judged by Chinese film scholars), as well as for their total viewership.
We hoped to identify films that most Chinese watched or would watch, so as to maximize
their value in our analysis.
Broad Themes
Although a full description of themes is not possible in this space, we provide some
insights from our analysis here.
First, revolution as a means of social change is a repeated theme across all periods of
film. Leftist and Communist films in the early film period suggest that the ills of society
are best cured by a total transformation - a revolution. This continues through the 20th
century, as the Communist Party justifies the revolutionary periods after the rise of the
Party, and even suggests that future revolution may be necessary.
Much revolution is directed against foreigners, or Chinese parroting foreign interests.
Foreigners are not to be trusted in Chinese film; westerners particularly are shown as
greedy, evil, and rapacious, with interests, tetally contrary to those of the Chinese people.
Foreigners are shown as needing to be removed from China, and that China should be left
for the Chinese.
Authority, and strong authority, is thought of as a stabilizing force that fights against
chaos. China is portrayed as being so large and unruly, with such a large population, that
it requires high authority to be stable. Corruption is a problem for authority, but
corrupting influences are commonly capitalism or foreign influence. Sacrifice and duty on
the behalf of revolution or authority is viewed as noble and necessary.
Social norms are portrayed as changing rapidly as China has opened up and modernized.
Film is ambivalent about modernization, there appear to be great benefits in raising
quality of life, but detrimental effects as well, such as conflicts between individual
obligations to work and obligations to family. Many characters across films are portrayed
as trying to cope with a new world. Capitalism, in Chinese film, is a double-edged sword.
Relation with modern China
How does this analysis shed light on issues facing modern China?

rosca:
Also included, as appendixes, are a list of some further readings into the areas of using
movies to understand society, as well as a mini review of John Weaklands' work in the
area. Both indicate possible extensions to the' present analysis. As Marshall mentions in
the quote above, Leites' work on movies is fascinating not just because it opens an
important window into understanding a society's values and dynamics; but also because it
makes even not too interesting films worth seeing (and even for people not terribly
fascinated with movies to begin with!).
EXECUTIVE SUMMARY
Introduction
Throughout his career, Nathan Leites did significant work examining mass psychology
and how it manifested in leadership and management styles. Leites captured the "spirit of
the ruling class" through his work in operational code analysis and his extensive
examinations of Soviet style in warfare and management. His work was not limited,
however, to the elite. His book with Martha Wolfenstein, Movies, examined themes and
characters in American films, and provided a comparative psycho-cultural analysis of
American, British, and French film.
In this study, we take a "Leitesian" approach to studying Chinese film. We examine over
100 Chinese films from 1934 through'2012 The rise of China is one of the most
to the !
interesting and challenging problems of the 21 f.century. How will China manage its
rise? How has China's rise affected the Chinese people? How will China's rise affect its
relations with its neighbors and other world powers? We believe a psycho-cultural
approach, examining China's cultural artifacts, will help shed light on some aspects of
these questions, as well as open research directions to help students of China's rise better
understand what the future might bring.
The Psycho-Cultural Approach
Why a psycho-cultural approach? We view psycho-cultural analysis as an important part
of the analyst's toolkit. Psycho-cultural analysis aims to capture the spirit of another
culture, as informed by psychological analyses of cultural, historical, social, and political
artifacts. The objective is to understand a society, to understand how it functions, what is
culturally and psychologically important, what motivates individuals and collectives, and
how leaders and followers interact. The approach blends aspects of sociological and
psychoanalytical techniques to understand the underlying spirit of the society. Nathan
Leites, in his many works examining Soviet and other Communist societies, was a
pioneer of this approach.
Leites' most widely known works involve detailed analyses of Soviet leadership groups.
His monographs, The Operational Code of the Politburo, Soviet Style in War, and Soviet
Style in Management provided insight into not just how the Soviet leadership thought
about problems, but how they went about addressing them. His early work spawned an
entire field of operational code analyses, although the modern work focuses primarily on
.
psychological profiles of political leaders following George's (1969) re-invention of
operational code methodology.
By turning back to the original thinking behind psycho-cultural analysis, and focusing on
a Leitesian style of analysis, we hope to shed light on change in Chinese society. We
believe this has security implications. China faces a number of challenges as its economy
matures. Chinese leaders will need to decide how to address both external and internal
challenges. Chinese society, more broadly, plays a role in bringing some challenges to
leadership attention, and then reacting to leadership initiatives. If, as students of China's
rise, we hope to understand how China will manage its rise and what it will address,
considering psycho-cultural artificats can help shed light on the spirit of Chinese society.
What are Chinese people concerned with? What stirs emotions? What motivates them to
work together, and what motivates them to stay independent? These questions might be
answered, in part, by performing a Leitesian analysis of Chinese society.
These questions have obvious defense implications Although China's government is
democratic, it is still beholden to the broad masses of society - both in spirit as a
purportedly Communist entity - and in practice as needing broad societal support for
legitimacy. What is represented in Chinese psycho-cultural artificats affects and is
affected by government action. One aspect of understanding the future of Chinese
government plans within China and in East Asia more generally might be to
understanding the psycho-cultural factors entering decision-making.
Film as Cultural Artifact
Is film one such psycho-cultural artifact? We believe it is. Leites and Wolfenstein
conducted a book-length study of American, British, and French film in the first half of
the 20th
century. They suggested that society expresses itself in all aspects of life, and
film is one important cultural component. Film expresses social and cultural values,
beliefs, and emotions, as well as, as a surface-level, a broad description of the themes,
characters, and plots that inhabit daily life: Film is an abstraction of life, and it can be
considered one way of better understanding Chinese society.
Chinese film is a particularly relevant for understanding society. We believe Chinese film
contributes to our understanding at two levels.
First, Chinese film can help describe many of the societal facets we have mentioned.
Themes and plots from film are reflections or mirrors of not just daily life, but the
aspirations and hopes of the Chinese people. Examining the heroes of film helps us
understand who the Chinese believe should be heroes; examining the villains show us
who the Chinese are wary toward or distrust. We hope to shed light on some aspects of
Chinese society by examining the themes, plots, and characters that cut across films, and
how they evolve over time.
Second, China's government has a remarkable degree of control over the film industry.
All films released in China are first reviewed by the Chinese government, who have
: :
First, many of these themes reflect changes in modern Chinese society, particularly
exposure to western culture and society. Chinese citizens and government accept
superficial quality of life improvements, and seem to espouse it in film, but they never go
so far as to embrace democracy or actual western values. Westerners are still viewed as
untrustworthy and counter to Chinese interests: There appears, however, to be some
growing liberalization with respect to elements of social changes like homosexuality,
suicide, and depression, which are discussed in film.
Films might reflect Chinese social mood. There appears to be an underlying optimism
about China's place in the world - characters in Chinese films know that China is on the
rise and are eager for China to assume its rightful place after centuries of humiliation.
With this, they know that China will face many challenges and agree that a high degree of
authority and government control is necessary for these challenges to be overcome.
Chinese core values have not changed. Duty, loyalty, care for family, and the desire to
strengthen China are all core values that show up across all periods of film. The PLA is
shown as a universally loved institution, PLA soldiers can do no wrong and are always in
the right, supporting the Party and the people - which are shown to be the same. The
people's war concept shows up in certain films; as does the idea of resiliency - that China
is a resilient country that has endured for thousands of years and that will continue to
endure in the future, regardless of western actions. China's standing as a major player in
the world is viewed as an inevitability, and although outsider powers may take steps to try
to prevent it, nothing can stop China from' assuming its place of leadership.
Conclusion
Film is a powerful medium to explore society. As students of Chinese society, interested
in the ramifications of China's rise in the 215 century, we feel that there are many lessons
to be learned from a psycho-cultural examination of Chinese film.

1. A "Neo-Leitesian" approach to the analysis of Chinese movies
Nathan Leites did important work during the Cold War on the understanding of
Soviet behavior, and various studies have been made trying to apply some of his insights
to other (post-Cold War) opponents. Leites' studies of the operational code have spawned
a wide literature examining operational code of various world leaders (George, 1969; see,
for example, Schafer & Crichlow, 2000). [More - MA]
There are many possible lessons of Leites work for understanding China. One aspect
could be to develop an 'operational code' for the Chinese; another complementary
approach could be to outline what the parallels to Leites' books on "Soviet Style" in war
and management would look like. But also outside of Leites' work aimed at
understanding the Soviets can we find fruitful lessons, for his whole approach reflected
his particular unique awareness of historical, social, political, cultural, psychological and
other aspects of society and how it is reflected in, and influences, political and strategic
behaviors. Leites himself outlined part of this in his article, "Psycho-Cultural Hypotheses
about Political Acts" (Leites, 1948). One could even imagine learning valuable lessons
from a Leitesian understanding of Chinese art / artists, following how he analyzed
Michelangelo (Leites, 1986). The evolution of the themes in his work reflects at least in
part the strong curiosity he had; never feeling constrained to work within one or just a
few disciplinary perspectives, as the world rarely fitted. Schlesinger noted that he found
Leites to "immensely and widely curious .... something of a refreshing contrast to much
of American intellectual life, which was marked by a high and undue degree of
parochialism" (1988, p. 56). In a sense, he took an interdisciplinary spirit and carried it
further than most - even further than Herbert Simon ('further' here meaning stretching
interdisciplinarity, not in a career sense): "Too many academics tended to lose interest
when conversation strayed beyond rather stereotypical boundaries, and would excuse
themselves (at least to their own satisfaction) by unapologetically stating: 'this is not my
field' (Schlesinger, 1988, p. 56). There remains great potential to learn from his individual
works as well as his approach.
This present report aims to develop one 'application' of Leites ideas by applying
his analytical method in "Movies", or more specifically, the approach he (and Martha
Wolfenstein - his wife at the time) took in their book and associated articles. This aspect
also might lead to fruitful insights for our understanding of China.
The main work of their collaboration was a book-length study by Martha Wolfenstein and
Leites published in 1950. There are also a couple of associated articles that featured
aspects of their study. And Leites of course' remained interested in the influence of
psycho-cultural aspects of society, and therefore the use of psycho-cultural analysis to
understand the world, throughout his life.
An important insight from Leites (not just in "Movies" but also in other areas of
his work as well as implicit in his approach to the topics he was researching) is that
society expresses itself - or is reflected in - all aspects of life, including economic,
cultural, social, psychological, religious, artistic, and other aspects. At the level of
individual decision making, Leites studied Michelangelo's art and life and found themes
in his work that reflected not only his own history and experiences but also to an extent a
'collective unconsciousness' of the society and times in which he lived (Leites, 1986).
Movies can then be seen to also express social and cultural values, cultural and social
beliefs in a sense, as anthropologists, psychologists and psychiatrists have pointed out.
Leites approach was, as Schlesinger notes, an illustration of Leites' 'sophisticated form of
content analysis' (Schlesinger, 1988; p- 57)
...
:
į
:
Leites' work (and way of working) and early intellectual interests
Leites's collaboration with Wolfenstein and his work on movies came at a period of his
life where he had already made importanticontributions to content analysis under the
influence of in particular Harold Lasswell, and early mentor of Leites. [MA - short on HL
mentorship and their joint work].
He already was interested in psycho-analytic approaches - as a way of study and as
useful for personal development (as evidenced in his correspondence with Lasswell early
on). He also seems to have developed - perhaps more than Lasswell early on - an interest
in the psycho-cultural aspects and the cultural anthropology work of Margareth Mead and
Ruth Benedict.
[ma - write early NL / ML relations and their work before they met - including a bio note
(remember the piece that the archivis from NY PI wrote about Martha]
Also mention their awareness that movies a moving object of analysis:
"Twenty years ago it was easy to discern differences between the films
of the .. cultures we compared. .. Film making has since tended to
become an international mélange, and the question arises to what extent
films now express a more inclusive western, or world culture, to what
extent traditional styles retain their distinctive qualities. Whether in a
cross cultural frame of reference, or one of merging cultures developing
over time, the problem remains of analyzing the variations of the basic
human themes" (Wolfenstein & Leites, 1970, the preface to the
paperback edition of their book).
2. "Content analysis of Movies": Movies as Windows into Society And
Culture
"In the late forties ... Nathan and his wife devoted a year to seeing
countless movies, American, British, and French. After these rather
varied and extended encounters, they had written a splendid diagnosis of
the divergences among these principal national cultures. Each nation's
films reflected the presuppositions the values, and above all the hopes of
the society - and these varied sharply. From this survey, it was made
---- ---
quite clear how the movies simultaneously both revealed and reinforced
the underlying attitudes in the different nations. It was a good illustration
of Nathan's rather sophisticated form of content analysis. Earlier than
most, Nathan had come to understand what a powerful medium motion
pictures (and subsequently television) represented. .... He understood
even then that to a large extent the message was shaped by the
medium" (Schlesinger, 1988, p. 57).
In their joint work on movies, Leites and Wolfenstein take a psychological and psycho-
analytical approach, discussing movies as reflecting the emotions and beliefs in the
societies / audiences which they address, emerging out of the day-dreams of the writers
which themselves are emerging out of a collective social and cultural consciousness or
life; thus the note:
"Where a group of people share a common culture, they are likely to have
certain day-dreams in common. We talk for example of the American dream, of
which success in the sense of continually rising to better jobs and higher
income, and the acquisition of gleaming cars and ice-boxes are components.
The common day-dreams of a culture are in part the sources, in that the
products of its popular myths, stories, plays and films. Where these productions
gain the sympathetic response of a wide audience, it is likely that their
producers have tapped within themselves the reservoir of common day-
dreams" (Wolfenstein & Leites, 1950, 13).
Thus, the themes and plots of the films 'of a particular era or culture have particular
characteristics that one can analyze and use as window into societal and cultural values.
And although the relationship between movies and the societies or collective
unconsciousness they reflect is probably a lot more ambiguous and complex,
understanding movies might help us understand the cognitive and interpretive
frameworks that they represent, because the larger cultural and societal patterns reflect a
share awareness or experience in a society. In Leites' words, "The ready-made day-
dreams of movies are thus related although in quite complicated ways to the larger
pattern of the culture of which they form a part"? (Wolfenstein & Leites, 1950, p. 307). So
rather than seeing movies as representing directly an accurate reproduction of "manners
and customs" in society they "find in these ready-made day-dreams an interplay of real
life experience with the hopes, and, fears it awakens, the wishes it encourages or
rebukes" (p. 306).
The larger academic literature
Leites and Wolfenstein's approach to movies is one among other approaches to movies as
windows into society. Indeed, in the decades since Leites and Wolfenstein's work, the
study of movies as academic and scholarly topic has changed from ranking lower than
"such acceptable relaxants as detective mysteries" [!] (Jarvie, 1979, p. 374), to becoming
acceptable amongst scholars of various academic tribes, including sociologists,
historians, art-scholars, psychologists, psycho analysts, philosophers and even
economists. In addition, the study of film-making and its cinemagraphic details has
become a rather big area itself; some would say at the expense of more important study
areas. As one critic commented rather cynically:
"Film scholarship has become a flourishing surrogate for a liberal arts
education. The film department has in fact become to academe in the
1970s what the psychology department was in the 50s - a way to
legitimize trendy mediocrity with a bachelor's degree. Students ill
equipped for either the jobmarket or a rigorous bacculalaureate can put
themselves on hold for four years, preparing themselves for a lifetime in
the dark. The graduate burdened with only one idea, namely Jean-Luc
Godard's claptrap notion that film is 'truth at 24-frames a
second"" (Dunne, New York Times Book Review, April 1, p. 11-12).
Some of the more interesting contributions emphasize or combine a perspective on
movies as windows into society (what movies represent), and windows on the movies
themselves (how movies are studied). Jarvie quotes an Oxford historian, "the silent films
from 1895 to 1927 recreate for us the society for which created them" to illustrate movies
as windows into societies and their histories (p. 375). Semiotics perspectives have
focused on the role of symbols, rituals, and stories as guides to action and called for
'semiotic awareness' in the viewing of films (Eco, 1977, p. 14), within which one can
miss the difference between 'things making film's' and 'films making things'. Another
scholar uses mostly a sociology perspective to discuss the role of movies in the teaching
of sociology, as well as some limitations, (if viewed as illustrations of 'sociological
reality', movies can become 'pedagogical booby traps') (Demarath, 19xx).
The traditions that Leites and Wolfenstein drew from - psychology and psycho analysis -
are the ones which also in the subsequent literature on movies seem most prominent.
Which means, of course, that the study of movies has been vulnerable to the ebbs and
flows of trends and fads within those perspectives (which Leites was very well aware of,
and published major work criticizing some fads in the psychoanalytic movement, cf.
Leites, 1971).
Psychology/Psychoanalysts Perspectives on Movies
The interrelations between movies and psychology/psycho-analysis can take place at
several levels / dimensions, and there is therefore different literatures on can trace on this,
such as the psychological themes / analysis represented in movies; the depiction of
psycho-analysts in movies; how movies have influenced psychology - psycho analysis
and vice versa; and how larger societal trends have influenced both. At the most basic
level, one can see movies as representing the society's 'collective unconsciousness'; and
both movies and psycho analysis have as focus the unconscious ways and representations
of human motivations, emotions, and desires.
The two big themes, psychoanalysis and movies, brought together in Leites and
Wolfenstein, although different (one being an intellectual movement/discipline, the other
a visual media), did overlap at times during historical evolution (Creed, 1998). They
were both born roughly at the same time (end of the 19th century), and one of their
earliest interrelations was during the surrealist movements in the 1920s and 1930s where
some films tried to capture Freud's perspectives on dreams through visual techniques
such as the slow motion and dissolving images, or drew on Jung's ideas on the centrality
of the 'archetype' such as the shadow (Creed, 1998, p. 77-78). Psychoanalysts also served
as consultants to filmmakers since at least the 1920s, and even earlier, in 1916, a Harvard
psychologist wrote a psychological study of movies suggestion how films transform the
external world into internal words through mechanisms of the mind such as imagination,
attention, emotion, and repression (Munsterberg, 1916).
Psychoanalysts have also written about the depiction of psychoanalysts in movies [ !! -
perhaps Leites would notice the irony here .. narcissistic tendencies of analysts?]. To
some extent Leites and Wolfenstein anticipated this in an article examining the role of
psychiatrists in movies. Notice how they focus on the general character elements and on
the role of cultural differences (not on the fact that they are psychoanalysts per se):
"psychiatrists in films assume a variety of aspects. The various roles
revolve around the chosen way of dramatizing guilt. In American films,
guilt is dealt with by projection and denial. The good psychiatrist is the one
who collaborates in this solution, relieving the hero of mistaken self-
accusations and the suspicions of others evolved by misleading
circumstances, and helping to pin the blame on someone else. The bad
psychiatrist works in the opposite way, falsely incriminating the hero and
aggravating his suspicions about himself. The effort to establish an image
of a benevolent psychiatrist does not seem to have achieved a stable result.
The good psychiatrist (who tend to become effectual) is haunted by his
opposite, the malign and dangerous one, and more or less unwittingly plays
into his hands. In British films, which are concerned with the struggle to
control one's own destructive impulses, the psychiatrist appears in the
series of tragic heroes who are overwhelmed by such impulses, especially
in the direction of destroying women. The psychiatrist is a reproach-laden,
inadequate father-figure who fails to provide a model for the control of
destructiveness" (Leites & Wolfenstein, 1951, p. 26-27).
Almost five decades later, Gabbard noted how movies have indeed become part of the
collective unconsciousness: "Films have become a storehouse for the psychological
images of our time. They serve many of the same functions for the contemporary
audiences as tragedy used to serve for fifth-century Greeks ... Not only do they provide
catharsis, they also unite audiences with their culture through their mythological
dimensions in the same way the Aeschylus or Sophocles provided vision for the citizens
of Athens" (Gabbard, 1997, p. 429).
Another trend is that - perhaps due to the success (and therefore narrowing) of the
discipline(s) of psychology/psychoanalysis, much of the literature on psychoanalysis and
movies seems to look for the use of psychoanalysis within movies, in essence
approaching the world in the same imperialistic way economists have been accused of
(and critiqued for): seeing only the parts of the world that fits one disciplinary
background.
Some have mentioned how movies - and the larger institution of Hollywood - have
served as the cultural anthropologists of our time, with films "tapped into the commonly
held unconscious wishes and fears in the mass audience" (Gabbard, 1997, p. 430). Others
have speculated about directors' intentions, motivations, and identifications, trying to
understand, for instance, the male-female relations in many of Hitchcock's films
(Modelski, 1988). Or that the emergence of science fiction films such as "Alien" in the
late 1970s and 1980s captured a societal 'common development crisis' experienced by
moviegoers; in the case of "Alien", an object created to represent societies "extraordinary
paranoid anxiety" (Gabbard & Gabbard, 19xx). Others have examined the representation
of certain psycho-analytic concepts, such as repression and displacement, in movies and
how they come to life for movie viewers through cinematic techniques.
Analysts of Jungian orientation have pointed to movies as representing basic collective
unconsciousness, and further, how movies, by evoking emotions, 'instinctive, involuntary
reactions which upset the rational order or consciousness by their elementary
outbursts' (Jung, 1939, p. xx), can be source of power, fear, and reassuring feelings.
According to Jung, the unconscious is normally contained by the conscious, by
repression, or other mechanism, but when it flows into dreams, free associations, or
emotions we are not always in control. The whole setting of cinema invites the lowering
of the consciousness, as it provides darkness, warmth, strong images, and sounds. Movies
allow viewers to live the flow of unconsciousness, to fuse with the characters, identify
with the heroes (or the bad guys) - but the length of the movie limits or contains the
unconsciousness' freedom to play and the loss of control is therefore limited, and can
vary in intensity due to level of sound, intensity of images, etc, as well as technical
choices such as camera angles (Beebe, 1996). Some symbols stimulate further
unconscious processes in the viewers - extending the field of people's consciousness by
extending the known or understood beyond what's commonly accepted, changing
perceptions from one psychological sphere to the other - the 'transcendent function'.
Another topic in Leites/Wolfenstein was the analysis of particular characters in movies;
Gabbard (19xx) mentions this has fallen out of favor in recent decades and has been
replaced instead with emphasis on the function of characters in the film maker's overall
narrative - but it is precisely the characters and the relationships between them that Leites
saw as central. So one of the broader thematic changes from the time where Leites wrote
is the move from understanding the central characters and relationships between them (as
they represent societal trends), toward analysis of the function of the characters within
movies themselves. This may also have to do with the increasing success and
commercialization of the movie industry, helping to shift focus from movies as
representations of (and hence, windows into society), to more instrumentalist focus.
A few concepts used. As mentioned, within the broader perspectives, some have written
on the analysis and application of specific psychoanalytic concepts. A few examples:
Collective Unconsciousness
The idea of movies as representing societal collective unconsciousness is relevant to
understanding trends of national-cultural nature, focusing on "the activity of national
audiences and the conditions under which they make sense of and use the films they
watch" (Higson, 1989, 36). Key to unlocking and understanding this window into society
through movies becomes then to focus on the overall common style or worldviews of the
movies; the representations or 'projections" of national character they show, and how they
display the idea of national character in the films and in the viewers (Ibid).
Active Imagination
The concept of active imagination also comes from Jung and refers to a process that leads
patients to increase unconscious thoughts and decrease of the conscious control, allowing
images and symbols to develop from the unconscious content: "Active imagination is a
matter of allowing the natural mind time and freedom to express itself
spontaneously" (cited in Izor, 2000, p. 279),,Movies can then be seen to represent the
virtual representations of active imaginations of the film-makers; and can affect the
imaginations of the movie viewers as they engage with the symbols and stories in the
movie that opens the door for the role of the motivations and intentions of the film maker.
Leites (and Wolfenstein) also mention this aspect, in the context of discussing how such
intentions to change audiences can also backfire.
The newer literature also emphasize how the viewing of movies also reflects the
psychological processes of the audiences; thus discussing why, for instance, people are
attracted to the horror-science-fiction-type movies (Alien is a prime example), when they
know such movies do not reflect reality. Some find that it is because such movies bring
people in touch with their primal fears, and that the viewing of them reflects children's
experiences of their mothers as alternating between good and bad (the demarcation line
between the two represented with the transformation of a good person into a bad
'alien' (often looking identical to the good one as the body is used) - a mechanism that
probably also a applies to non-alien 'bad' characters. The representation of nurturing
figures turning bad sometimes evokes aggression (trying to destroy the monsters that
changed the good/nurturing character into a bad one), seen to represent left-over
childhood anxieties about own aggressive tendencies (Izod, 2000).
Symbol Play
As movie viewers may begin to internalize some of the anxieties projected from a movie,
they can also see it as symbol play, another Jungian idea: "To think of symbol play is to
set screen narrative in a highly productive context in the Jungian schema, for Jung
himself knew well the creative potential to play, He found it allowed him to discover a
way forward through periods of loss and direction and mental crisis" (Izod, 2000, p. 268).
Movie viewing can also become part of a larger 'play'; largely unconscious play with the
symbols, their creation and their understanding - sometimes using symbols as
mechanisms to defend against issues such as separation, anxiety and fear. The imaginary
world that movies provide can give rise to additional imaginations and imaginary worlds
in the minds of the viewers through the engagement of play and symbols.
Once again, this is an example where the literature captures some mechanisms that are
probably very sound and that Leites would have probably found valid - there are symbols
used in movies and people understand and construct meanings through symbols in
different ways.
Psychoanalytic scholars on films have also discussed personality concepts such as
'narcissism' [elaborate - mention' -wall street/the devils advocate as modern example of
a narcissistic character who may also represent a larger trend in society at that time -
80s-]
Summary of Section: From Neo-Meta-Post-Leitesian to Neo-Leitesian analysis ..
"When clarity is so hard to achieve, obscurity may come to appear
as an advantage one claims rather than a defect one strives to reduce.
'The term identification', Loewald announces, 'is used here in ... loose
fashion, so not to prejudge what might be implied in the concept' -
which is presumably as harmful as disclosing one's fallback positions is
foolish when negotiation. From the plain fact that many and obscure
meanings are currently being given to our worlds - 'when we ask for
precise definitions ... of incorporation, introjection, identification .... We
are not .... Likely to come up with .. precise agreements' - Berenzin
seem to conclude to the propriety of this situation: 'we have to leave
these useful construction's in some kind of hazy balance of
understanding' - a contention that might sound less plausible were it
made starkly: we ought to leave these words hazy" (Leites, 1971).
Leites was not a person whose writings area easy to understand; yet, in the context of the
modern literature on psychoanalysis and movies, his critique of psychoanalytic literature
makes a lot of sense: if one avoid fluffy words, and excessive use of difficult concepts,
one can get to understand some very basic and sound mechanisms about human behavior.
But sometimes the words scholars use adds confusion.
Reading the psychoanalytic literature on movies with Leites' critique of psychoanalysis in
general in mind makes it possible (or at least easier it seems) to focus on what he
probably would have: the basic sound insights that are in that approach, and what they
tell us about basic human behavior and relationships. That is, keeping in mind and
avoiding some of the traps within the literature itself; most of that has to do with keeping
a sound empirical focus and avoid getting lost in the field's centripetal tendencies - as
illustrated for instance with these quotes:
.
Not losing one's empirical focus when words become explained with other words
instead of with reference to reality "Renaming more or less plainly described
event by a 'concept' may give the impression of having explained it' (Leites,
1971, p. 132).
·
Avoid defining a concept with another concept, making a word salad instead of
....
insight: "In the rare case when a definition is given, it may consist in replacing a
word by a nobler synonym" (p. 200).
·
As the field has progressed it has become increasingly inward looking, assuming
progress is made: "Increasingly so, as time passes: 'progress' in psychoanalytic
theory had led to 'a better integration, and even clearer connection' of its parts: a
point repeatedly asserted, but not proved, not even illustrated" (p. 165).
Interestingly, this is not limited to the field (psychoanalysis) that was the focus of
Leites critique; it is probably a more general characteristic of fields that as they
grow and become 'successful' (in terms of ideas taking hold), they become
increasingly narrow, inbred, leading to (at best) short term success of the subfields
that result, but at the expense of the health of the long term development of the
field, and at the expense of empirical insight. Thus the field of psychoanalysis has
developed different subspecialties, talking within themselves, such as a feminist
wave, structuralist wave, post modern, etc.
With Leites critique in mind, the basic insights from psychoanalysis, such as viewing
films as the society's collective unconsciousness; representing emotions and
psychological dynamics through characters and interactions remains.
2. The Leites Approach
"Many of the situations with which we deal, situations
provided by thousands of years of human history, can never be
replicated in laboratory settings. But with properly collected,
annotated, and preserved visual and sound materials, we can
replicate over and over again and can painstakingly analyze the
same materials. As finer instruments have taught us more about
the cosmos, so finer recording of these precious materials can
illuminate our growing ' knowledge and appreciation of
mankind" (Mead, 1975, p.110)0;1:
[MA to develop short section on their collaboration and MW's summary of method in
movies analysis in the mead book]
The original Leites and Wolfenstein book on movies focused in particular on
relationships and has four parts: the first dealing with movie patterns / themes of lovers
and loved ones; the second with parent and child relationships; the third with killers and
victims (violence and crime); and the fourth with relationships between performers and
those who observe them. Their sources include over 200 films (American, British and
French) released in the year September 1945 - September 1946. They looked for
regularities and varieties in the recurrent themes, plots and characters (for example, a
hero that appears to be criminal but is not); the psychological interpretations of the
emotional and psychological significance of those themes (for example, the extent to
which movie plots that see the hero's hostility as result of society is reflection of his
projecting his own hostility towards a threatening father figure); and they relate the move
themes to the cultural patterns in American, life (for example, an American expectation
that sons will achieve more than their fathers and thus not look to him as a model).
Throughout their study they compare American films with British and French examples.
For example, British movies have more focus on violence originating from inner forces,
whereas American movies focus on heroes being threatened by external forces, and the
French emphasize the irony of human justice. They avoid judgments in presenting the
comparisons and have a chapter summarizing the key plot configurations that
distinguishes each category of movies. Using such a psycho-cultural method (or psycho-
analytic-cultural analysis is probably a better term) their findings are full of insights and
understandings about the characteristics of the movies and the societies and cultures they
represent. Perhaps not surprisingly (given that human nature has evolved over centuries
and don't change fast), many of their insights are still very relevant; and as a lens for
viewing movies, remains very interesting.
Main Relationships, and character dynamics
Lovers and loved ones. Lovers and loved ones is the first major theme that Wolfenstein
and Leites discuss. They see the issue of love as having changed somewhat from earlier
(US) films, from no longer being characterized by vamps and seduction (as depicted from
19th century literature) and "sexual wickedness", to being more about "wholesome
love" (p. 19). But the "nice girl" image is made ambiguous by pairing it with crime, bad
men, or shady environments: "This is essential to her attractiveness", Wolfenstein and
Leites finds, "it relieves her of the dullness of the obviously good girl" (p. 20). Central to
this is that her shady characteristics that may at first attract the hero to her, are only just
appearances and that in the end she is the same good girl that she was underneath it all
("The potentiality which she reveals in the end was always there, only the hero was in
doubt about it, and it is this which has made her interesting"). The authors find this to be
an "eat-your-cake-and-have-it-too" solution to the paradox of sacred and profane love,
resulting in what they call the "good-bad-girl" character that they find in American films.
Women may appear bad at times, but they are generally not, and often the movies are
about uncovering the proof that she is really: a good person, and break down the false
appearances.
Aightsean
Likewise, they find that the 'seducer' who formerly preyed on women is not out as a male
movie characteristic, because the current women are now too knowledgeable to be easily
seduced. But out is also the male protector, and Wolfenstein and Leites find that in
current (as of 1950) movies, love issues are mainly framed as female functions and
characters, with the good-bad-girl character showing both sides:
"[T]wo current love requirements, which in part conflict with one another, find
satisfaction in various film heroines. There is on the one hand the impact of
what we call goodness morality, which leads to high estimation of the charms
of wickedness as well as to guilt about pursuing them. The good-bad girl
represents a solution to the problem which goodness morality poses to the
:
man. On the one hand, expressing a more recent trend, there are the demands
of what we call fun morality: you've got to have fun (whether you like it or
not). If you are not having fun, you must ask yourself what is wrong with you.
The strength of impulse, which seemed so assured when faced with the
barriers of goodness morality, often dwindles before the imperative of fun
morality. A relatively new type of heroine has appeared to help the man over
this difficulty. She boldly takes the initiative in love relations and assures the
man of her confidence in his masculinity even when he is not proving it. She
estimates appraisingly the quality of pleasure produced by a kiss, but does not
seem to demand any all-out letting go of emotion, which might be difficult to
achieve. Thus she approaches sex with arman's point of view, helps the man
who is inhibited when confronted by an excess of femininity, and makes the
requisite achievement of fun seem not too much of a strain" (p. 21-22).
The development of the heroine in US films, to be almost more competent than men also
has destructive sides (they tend to be controlling and have tendencies to direct the lives of
their men) but the destructiveness does not always break through. But much more than
being just about female domination, Wolfenstein and Leites find it is also that the female
arranges things in order for her man to be successful (seeing his talent and helping him
get credit for his merits). Women must be careful though not to use her 'superior
competence' in too independent ways or she will be penalized (p. 22).
In contrast to this conception of women in US films, Wolfenstein and Leites note that
love relations in British and French films often display a different and contrasting picture;
in particular, the tension between good or bad girl tends to be more tragic (p. 22-24): the
bad woman retains her charm and the man suffers disappointment through her, idealizes
her but then discover, painfully, her badness. This essentially reverses the order in the US
films where the bad images are first and the real underlying heroine emerging only
gradually. Thus, Wolfenstein and Leites noter 14
-
"French films dramatize the breaking down of the ideal image of the
woman, American films, the justification of this image. British films in
contrast to both American and French tend to see women more as possible
victims of men's violence or betrayal" (p. 23).
British films also give more emphasis on the dual potential of men as both rescuers and
attackers, thereby giving more value to the unexciting but wholly good man than the US
films (they point to a recurrent theme in British films with a girl strangled in the park or
driven to suicide by her violent man). Essentially, in British films the male character has
much of the ambiguity that the female character possesses in US films.
Another theme is the endurance of 'true love,' if it is really true love. Wolfenstein and
Leites find that it remains 'as if it has been wrapped in cellophane and kept in the
freezer'. If feelings do change in movies, it was not real love to begin with. There is thus
little risk of suffering through true lovehin American films, whereas in British and
especially French film, this is not so, and lost opportunities between lovers can lead to
profound sadness.
Within the general topic of 'lovers and loved ones', Wolfenstein and Leites consider
several sub-themes and characters, including the different types of women pictured; the
transformation of feelings; the educational role of the female hero as taking initiatives in
love relations; and the general attitudes about loved expressed in movies.
Love occupies the attention of a great number of Chinese films. These are few deeply
romantic Chinese films (Love for Life, a love story about AIDs victims, and The Road
Home, a retrospective look at love in a, ruralfivillage, are notable exceptions). Chinese
films depict men as pursuers and women as the pursued, and they are all good girls (Go
LaLa Go!). The vamp does show up (Lust, Caution), but Chinese filmmakers have taken
a cautious approach in depicting romance;in film by idealizing it, by stylizing it as a set of
ritual encounters (Dear Enemy):' Love does end - breakups and separations are shown
throughout films, with dire emotional consequences (The Breakup Club); however,
despite challenges, if the couple felt true love, they would get back together and work out
their differences (If You Are the One 2). In this conception of love, there are few problems
that cannot be surmounted.
Sometimes, love can arise out of unusual circumstances. In Red Sorghum, the two
protagonists meet out of chance. A woman, betrothed to the owner of a sorghum wine
distillery, does not know what to do with herself once her husband dies. A peasant,
formerly part of her marriage procession, ingratiates himself with her - and the two fall in
love. Love in this example came out of opportunity. Love in romantic comedies tends to
come from unusual circumstances - happenstance meetings that develop into something
more, or friendships that turn into romantic relationships. One of the most popular films
in recent cinema history, If You Are the One, the lead male character becomes a
millionaire from swindling an investor and then posts an online dating ad to find a wife
and settle down. While he dates a wide variety of women, he ultimately ends up falling in
love with an airline stewardess reeling from a relationship disaster of her own.
With respect to the conception of women in Chinese film, it is in general closer to what
Wolfenstein and Leites suggest was portrayed in the United States. In If You are the One,
the filmmaker presents bad images - divorce,
Vorce: breakup, emotional trauma - and over the
course of the film (and its sequel), portrays the heroine as a complex but troubled figure,
who requires the help of a man for the underlying goodness in her to come out.
When the heroine takes on a lead role - an action role - she does not have time for love.
In The Red Detachment of Women, the heroines are a company of female guerilla fighters
on Hainan. The fighters operate in an all-female unit, although they take male direction
from superior officers. The lead female fighter is a diehard Communist, and when she
interacts with her superior officer, it is clear to the viewer that she admires him not just
for his values, but because there is a romantic element as well. However, she suppresses
those thoughts and focuses on the fight. In Liu San Jie, a film about a female folk singer
who battles rapacious landlords, the idea of love does not enter at all. When women have
a great deal of agency, they seem to move away from love and focus on themselves.
In somewhat contrast, modern US movies often portrays the heroine as being capable of
both a lead role and a female involved in romantic relationship (reflecting probably at
least in part the increasing role of women in the work place the last decades), although
there are of course complications such as career/romance trade-offs often being perceived
as being more prominent for women. An example of the latter is the a heroine in the
long-running TV show "NCIS" - the first female director of the agency - who had a brief
romance with one of the heroes before becoming director and is mentioned as having put
aside romance "because it didn't fit into her 5 year career plan". Some movies also have
female heroes that put their mission ahead of romance, or sometimes part of the plot is
that the death of a romantic friend entangles the female hero (more or less ancidentially)
even more in the mission. [mention other examples of the evolution of strong females -
seem to start in 80s and move articulated through the 90s- some, not all, are god/bad
girls; some are good/good MA]
The Good-Bad Girl
..
The dilemma of choosing between a good or a bad girl is a reflection of a major problem
in love-life in western culture, Wolfenstein and Leites find (p. 25), because the sexual
attractions a man feels towards a bad woman is hard to reconcile with the affection he
feels for good women ("a good girl is the sort that a man should marry, but she has the
disadvantage of not being sexually stimulating", p: 26). The paradox has several solutions
(sacrifice one side of the impulse over the other or combine them, sequentially - having
bad women before marrying a good one - or simultaneously through having wife and
mistress). And it is these efforts to solve this paradox and combine the types that produce
some characteristic feminine types, such as the American character who appears to be bad
(promiscuous or deceiving) but is really good. However, the bad images often prevail
until the end of the movies ("Till the End of Time" is one example). This character type
they find is a replacement for earlier images of female characters as 'vamps', a
transformation they illustrate with the change in spy movies from WW 1 to WW 2. An
early female spy seduced men from the other side and betrayed them, until she fell in
love with one and had to be killed by her employers. By contrast, WW 2 female spies
tended to be "clean-cut American girls doing a patriotic job" and are in love with men
from their own side (p. 34).
While the American style of integrating good girl with bad girl is to start with a good girl
and "spice her up" (p. 39), resulting in goodness concealed for a while behind a façade of
badness, French films tend to focus on the bad girl and when there is a conflict between
choosing a good or bad girl, the man chooses the bad (sometimes ennobling her, or
transforming her through love), sometimes through good first impressions, thereby
reversing the order of American films. Thus, whereas American films try and integrate the
goodness and badness, taking a good girl and spicing her up - French films do the
opposite. And British films seem to be less concerned overall with the goodness or
badness of the girls, focusing instead on the possible destructiveness in their men's
attitudes towards women. The women are mostly good but seduced by darker men with
destructive feelings who drive them to insanity or despair.
The good bad girl does not show overtly in most Chinese films. In most films, the
girls are purely good - they are chaste, caring, support their families, and support their
.. ..
partners (Eat Drink Man Woman). They focus on goodness in women and how women
can bring comfort to shattered lives (Ocean Heaven). They also show how women nobly
sacrifice themselves for the greater good (The Flowers of War). Where vamps exist, they
exist as an homage to old American, films (Shanghai); these films are set in 1930s
Shanghai, set in saloons and casinos, where the characters wear evening dress, drink
champagne, and converse in a manger similar to what a westerner would think from an
old American film like The Maltese Falcon. Or they show up in espionage films (The
Message). But by and large, goodness drives Chinese women and their goodness
brightens the lives of their men (Hot Summer Days) - this drives men's love, not
attractiveness or sexual stimulation, but inherent goodness.
Where the good bad girl does show up, it shows up in independent film. In Xiao
Wu, the title character - a pickpocket - enters a relationship with a karaoke girl who
seems bad, but whose badness is revealed to be a veneer over a good heart. She cares for
and is cared for by Xiao Wu, and Wu's drive to continue stealing is in large part due to
her. But independent films do not represent the mainstream of Chinese cinema, so the
extent to which this is applicable to broad analysis of film is unclear; generally, the good
bad girl is not a player in Chinese cinema.
Some of the earlier Chinese film from the "Golden Age" of cinema could be
characterized as good-bad girls, but due to the paucity of portrayals of love in general in
Chinese cinema, it is hard to relate this directly. Women are certainly portrayed,
generally, as good. In The Goddess, the filmmakers go through great pains to show the
goodness of the main character, who does traditionally bad things - prostitution - but all
to support her young son. Even in Springhin a Small Town, where a woman slowly falls
out of love with her husband, she is not portrayed as being good or bad, but a victim of
circumstances. Women, in love, have less agency - men push love forward, so it is
difficult to say that women act in a good or bad manner with intentionality. They act
based on the circumstances in which they find themselves, and men react and push based
on that.
What is not at all clear is which represents the "true" window into Chinese
society. There are a number of possibilities. One possibility is that the state-controlled
images of good-bad girls are the true reflection of society - this would imply that the
state's efforts at promoting certain types of political or moral messages have a direct
effect on the Chinese people. The Chinese people would then be conditioned such that the
good-bad girl would no longer exist overtly, and the state-controlled films would reflect
true reality. Another possibility is that the independent films reflect reality - the good-bad
girl is merely repressed by censorship, and the Chinese people do desire to portray the
character in films. Which perspective is "true"
truer is impossible to say - it depends on the
extent to which the analyst believes the; good-bad girl is an inherent psychological
character-type inherent in all societies, as well as in how much the analyst believes the
Chinese government is successful in it's moral and political conditioning through media.
[The good girl/bad girl still seem to exist in US movies [ma elaborate- e.g. Black Moon
Rising (1986), others]. There are also movies where women who appear as the good girl
is, in fact, the bad girl (in Acts of Contrition, the female character who plays victim most
of the movie is, in fact, the killer; but the good girl image prevails until late in the movie).
The Transformation of Feelings & Repeated Love
In European films it is taken for granted that love can come and go, and that one
can fall in love several times with different people, but in American films Leites and
Wolfenstein find there is less tendency to 'run out of love' for someone that is really the
right one to begin with: "If the once beloved person ceases to give pleasure there is apt to
be a complaining conviction that this could not have been the right one" (p. 47). Love is
then seen as a static quality that does not change through time. Wolfenstein and Leites
find the belief in static love probably a result of tradition of monogamy combined with
demand for constant pleasure (p; 48), in addition to the American denial of "the
evanescent aspect of life". At the same time, the characters of the loved ones lack
individual idiosyncrasy, although they deny replaceability of loved ones, indicating that
earlier relationships were not really with loved ones. If woman do love twice it is
sometimes because her first love was a mistaken one (p. 53). And widows often go
through struggle to allow themselves to love again a new man, which she can only do
when she has detached herself from her first love. True love never dies, but a beloved one
may die (they discuss several war-widow themed movies).
In British films, however, Wolfenstein and Leites see a possibility that true love
does indeed die sometimes. For example, a hero spy changing his identity and be reported
dead and his wife, initially sad, falls in love again with a man she doesn't confuse with
her husband or tries to make different from him. When her husband shows up she resents
his deception and looses her love for him. People can be genuinely in love with different
people, even when the loved one is not dead, but the wife simply falls in love with
someone else. Wolfenstein and Leites make an observation about the differences in how
movies portray middle-aged people: In the US there are "no middle aged people and, if
there were, they would have no emotional lives"; only young love exist and it lasts
forever (p. 58).
Love, in Chinese film, follows the (older) American model portrayed by Leites
and Wolfenstein. When one falls in love, one will stay in it. Despite some films that
portray affairs (Cell Phone), the implication is that the couple was never in love in the
first place - that is to say, marriage is not love, but a meaningful relationship that
underpins marriage is love. This, as well, i's similar to the (earlier) American mindset.
When recovering from a breakup, a woman might find the true love that she has been
waiting for all along (Love is Not Blind). And even after affairs, occasionally, couples can
get back together after finding they truly loved each other (The Law of Attraction). One
can, however, love more than once. Widowers, for example (Ocean Heaven) can find
love from sympathetic onlookers.
Love can more complex than simple heterosexual relationships. In Farewell My
Concubine, the two male leads have an arguably romantic relationship - or at least the
drama associated with it. One character fell in love with his opera partner and stayed in
love, while his partner was portrayed as philandering with women - as he was expected
to do. This is a unique case that was banned in China upon release.
Love is consistent. When a husband and wife are in love, it takes quite a bit to
break them apart. In The Horse' Thief, a portrayal of minority life in rural Tibet, even after
the titular character has been exiled from his village for horse theft, his wife goes with
him to an uncertain end. While love can be forced - for example, the arranged marriages
in Red Sorghum and Yellow Earth - when it takes hold, it stays.
In more recent movies, US films seem to have moved closer to what Leites and
Wolfenstein saw as characteristic of European films; people fall in and out of love,
relationships are dynamic, changing and resulting in complex extended families, (at
times) difficult tensions, but above all, blended families - reflecting probably the
increased tendency for men and women to divorce and remarry, in the US and abroad. It
is of course not always perfectly synchronized, leading to occasional heartbreak when
one lover moves on before the other; but it is often temporary and often a point later is
that both parties were better off with someone else. Even this very serial-but-ambiguous
characteristic of love has been the very focus of movies; the movie Hitch for instance is
about a dating consultant who makes orchestrating romance for others his business (until,
of course, something goes wrong and he himself. falls in love); Blended builds its whole
plot on the two main characters journey. to find each other (after being divorced / a
widower). Sometimes the processes of un-loving is depicted too which also can be seen
as an illustration of greater social accept of people loving more than once (and sometimes
loving more than one at the same time!).
Educational Quality
The superiority of women in movies'i's also seen to have an educational quality or
function, not so much in terms of the fact of social ascent alone (as one could perhaps
have expected at the time they wrote), since women advance in status due to their
abilities, but rather how women achieve improvements in position.
Wolfenstein and Leites find that women use their superiority harmlessly and
benevolently, and they help set the stage for men's rise in achievement: "She does not put
him into a passive receptive position, but arranges things so that he may realize his best
potentialities" (p. 61) and even have stimulating effects on their men. There is therefore a
tendency to deny any danger to men from female domination, by emphasizing her skills
rather than dominance, and with the men pleased by their achievements too. Manipulative
women appear mostly in comedies and mostly in an ironic way. Women also do not force
a demand for success on their men, but rather, guard and help them. Wolfenstein and
Leites link the theme of females helping or enabling male success to Margaret Mead's
observation that mothers in the US are concerned with the achievements of their children.
They are focused less on helping children, and more focused on what they can achieve;
they arrange situations that can help children experience success (p. 65): "The fact that
the situation has been arranged by the mother is not stressed; the emphasis is rather
placed on the fact that the child has accomplished something by his own efforts. This
emphasis is reflected in the American belief that one can succeed on one's merits if one
only gets the breaks, coupled with the belief that the favorable situation will somehow
resent itself" (p. 65). In other cultures children are expected to learn through failures or
protected from trying out their own strengths, and they do not have benevolent arranging
mothers.
Only rarely do men create opportunities for their women. When skilled women
are dependent on men, it is not because the men are superior, but because the women
need them for one reason or another. By seeing women with cultural demands and men
with biological needs, this aspect / theme. also reverses the earlier trend in movies with
female vamps distracting men sexually .: Wolfenstein and Leites also consider several
movies where the strong woman is a psychologist / psychiatrist helping men to recover
from mental confusion; him being dependent on her as patient but her on him because she
is in love with him (occasionally this can have destructive consequences, p. 72).
Sometimes women use physical force, and men their pawns with destructive
consequences ("Weekend at the Waldorf"),; marking a rare break of seeing female
dominance as harmless ("The Sailor takes a Wife"). Only very rarely is the man seriously
damaged by this ("The Macomber Affair") - which illustrates what Wolfenstein and
Leites see as a "pervasive trend" in American films: to deny dangers. There is no
suffering through love, suffering through sadness, jealousy or man made hostilities as
results of love and dominant women.
Wolfenstein and Leites also find a tendency for women to approach men in a
masculine way ("To Have and Have Not"), and for women to use a man's technique,
presenting men with images of themselves, and combining masculine and feminine
attractiveness. This type of woman also often takes initiative in sexual relations. But
women are seen to have to prove their masculine attitudes, while men are assumed to
possess them naturally (P. 77). They discuss how women make first contact through
offering males lifts in cars ("Blue Dahlia") or 'by taking initiatives at parties ("Till the
End of Time"), repeating otherwise masculine techniques. She also is less likely to be
overwhelmed by emotions and has a more detached attitude towards, for example, kissing
than was portrayed in earlier movies of the 1930s (p. 79-81). They note that the female
with a masculine approach makes the men feel accepted instantly, reflecting the image of
a woman compensating for (the authors quote Margaret Mead again) a mother's
"conditional love": "American children are often weighed and measured, and awarded
Mother's love to the extent that they compare favorably with others. The fantasy of the
immediate and unconditional award of love by the movie heroine seems related to the
suspense and uncertainly of this childhood experience" (p. 83). Such masculine-feminine
women do not always have the appearance of bad girls. A contrasting figure, of a woman
as being obsessed only with sex, they find in 'the bitch,' who never lets her men feel
good.
An early line for initiating conversation in US movies was to ask "Are you cold?"
A variation of this Wolfenstein and Leites find still present. They discuss the ways in
which first encounters happen between the hero and heroine through indirect devices for
achieving contact (incidental, etc), as well as more deliberate approaches, both of which
they find as related to the difficulties in US films of being impulsive; few couples or
lovers have much capacity for 'emotional exuberance' (p. 87). Accidental circumstances
may lead to intimacy but impulsiveness; is not a characteristic in most cases.
Occasionally, strangers even facilitate the intimacy. By contrast, they find that French
films present an opposite picture, with circumstances being manipulated consciously to
initiate intimate contacts. French films thus more readily embrace human impulses while
also at the same time acknowledging the potential disastrous feelings resulting from
unanticipated love, although both US and French films then seem to want to get rid of
impulsive feelings:
"Where French films may demonstrate the disastrous consequences of
being carried away by impulse, American films are more likely to show he
happy results of impulse within bounds. Thus both French and American
films seem to express distrust of impulse, but in different ways: the French
by facing the danger, the American. by denying it" (p. 89).
In discussing missed opportunities they note that these rarely are seen as permanent
losses in American films, whereas in French films such missed opportunities are more
likely to lead to regret (p. 92). British films too present lost opportunities as
'irretrievable' (p. 93) but with the difficulties arising from the intensity of impulse.
Female superiority in Chinese film is intellectual. They are level-headed,
determined, and have drive - in contrast to their boyfriends (A Place of One's Own). The
heroines are confident and capable, and while there are a multitude of examples of men
doing the same, it is evident that women (in modern depictions) have rising status and
capabilities commensurate with China's rise and opening (Sophie's Revenge). They can
even manage households or business (Red Sorghum; A Woman, A Gun, and a Noodle
Shop). Men seek to serve them (If You Are the One; A Place of One's Own). Yet, they are
loyal to their men (Serfs; The Horse Thief), most of the time (Springtime in a Small
Town). In Chinese films set in modern China, women are almost always shown as career-
focused and with a clear role in mind; they push their men forward, or drop them (Qin Jia
Guo Nian). They find men who are equally driven and passionate (A Beautiful Life, Great
Wall My Love). Women rarely overtly fail (The Postmodern Life of My Aunt), but when
they do it is because of a man.
Women are occasionally shown in traditionally masculine roles as well (Red Cliff). In
these roles, women become martial arts heroes (Bodyguards and Assassins). They fight
more elegantly than men, but fight alongside them in some cases rescue them. In cases
where they take violent roles, they play a traditional female character with elements of
violence, which is jarring and rare (The Sorcerer and the White Snake). They might also
become resistance fighters against the Japanese - in overt resistance or in espionage
(Shanghai).
Women are shown to have an active role in conflicts against China's enemies. They might
be victims (The Flowers of War), who suffer from foreign invaders, or they may be
resistance fighters (The Red Detachment of Women; The East is Red). The implication for
women - as well as for men - is if they do not seize agency for themselves, and to take
their future into their own hands, they will fall victim to outside forces that will not work
in their favor.

Love without emotions/feelings?
Wolfenstein and Leites end the section about love by discussing that, since in American
films, suffering is seen as pointless, movies do not tend to represent emotions deep
enough to lead to despair if lost: "There is a tendency for the feelings to become quickly
detached from a loved one who has proved disappointing by not loving in return, or by
dying, or by turning out to be a criminal ... There is thus little danger of being dragged
down by love for an unworthy person; love is awarded to the person who deserves it" (p.
94-95). Similarly, they avoid lovers suffering through uncertainty about each other or
temporary absence. Emotional vulnerability: is rarely shown - thus 'dangers' in US
movies tend to result from external violence rather than inner conflicts:
"The threat of falling hopelessly in love with someone bad or
exploitative or unloving, and so having one's life destroyed, seems
unreal. The shot in he dark, the beating in the lonely shack in the woods,
or the false crime accusation - these are the images of danger. In
American films, men and women may, and often do shoot each other.
They do not break each other's hearts" (p. 99).
This is not the case in Chinese film. While the end-state of love is eternal happiness, there
is great drama on the way there. Breakups, affairs, deep discussions about life directions,
separation due to circumstances, and other factors all stand in the way of love, and in
overcoming them characters feel despair. But after despair, the light of the end of the
tunnel becomes happiness (If You Are the One, If You Are the One 2). In Shanghai, a spy
film about late 1941 Shanghai, the primary male and female characters only find love
after enduring a Japanese invasion. In Red Sorghum, love comes out of suffering - out of
the suffering and uncertainty a widow feels after the death of her husband, and they find
happiness (until the Japanese invasion). The entire plotline of Farewell My Concubine
revolves around the suffering that arises from unrequited - in this case, homosexual -
love. The Wedding Banquet tells a story of pain arising from homosexual love from the
perspective of a westerner trying to break into a Taiwanese family. In Frozen, a suffering
artist's girlfriend much watch his: self destructive tendencies, while still trying to hold
onto their love. Drama, suffering; and sadness characterize the formation of love, which
makes its eventually attainment all the more meaningful.

through (and from) uncertainty [examples] [MA: do half page on how this holds for US /
