Parents and Children
The second theme of relationships and characters Leites and Wolfenstein consider
is that of family ties. In general they find that American movies tend to see heroes as
unbound by family ties and that more than half of the heroes have no relations. Among
the relations that are portrayed, the most frequent ones are between heroes and in-laws
and children, reflecting the US emphasis "on the family you make" and not the family
from which you come (p. 101). If children are 'present they almost always influence how
:
..
1
the plot unfolds - something they attribute in part to American emphasis on rivalry with
age-mates and older generations fading into the background. Female heroes more often
have ties to family through their fathers.'In contrast to these trends in American movies,
British and French films more often have family relations, with the French showing
women more bound by families than the British ones (p. 102).
The images that movies present of the variety of family relationships and the
quality of feelings and behaviors between family members also have differences. While
there is immortality in presenting sons, US films tend to present families in terms of
clearly delineated generations, and not a continuous series of stories through life (p. 103).
Thus they find a significant difference between US and French films in the role of the
older fathers, who tend to be central characters in French films, shown with an active
personality and particularly involved with raising children, whereas American movies
present fewer relationships between generations:
"Where parents or parent substitutes play a more active part, the children
tend to effect a quick and complete emotional detachment following a dramatic
break. The tragic tradition of Oedipus and Hamlet, where the son's life is
overshadowed by his father's death, is not perpetuated. In British films, on the
other hand, a son may blame himself for his father's death and devote his life
thereafter to self-imposed penance! In: American films there seems to be less
guilt as well as less positive feeling towards the antecedent generation" (p. 104).
Wolfenstein and Leites link the fact that children are not involved in long-term
relations with their parents to another basic trend in American films - the tendency of
characters not to be so strongly attached, emotionally, in love relations. Love can be
withdrawn and re-directed if needed, disengagement is relatively easy, and feelings are
relegated into the background. In American movies, the relegation of parents to the
background facilitates "the displacement to other persons of unresolved feelings towards
mother than father" (p. 106), attempting to reduce the image of father-son rivalries.
However, Wolfenstein and Leites find that it still shows up - especially in dramas where
the hero is involved in conflicts with an older boss.
American films also assume that children are innocent, and that they learn bad
impulses from their parent figures, possibly representing an interaction of the
immigration tradition with the culture left behind and the children successful in
emancipating themselves from that cultureranditheir parents. One way in which movies
portray the hero achieving emotional independence from family is by projecting emotions
onto others. The often violent impulses of sons towards fathers find a reverse expression
with a hero under attack by his boss; which makes the hero free from blame as he was
attacked by outside forces (in contrast to British films). Rivalry between siblings occurs
more often between sisters than between brothers, and also here, the hero is protected
from guilty feelings due to outside forces driving events. When, in dramas, there is
danger involved in fulfilling certain wishes, it is often accomplished through denial and
projection: "The impulses in one's own nature which may lead to danger and self-
defeating conflict are ascribed to other characters. Dangers from outside are thus
substituted for dangers from within" (p. 109).
1
Parents play a crucial role in Chinese film - in Chinese culture, the nuclear family unit is
the foundation for social and economic life, so to the extent to which films might
reinforce these values, parents are shown as pillars in the lives of their children. Large
numbers of films show children making sacrifices for the sake of filial piety (Great Wall
My Love, A Simple Life, The Road Home), which others show parents making sacrifices
for their children (Sunflower, Aftershock); Parents support children, they provide them
with houses (Qin Jia Guo Nian), money, advice, and their children disobey them at their
peril. In A Simple Life, the mother figure is introduced; when a man's maid (who has
raised him from childhood) falls ill, he takes ever effort to make her comfortable just as if
she were his mother.
In Chinese-language films produced outside of the mainland, there is some
resistance to this traditionalism, especially in the early 1990s. In The Wedding Banquet,
the main character has to reconcile his marriage to his gay lover (and impregnation of
their mutual friend) with his traditional Taiwanese parents. In Eat Drink Man Woman, a
Taiwanese film, three daughters actively resist their father's attempts to teach them - and
scorn him for his romantic choices.
For those who disobey their parents (or those parents who have no children), there
are dire consequences. The absence of the strong parent-child relationship leads to grief
and despair. In The Postmodern Life of My Aunt, an old woman with a distant daughter
suffers mayhem at the hands of scammers in Shanghai, being eventually forced to retire
to Anshan with her daughter. So although the relationship may not be strong, its existence
is a strong safety net for parents.
Manifest parent images
The portrayal of the older generation is usually friendly, but fathers have little
influence and are a 'non essential background figure.' This is sometimes contrasted with a
dominant mother, as in "Till the End of Time". As a result, there are more man-man
bonds than man-woman (wife). Other times the mother is critical of the hero. Fathers also
tend to appear not as competent as their sons.
The relationships between father's and daughters are also almost always positive.
Fathers become allies with her prospective husbands in order to try and disarm any
hostility towards her. Such possessiveness 'may be a result of 'mild retaliation' against
dominant women, as well as friendly sharing among men (p. 114). However, American
films avoid presenting intense father-daughter relations, so they are mostly superficial.
This is in contrast to French treatment of this theme, where father-daughter relations are
often emotionally intense.
Films with mother-son relations often show her as contributing to
tensions, although with little true influence - the potential destructiveness is rarely shown
in American films. And in general the images of mothers are in contrast to the positive
images of fathers (p. 119). Female heroes have mothers less often than fathers. When the
mother is portrayed, she creates tension, although in general, mothers appear to be less
foolish in relation to daughters than to sons. Only adoptive mothers tend to play more
active roles in the heroine's life.
Wolfenstein and Leites refer to Margaret Mead's assertion that American parents
(at the time Mead conducted her analysis) tend to bring up their children different than
how they were brought up, partly due to their immigrant backgrounds and partly due to
expecting children to do better than their parents (p. 124). Thus, heroes in movies have
different relations with their own children than they do with their parents. Whereas the
heroes' fathers are usually ineffective, the hero, tends to influence his own son more and
also have more dramatic relations with him than he had with his father. The heroes' sons
represent their immortality, but the continuity of generations extend only forwards, so the
heroes themselves do not represent the continued line of their fathers. He also seems to
have better relations with his daughter and inspires her. In French films the heroes often
suffers from love towards a woman who is a daughter figure (rather than a mother figure).
And in British films, father figures may have intense love for daughters in ways different
from both French and American ways, since he tends to be more cruel and terrifying (P.
128).
When female heroes become mothers in American movies they tend to find it
difficult and frustrating, so they are absent - especially towards sons. She is often more
maternal towards a father figure than towards her own son. This is in contrast to the hero-
father's good relations with their daughters. Heroines are also unhappy in relation to their
daughters, possibly because American mothers make their children feel they have to
fulfill her ambitions in order to be loved (P. 138). Sometimes this turns the daughters
against them.
They also appeal to Margaret Mead's insights when discussing siblings, especially
twins, as she pointed to the tendency to compare between age-mates, thereby giving rise
to rivalry and solidarity through eliminating causes for rivalry. Sister relations tend to be
more hostile than brother relations and sisters often love the same man and compete for
positions.
Heroes and heroines have parents at equal rates in Chinese film. Everyone has a
parent, and everyone's parents are to some extent involved in their lives. When they are
not, the characters become aimless (Lost in Beijing). In Jump Ashin!, the hero's parents
play an integral role in his development and recovery. The teacher/student relationship
sometimes substitutes for the fatherly relationship (Crazy Racer). The exceptions to these
rule come in historical films set during the imperial era, where princes often conspire
against their emperor fathers (Legend of the Golden Scorpion). Parents are willing to go
through great sacrifices for their children, for'example in Aftershock, which follows a
family trying to stay together after a major earthquake. Ocean Heaven portrays a
terminally-ill father trying his best to provide for his mentally retarded son after his
death. Children are similarly willing to sacrifice for their parents; a daughter undertakes a
journey on behalf of her father in Great Wall My Love, while a son grieves for and honors
his dead father in The Road Home by becoming a teacher in their hometown. Qin Jia Guo
Nian describes how complicated family relationships can become when multiple sets of
parents spend time together.
Siblings are very rarely portrayed in Chinese film. In the one film where sibling
relationships play a crucial role (Echoes of the Rainbow), the relationship between
brothers is shown to be incredibly important and positive - but for the majority of films,
siblings are absent. Another brother sister relationship in Summer Love is also very
positive. In both of these cases, one sibling is an adult, while the other is a child. In
Aftershock, the brother/sister bond among twins is separated by the Taishang earthquake;
but the inherent relationship is so strong that after the twins are reunited 20+ years later,
they instantly recognize the bond.

Concealed triangle
Because American films focus more on families that one makes, rather than
families from which one comes, there is less emotional significance in family relations.
However, Wolfenstein and Leites note that there are likely lasting impressions from
children's relations with their parents that remain, even if although unconsciously. The
emotions that are expressed instead towards others, such a boss, a gang-leader, or an
older caretaker who are often directly hostile and criminal. The hero is attracted to bad or
dangerous father figures and attacked by them. But whereas American films are
concerned with who is to blame for the violence, French films tend to express more that it
is not so much about assigning blame but about the internal feelings leading to conflict (p.
160).
American images of fathers picture him as violent. French images of fathers are
amorous. British movies sometimes have ideal or clean father figures with no sex or
aggression (p. 163).
Chinese films focus on families that one makes in the context of the one that one
comes from (Qin Jia Guo Nian), so there is significant emotional significance from from
matters. With respect to the triangles, there are few except in the cases where there are
coaches involved with children (and parents are still alive) (Crazy Racer). In one film,
Together, a child actually ends up rejecting. his coach and doing his best to win back his
father after the triangle led to a falling out.
Killers and Victims
The third theme Wolfenstein and Leites discuss is that of killers and victims, as
partial presentations of the destructive potential in all humans. Even killers act on only a
fraction of the destructiveness of which they are capable. One function of movies is to
present what such impulses might look like if more fully expressed (p. 195). They find
different distinctive lines in the movies: in American movies violence is not a threat of
ones impulses, but arises from outside dangers. The hero might have motive but is
usually not the murderer. He must rid himself from the false accusations and his own
guilt ("Human beings have a tenacious susceptibility to feel guilt for mere wishes"). The
hero is thus in danger not just from false accusations, but also from his own 'projected
destructive impulses embodied in external agencies ever ready to attack him' (P. 177).
Investigating authorities are often incompetent, leaving it to the hero to clear himself and
prove that he is ultimately a good person: "The hero, like the good-bad girl, has not done
anything; what is accomplished in the course of the plot is to replace a deceptive
appearance by an exonerating reality" (p. 178). Modern versions of this theme are found
in movies such as "The Fugitive," "US Marshals", and "Rules of Engagement."
While American films may be violent; fast-paced, or technologically advanced,
there are fewer emotions involved and usually little attachment or intimacy to the one
who gets killed. Murder happens when someone is in the way of the pursuit of a certain
goal. Victims are reduced to 'things,' and death, seems almost unreal in American films -
murder is a starting point or ending point Victims do not haunt murderers - only the
police. Thus, they conclude, in American films "the dead end either to be forgotten or not
to seem very dead. For the most part death remains inapplicable to the hero" (p. 178).
In contrast to American movies, British films focus on the internal destructive
impulses that one can lose control over. Murderers more often know their victims,
through love or other intimate relations, and the hero is seen to be overtaken by his own
violent impulses. Sometimes the murderer announces his next murder to the police and is
drawn into double identification. Violence is also more central; American films are based
on fantasy, but British films put the violence itself in the center as an act of destroying or
being destroyed (p. 179). There are also more feelings of guilt involved and the
authorities usually accomplish justice - Scotland Yard man.
French films are not so focused on internal dangers (like British films) or external
dangers (like American films) of the violent impulses in man. Instead, violence occurs
more when the central theme is 'irony of human justice' (p. 179). Justice is not always
served and both murderers and victims are seen as mixes of good and bad impulses. As a
result, death in French films appears very real and the hero too is mortal.
A central character in American films is thus the falsely accused hero - often a
stranger in town when a murder happens and forced to leave and to clear his own name.
Heroes feel like strangers pursued by the world for crimes they did not commit. They are
often weary and unsmiling (Humphrey Bogart) (p. 182). "They are hunted men, moving
among strangers" (p. 182). He suffers until he is cleared, and in that process more crimes
are committed, and/or the hero himself gets into conflict with the law, making it more
difficult for him to clear his name. The flight from police is itself a crime. "Aloneness" is
a central theme for this character:
"Perhaps the thing from which the hero suffers most, and which
contributes most to the semblance of his guilt, is that he is alone. As
Margaret Mead and Geoffrey Gorer have pointed out, Americans tend to
feel uneasy alone; they feel they are unloved and therefore unworthy of
love - there most be something wrong with them. They need the constant
reassurance of the positive response, of others to feel that they are
lovable" (p. 184).
Perhaps a modern example of the weary and unsmiling hero is represented in
characters such as The Fugitive (Harrison Ford being the falsely accused; Tommy Lee
Jones the US Marshall pursuing him - both unsmiling heroes in the movie); and US
Marshalls (Tommy Lee Jones again pursuing a falsely accused stranger) - in both cases
the falsely accused is working to clear his own name for crimes he did not commit:
hunted among stranger with no one to trust. The violence in such films is often more
personal than mass-casualty movies: and the theme of aloneness more articulated. Here.
the destructiveness comes not just from the violence itself. but from the lack of trust and
the uneasiness of no-one believing their innocence. Rules of Engagement takes the theme
of aloneness even further; not only is the falsely accused hero (almost) alone: his legal
defender is a recovering alcoholic, struggling to stay sober, and to live up to his retired
fathers unfulfilled ambitions for him.
Rather than partial representations of destructive potential in humans, Chinese films
portray violence as the natural state for humans. Violence needs to be repressed in
humans, or in the absence of authority; humans will prey on each other (Back to 1942,
Hero). And humans not only commit violence, but use violence to enrich themselves.
Most films set in the imperial period (Red Cliff, The Emperor's Shadow) demonstrate how
to corrupt emperors use violence to extract resources from their people.
At the same time, violence is used as a means of solving problems. In the same
types of films, in order to eliminate a corrupt emperor or leader, the people use violence
in rebellion - with varying results. Violence in struggling against foreign invaders is a
common theme as well (City of Life and Death). Violence used in protecting others from
harm is also morally sound (Bodyguards and Assassins). And in protecting the state (who
the character believes represent moral fortitude), violence is common (Lust, Caution).
Violence is justified when fighting against foreign invaders (The Flowers of War).
Violence may even be the only way - that is, violent struggle - to solve political
problems. Chinese films celebrate the overthrow of the old world through revolution, and
the institution of a new order (Liu San Jie). All members of society are expected to
participate in violence for such ends (The Red, Detachment of Women), for China is
always at risk of invasion and destruction if the Chinese people were to let their guards
down (The Burning of Ming Yuan Ming). Violence is sometimes used with comedic effect
(Let the Bullets Fly), although this i's rare.
The genre of wuxia films - those films set in the Chinese historical period that
glorify sword-fighting, heroes, and the promotion of stability - all portray violence. Films
like Crouching Tiger, Hidden Dragon; Hero; The Lost Bladesman; Red Cliff; White
Vengeance; The Sorcerer and the White Snake; House of Flying Daggers; and others all
portray heroes grappling against evil - with the only way to defeat evil to use force to
totally destroy it. Violence is used with purpose.
An important note is that violence never mixes with relationships - romantic films
contain nearly no violence - with the exception of Shanghai and similar spy films where
the element of danger adds to the thrill of love. But in relationships in the modern era,
love is just that - there are no crimes of passion - violence is used only to facilitate happy
ends.
Modern US TV shows (such as NCIS and NCIS-Los Angeles and Law and Order)
contains elements of both the "old school" Leites/Wolfenstein themes as well as some
commonality with Chinese wuxia films (violence used to defeat evil and facilitate happy
1
:
endings). The heroes are occasionally depicted as feeling guilty of having to use violence
(especially as rookies), but it is seen as 'what one has to do in the line of duty' (unlike in
Chinese films, the heroes often have to see a team-psychologist after (NCIS-LA) or
someone consulting for the team after crisis (NCIS) or take anger management classes
(Law and Order).
Performers and Onlookers
Wolfenstein and Leites' final theme is that of performers and onlookers. They
argue that American films "are preoccupied with showing events from a variety of
viewpoints" (p. 243). The theme is that of "looking and being looked at "(p. 245).
Looking seems to be preferred to performing; Leites cites the example of plots involving
professional performers - their careers are "fraught with danger" and they are often
"carried away by destructive impulses he cannot control" (p. 245). Conversely, "the hero,
as long as he confines himself to the role of investigator, triumphs; when he assumes the
role of performer he is likely to suffer for it" (p. 246).
American comedy films, make light of onlookers - comic onlookers habitually
catch other characters in "ambiguous but actually harmless" situations (p. 247). These
false appearances seem central to the drama of the film, and "resolution of the comedy of
false appearances corresponds in part to the conclusion of the melodrama in which the
hero is falsely suspected" (p. 249-250). Their prevalence, to Leites and Wolfenstein,
suggest that Americans naturally behave properly - there is no need for social sanctions
to enforce good behavior (p. 251). But this does not mean wishes to act badly do not exist
- the prevalence of false appearances in film also stand for "wishes that are not acted out
and which often remain unacknowledged" (p. 252). These differ from French films, for
example, where false appearances show discrepancies between facades and intentions; a
harmless façade might conceal a bad intention. Moreover, in French films the hero "first
sees things are he would wish them to be, later learns that they are different," while "in
American films, the hero first sees things as he fears they may be, in the end gets the
assurance that they are just as he would wish" (p. 262).
Other onlookers include promoters of, love - they "sanction or promote the
relation of the young couple or at least accept it with an amused tolerance" (p. 262). They
approximate fathers or might be buddies of the hero. Onlookers unite couples, provide
reassurance, arrange reunions, and are unenvious of the hero. Films frequently close with
shots on the onlooker rather than the couple (p. 266).
With respect to performer's, "we find that professional performers in American
films are very often shown as unhappy" (p. 268). Unhappiness comes both from negative
feelings about work and from failure to find happiness outside of work. They frequently
cannot find happiness in love. Comic heroes seem to be able to get around these issues by
themselves displaying "weakness and ineptitude" (p. 275), thereby becoming exempt
from penalties. Wolfenstein and Leites relate this to the practice of contemporaneous
American public speakers telling jokes 'and stories at their own expense - themselves
becoming comic heroes.
Men who exhibit women (as designers of clothing, discoverers, or agents) display
tension between the tandem issues of wishing to see other men attracted them, while
becoming attracted to them themselves (p. 278). In American films this leads to severe
conflicts. There plots to this effect - the so-called "magic contact," where a young girl
who has not yet begun her career fixates on an older male authority figure that might help
her (p. 284). And in an ultimate display of the performer idea, some films show
performers playing performers - actors playing themselves (p. 289).
This theme does not play a large role in Chinese film. There is one film about
Chinese performers (Platform), but it is oriented on showing how Chinese society
changed with the close of the Cultural Revolution and China's economic opening in the
1980s. Performance happens in karaoke. (A Beautiful Life, Xiao Wu); instead of
performance for others for pay, Chinese perform for themselves and their friends in
private rooms. Performers are explicitly portrayed in Frozen (a film about performance
art), and Farewell My Concubine (a film about Beijing opera) - but the focus is not on
performance for performance's sake, but on the relationships and plots that surround the
act of performance. Performance is only important for the types of stories that can be told
about performance.
Onlookers do play a role in some films. In Ocean Heaven, the main character
finds himself trying to find a home for his mentally-challenged son after discovering that
he himself suffers from a terminal illness. In doing so, an onlooker (a neighborhood
woman) falls in love with him, agrees to care for his son after his death, and forms a
relationship with him before he dies. Voyeurism is a plot element of several films - the
opening scene of In the Heat of the Sun is a young boy spying on a girl. In police films
dealing with wiretaps, the police themselves become onlookers or voyeurs in listening to
criminal conversations (Overheard). In Don't Go Breaking My Heart, voyeurism is used
as a means of seduction; a powerful businessman watches a woman across the street from
his office window, and by writing messages to put on the window, he seduces her.
[MA: do half page on how this holds for US): Western ones still (or not]
3. Chinese Movies and Movie Industry
How can one apply Leites and Wolfenstein's approach to film analysis to Chinese
society? Both old and new movies may yield insight into how Chinese society has
evolved. We have indicated above a few examples of the application of the characters and
dynamics to Chinese movies - but have not said anything about the differences in
societies and organizational issues that may affect the differences in themes.
In particular, one would also have to take into account the involvement of the
Chinese government in the movie industry A a theme itself that deserves attention since it
may reflect how the Chinese government interacts (or sees itself interacting) with the
world and with the Chinese people. Here,one could start with Mao's approach to cultural
products:
"In the world today all culture, all literature and art belong to definite
classes and are geared to definite political lines. There is in fact no such thing
as art for art's sake, art that stands above classes, art that is detached from or
independent of politics. Proletarian literature and art are part of the whole
proletarian revolutionary course; they are, as Lenin said, cogs and wheels in the
whole revolutionary machine" (Mao, 1942, p. 86).
And his statement:
"An army without culture is a dull-witted army, and a dull-witted
army cannot defeat the enemy" (Mao( 1944, p. 235).
Mao thought of culture as a political tool. This tradition continues. Before release
to the public, a central government bureau reviews all films in modern China - at the
level the script and at the level of the final cut. This may reflect an approval that the
movies will illustrate some values and beliefs - and not others. Directors have spoken out
about the extent of Chinese government censorship in the development process.
Thinking about this process is important for understanding of Chinese
government, how it interacts with the society it ostensibly serves, and how it interacts
with the world. Other governments around the world use film as propaganda, including
famously, the Nazi regime of Germany in the 1930s-40s and the current regime in North
Korea. While China is not a despotic regime like those, it is still worth thinking about the
means by which an authoritarian government tries to keep control of its people.
The Chinese film industry is now the second largest in the world, with hundreds
or thousands of films produced annually. These range from big-budget thrillers, to
historical dramas, to independent films shot by a growing generation of digital-camera
wielding directors (as depicted in Stand by Me). Chinese cinema could be seen as a
window into the state of Chinese society, and those interested in keeping an eye on what
ordinary Chinese citizens are thinking about could learn much from watching the films
they care about. The themes, characters, plot, and relationships in these films could be
representative of broader trends in Chinese society. For many reasons, it could be
important to understand broad trends in a society like China's, so this project has both
intellectual significance and real-world "relevance."
This is particularly true because of the insights it could give as to the values of the
Chinese Communist Party. Research into the inner workings of the CCP has been
hampered by the lack of availability of reliable primary source data. All films released in
China have to be screened and approved by a Party organ, the State Administration of
Radio, Film, and Television, the main censorship body for such media in China. It also
administers or controls state-owned media production agencies. For the purposes of this
study, it will be assumed for now that the SARFT largely follows the direction of the
central Party authorities in making judgments about media and is not entirely an
independent actor. It could actually be possible that the SARFT could be a progressive
!
:
force in the Party, promoting media that may not entirely align with the interests of the
greater Party, but there is no evidence of this so far.
Chinese Film Genres
The United States has no formal censorship system for film. Rather, a film industry
group, the Motion Picture Association of America, administers a rating agency to which
most theaters showing films subscribe. A voluntary process, filmmakers submit films to
the rating agency, which recommends possible changes and then issues a final rating. A
film's commercial viability could be contingent on its rating; films that are rated explicit
(NC-17) rarely perform well in theaters. This process is devoid of government
interference; it is a voluntary process perpetuated by organizational processes and social
norms. Censorship is self-censorship done for commercial success; filmmakers promote
some themes because they sell better. To what extent does this factor in China?
Chinese film revenue
In China, all films released to the public must be appropriate for all audiences; there is no
age-rating system like there is in the United States. The State Administration of Radio,
Film, and Television reviews all films prior to commercial release and mandates changes
that must be made prior to release. Fu, writing in the late 1980s, wrote "all mass media,
publication, communication, education, ideology, and all forms of social and economic
organization are under the control of the party/state" (Fu, 1989, 268), although this has
probably relaxed in the intervening years. These changes ensure that films conform to
accepted Chinese Communist Party ideology and send the right message to the people.
Independent films could be released "underground," but their viewership would probably
not be as high as if they were released in theaters. Independent filmmakers could also
face sanctions if they are caught promoting offensive or illegal material.
.+
Chinese film director Lou Ye has provided some insight into the workings of the
censorship process on social meeting. His film, Mystery, required significant edits from
SARFT due to a violent murder scene. In negotiations with SARFT, in which he
experienced delays and lack of response; he publically announced to SARFT the
following:
""I'm waiting for an answer: Can the film be released on time
without any changes, yes or no? The answer is so simple but so difficult-[the
process] makes me feel disappointed and sad, but I also feel a sense of
understanding and support. China's domestic film industry needs everyone to
work together. I totally accept the fact that I'm a director in the age of film
censorship. I just want a dialogue [with the authorities], and a dialogue is not
a confrontation. There are no winners and losers in a dialogue. There are no
enemies."{{1}[1]等待一个回答,能不能不做修改如期上映,行还是
不行?这个回答,如此简单,但又如此之难,从中我感到遗憾,失望,
从中我也感到理解支持和感动,中国电影需要大家一起努力,我完全接

The industry itself is comprised of a mix of state-owned and private studios, with the
number of private studios growing to compete for a growing market share. Large state-
owned studios include China Film Group and, Shanghai Animation Film Studio. There are
also thriving film industries in Hong Kong and in Taiwan, and these studios often engage
in joint ventures with mainland studios when releasing films. Tickets are moderately
priced and available to a large segment of the Chinese population, especially its rapidly-
growing middle class.
According to industry watchers, there are 13,500 movie screens in 3,700 theaters across
the country - one screen per 100,000 people. This is far less than the one per 8,000 figure
in the United States - China needs to construct 155,000 screens to reach that ratio - but
this number is rising fast. On a revenue basis, China's industry is growing six times faster
than the US film industry. Not only are Chinese films shown in cinemas, but US films as
well. US films actually enjoy the highest ticket sales, and SARFT allows 34 foreign films
to be released a year. These films account for 40% of China's box office revenue.
Selection of Chinese film
The evolution of the Chinese film industry in the context of the evolution
of Chinese film
The early history of the Chinese film industry reflects the societal upheaval China
underwent in the 20"" century, as well as China's transformation from a feudal empire, to
a Communist people's republic, to a modern state embracing some aspects of capitalism.
A brief history of the Chinese film industry can be divided through into several periods -
starting with the early activities immediately after the invention of film, moving through
the golden eras before the Pacific War, the resurgence of film in the Communist era, the
silencing of filmmakers during the Cultural Revolution, and the new generation of film
makers who emerged from the turbulence. Each represent periods in the society; but also
represent the themes in Chinese society over time - hopes, fears, dreams, visions, and the
interaction of ordinary Chinese people ....
There are three main sources useful for understanding patterns in early Chinese
films: Jeff Yang, Once Upon a Time in China (Yang, 2003); Yingjin Zhang and Zhiwei
Zhao (eds), Encyclopedia of Chinese Film (Zhang and Zhao, 1999); and Tao Ye and Yun
Zhu, Historical Dictionary of Chinese Cinema (Ye and Zhu, 2012).
Overall, there are five traditional periods of Chinese cinema (Ye and Zhu, 2012;
Zhang and Zhao, 1999). The first was from 1894-1934 - in this period, film culture was
introduced to China. The second (1934-1949) saw a consolidation of film production and
rapid growth in the number of films produced; this period contained two 'golden ages' of
cinema. The third (1949-1979) were the years of Communist control, with no foreign
films and propaganda driving themes and production. The fourth (1979-1989) saw
changes in film due to economic opening and reform, as well as the importation of
foreign film and the export of Chinese film to the west. The final phase (1989-Present) is
the post-Tiananmen period where Chinese film has matured and begun to explore as wide
a variety of themes as films in the west.
Scholars have also divided Chinese cinema history into generations based on filmmakers
(Ye and Zhu, 2012; Zhang and Zhao, 1999). The first generation (1905-1937) ended with
the start of the War with Japan and included early experimenters in film. The second
generation (1937-1949) of filmmakers was active between the war with the Japanese and
the civil wars, producing many pieces of "realistic socialism." The third generation
(1949-1978) includes the Communist era, through the end of the Cultural Revolution,
where themes were restricted to Communist propaganda. The fourth generation
(1978-1983) covers the start of the reform and opening period. The fifth generation
(1983-1989) led the way in changing Chinese film during reform, as the first class of
serious filmmakers to emerge from Chinese film academies. The sixth generation (1989-
present) represents the new wave of filmmakers coming out of the post-Deng period,
including many independent filmmakers.
The first film was screened in China in 1896, but the first film produced was in 1905 in a
Shanghai studio by Ren Fengtai - called Con
nquering Jun Mountain, the film was a
segment of a Beijing opera production (Yang, 2003). Indeed, Beijing opera influenced
Chinese film greatly - from supplying ready, stories to be filmed, to supplying actors and
actresses to act in them. Chinese audiences linked films to traditional performances like
shadowplays and opera and used those as context for assessing them. The party
denounced Beijing opera as feudal entertainment (for example, see Farwell My
Concubine), but this did not stop CCP officials from integrating operatic forms into their
own propaganda pieces - such as the dance and songs from the epic musical The East is
Red.

During the First World War, there was a dearth of film production, as Chinese domestic
industry, at the time, did not produce the necessary equipment for large-scale production
(Leyda, 1972). After World War I, it became easier for filmmakers to import physical film
reels for production from Europe, particularly from Germany. There was a blossoming of
studios, films, and themes - but as the KMT Nationalist government took power, by the
late 1920s there were fewer studios in operation, and what films were released were
subjected to heavy censorship. Legends and myths were phased out, along with martial
arts; there was a push to make film politically palatable and to be used to shape society -
in this vein, there was tension between films that supported the KMT government, and
films (leftist films) that were highly critical of the KMT - leftist films advocated for
radical social reform, and they portrayed poverty, class conflict, and moral decay. The
early Chinese communist party had a role in pushing forward leftist films, which focused
on social problems and criticism.
During the Pacific War, film production dropped significantly. What films that were
produced were propaganda; whether anti or pro Japanese. In unoccupied areas, films
produced showed themes of nationalism, resistance, and patriotism. Post-war, there was
competition between Communist and KMT-backed production studios. After the
Communist victory in the Chinese Civil War, a large number of KMT-affiliated
filmmakers fled to Taiwan and Hong Kong, where they set up independent film studios
that turned into major industry hubs. Hong Kong grew to be a center for Asian cinema,
popularizing wuxia and other martial arts films inspired by traditional Chinese stories.
The Communists understood early on the propaganda value of film, establishing a bureau
within the party to oversee the messages they wished to be sent through the medium. By
1938, the CCP and KMT actually had a joint propaganda office, in 1938 headed by Kuo
Mojo. Zhou Enlai, a vice-minister of the National Military Council, served with the
Political Section in Chungking as part of the joint government - this section oversaw film
studios (Leyda, 1973). KMT and CCP film studios, with KMT filmmakers operating out
of Shanghai and CCP filmmakers operating out of Yenan, produced films occasionally in
tandem, but as the Japanese invasion waned, often in opposition to one another.
The CCP Film Bureau both produced films and approved scripts for production from the
private studios (Ye and Zhu, 2012; Leyda, 1973). During the civil war, the Communists
and leftist filmmakers used film in polemics against the KMT to rally the population to
their side. After the Communists took power in 1949, the film industry exploded, with
hundreds of films and millions of viewers. The CCP operated a Ministry of Culture and a
special Department of Propaganda that had direct authority over films. The propaganda
power came outside of government ministries from this special body.
Whale
In 1950, an eminent filmmaker noted:
"In the film industry, the basic principle is to eliminate the poisonous imperialist
films gradually and strengthen the educational nature of the people's film industry,
orientating our films towards the masses of workers, peasants and soldiers ...
Before the liberation, seventy-five percent of all the movie-goers in Shanghai
went to see American films, but the percentage was reduced to 28.3 by June 1950.
This is a great victory on the cinema front" (Leyda, 1973, 188).
The themes during this period primarily covered the lives of ordinary Chinese, These
included Party-directed depictions of "realistic socialism," and other themes important to
the Party. These themes were those advocated by the party for consumption by audiences
- not necessarily desired by audiences themselves. The CCP eliminated western cinema,
particularly at the advent of the Korean War; these were replaced by Soviet and Eastern
European films.
The CCP actually instituted a quota system for themes important to the Party. Xiao
(1999) reports that in 1951, the Film Bureau projected production of 18 films - of these,
4-5 were to deal with socialist construction, 3 with the war against the Japanese and
KMT, 2 with rural life, 2 with world peace, 1 with science, 1 with ethnic minorities, 1
with cultural matters, and 1 with children. While these were for state-produced films, the
Film Bureau's control over script approval meant that these themes reverberated in the
private studios as well. That is, this production mix was meant for state-owned studios as
a clear directive to be followed, but also meant for private studios as a signal meant to be
followed.
Communist rule was not always positive for the industry. The Cultural Revolution nearly
wiped out the Chinese film industry. Prior films were banned and very few new films
were produced; after the Gang of Four assumed control, there was some revival in film
production but only in propaganda films that buttressed their regime. Films were viewed
as counter-revolutionary, and like many aspects of the "old society," meant to be
jettisoned to build the "new society."
The final downfall of the Gang of Four ushered in a revival in film production, leading to
the rise of the so-called "Fourth" and "Fifth Generation" of Chinese filmmakers. Many of
these directors graduated in the same class from the Beijing Film Academy - the class of
1978. These directors embraced the opening of China and explored more realistic themes,
moving away from pure propaganda films to examine human themes common to the
experience of most Chinese. A small group of filmmakers produced "scar dramas" that
assisted in the repudiation of the Cultural Revolution - these films examined the CR's
negative impact on Chinese society. Other themes included examinations of how people's
lives changed from economic reform and opening - this led to a cultural reflection
movement from the "Fourth Generation" of filmmakers, who sought to reexamine
traditional culture. The late 1980s saw a rise of morally cynical and politically apathetic
films that ridiculed traditional values and showed the disarray of Chinese life.
Xiao (1999) also reports that since the 1990s, three major trends have emerged in Chinese
filmmaking - (1) commercial films; (2) mainstream/propaganda films; and (3) art films.
Commercial films have dominated private production, and the government has sponsored
propaganda films. Due to changes in the industry (studios suffering severe losses), the
new Sixth Generation directors began as independent filmmakers and have injected more
dynamism into the modern industry.
What explains film's success in Chinese society? One can see film's success as resulting
at least in part from the influence of the Communist Party in shaping mass entertainment
during the mid-20 century. First, the Party Viewed traditional dramatic forms - such as
Beijing opera - as "feudal" and instituted campaigns against them. They lost popularity
with the masses. At the same time, the Party established organs to promote film as one of
its primary propaganda tools. The Party's promotion of film and simultaneous denigration
of other forms of entertainment, coupled with the construction of theatres across China
(with low admission prices) made film one of the most important modern forms of
entertainment in China.
Scholarly perspectives on early Chinese film
Trying to distill the results from an entire literature is challenging - Chinese film has
attracted varied attention, with scholars approaching film from historical, sociological,
psychological, or aesthetic perspectives. Like the literature has evolved on movies in
general (see earlier), scholarship on Chinese' film includes not just analyses of films, but
criticism as well - in addition to perspectives coming from both inside and outside of
China. We present a brief overview of a few areas that have interested scholars in and out
of China.
Chris Berry has been a leading western scholar on Chinese film, producing books,
articles, and edited volumes. Berry argues (2004) that socialist film (1949-1976) was
pedagogical in nature, designed to push forward state control. Berry quotes Mao's
speeches from the Yan'an forum, where he argued there was little separation between art
and politics. For Berry, characters in socialist film had character traits that "are more
social than psychological, in accordance with the Marxist class-based worldview favored
by the Chinese Communist Party" (Berry, 2004, 35). Political loyalties separate
characters; "at no point is there any discussion of the characters' family backgrounds,
childhood experiences, or any other psychologically significant formative
experiences" (p. 37). He also suggests Chinese films contain characters designed to serve
as apprentices; they know nothing about revolutionary work, but learn over the course of
the film - serving as role models for the audience. He even argues the mise-en-scene,
framing, lighting, etc are presented to construct a pupil-teacher relationship with the
audience.
After Mao's death in 1976, Berry argues the power struggles, political change, and
opening in broader Chinese society was reflected in "post-socialist" film. One important
change was the decline of simple, heroic characters - rather than serving as just
revolutionary martyrs, the didactic paradigm waned - and characters became more
complex in both trait and function. For example - characters ceased being members of
the party apparatus or members of the revolutionary classes. There was also thematic
shift - such that love scenes (between individuals) became far more common. Rather than
films that tried to separate families and subordinate the needs of families and lovers to the
needs of the Party, the corresponding decline in the revolutionary hero and the rise of
romantic love signaled a shift in values. Importantly, Berry argues that these shifts
occurred before the rise of the Fifth Generation (155).
Chinese film scholars in the aftermath of the Cultural Revolution and economic opening
had similar interests in the new wave of film. There were a few separate areas of interest.
First, scholars became interested in films as serving not as education or propaganda in
class struggle, but as representations of culture. That is, there was a sense that China had
achieved success in eliminating exploiting classes; films therefore reflected not political
and class struggle, but when there was conflict, they reflected cultural struggles -
particularly struggle with China's ancient struggle (Chen, 1993, 52). Second, there was a
greater interest in "entertainment films," films designed purely to entertain the masses,
rather than send any kind of political or educational message. Third, there was an interest
in evolving Chinese film theory; rather than a focus on story and action, there was a
growing interest in the form of the film - how it was shot, the colors used, editing, and
camera movement. Scholars debated on ways to approach film - this represented an
evolution in Chinese film theory that led to the incorporation of western ideas, such as
relating the films to aspects of society and national culture (Semsel, et. al., 1993).
What is interesting from reading the Chinese sources is the relationship between film
theory and filmmaking in China. While in the west, filmmakers may operate
independently of the theory and analysis that follow their work, in China filmmakers can
be critics too - and trained at academies where the study of film theory is important,
Semsel and colleagues argue that Chinese filmmakers are to a greater extent informed by
theory - and the study of Chinese film theory (yingxi theory) could be informative for
understand how the filmmakers themselves think about the films they make.
Berry and Farquhar have considered gender role's'in Chinese film. With respect to
women, they argue that the role of motherhood - a role that "links women and the nation"
is "present in Chinese cinema, but not so prominent" (Berry and Farquhar, 2006, p. 109).
There seem to be few "Mother China" figures in Chinese film; Berry and Farquhar
suggest this may be because the "Chinese, term for the nation to which one owes
allegiance is already gendered male" (p. 110). Women in Chinese film exist in public
space, but they serve in patriotic roles - women are not mothers of the nation, but good
daughters. For me, their portrayal reflects normative codes - filiality, brotherhood, and
loyalty - stemming from premodern Confucian principles. These codes are reflected in
the roles and actions of male characters.
Methodologically, authors take a case study approach. They watch a wide array of films,
tie in ideas from related disciplines (philosophy, sociology, anthropology), and
demonstrate examples of their findings by pulling evidence from characters, plots, and
themes in film. They do not always tie their findings back into representations of Chinese
society.
Studies have a variety of themes, including looks into gender roles, change in film over
time, differences between the mainland/Hong Kong/Taiwanese industries, how film
reflects the political and economic change in China, postcolonialism, realism, Chinese
identity, and directorial styles, to name just a few.
Impressions from films
It would be tempting to stylize Chinese film"- to suggest that Chinese filmmakers portray
the same themes the same way across all time periods. But the Chinese film industry did
not grow to be the second largest in the world - and still growing at a rapid rate - by
rehashing tired themes and characters. The story of the early Chinese film industry is one
of continual evolution and rebirth. Affected like the rest of Chinese society by the
political turmoil of the 20th century, Chinese filmmakers reacted to the changes facing
their country (and pleased their censors) by portraying the plight of China and the
Chinese people in a way that would resonate with ordinary Chinese.
There are several thematic waves in Chinese film that correspond in part with the
generational system - the generations of filmmakers and of "film style" referenced in the
academic literature.
Chinese film came into its own in the 1930s. (Leyda, 1973). By the 30s, the export
restrictions on German silver film stock had lifted after the First World War (Leyda
reports that it was not until the 1960s that the Chinese film industry could rely on
domestic production of cameras and stock), a number of studios were producing
innovative films, and a cadre of actors and actresses (some coming from the Beijing
opera tradition) worked under the watchful eye of Nationalist censors. These were a
combination of silent films (with a music soundtrack/backing) and "talkies." These films
had a leftist slant - they intended to portray the state of Chinese society as they saw it -
in particular, themes like the oppression of foreign powers, the plight of China's poor, the
crimes perpetrated by the upper classes and their "running dogs," and the need to build a
new society (in this case, led by the Nationalists). A representative film in this area is The
Goddess (1934), a silent film starring the famous actress Ruan Lingyu. Ruan plays a
single mother who becomes a prostitute to afford to send her young son to school; when
his classmates find out his mother is a prostitute, their parents push to have him expelled
in moral outrage. Upon demonstrating to the principal her true love for her child, he
acquiesces - but is forced to resign by a group of concerned parents who do not accept
his ruling. Ruan (the unnamed Goddess) is arrested and sentenced to twelve years in
prison, and the principal agrees to adopt her son '- telling him at her request that she has
died. This film hits several of the major themes of this period - particularly the plight of
the poor and the oppression of the upper classes.
Another representative film is The Big Road (1935), also a silent film. This is a film
meant to inspire viewers to contribute toward China's national defense. This picture is set
during the Japanese incursion into Manchuria - although the Japanese are never
referenced in the film and only called "the enemy" - and the attempts by a work crew to
build a road to deliver the Nationalist army (again, not referred to by name) to fight them
off. This film is a celebration of male strength (focusing only on a crew of a half dozen
young men) and the nobility of peasant labor; the laobaixing in the film lament that they
are poor, but yet they realize the enormity of their task and make the sacrifices necessary
for China. They work on the road until their deaths, after being strafed by a Japanese
plane. But the filmmaker portrays their spirits as still working on the road even after
death, happily giving their labor to the Chine
se cause.
Indeed, the idea of a unified China" - of a Chinese spirit - is hinted at in many films.
There are snippets of dialogue referencing things that normal Chinese people would do,
or asking if a character is a real Chinese, or talking about the importance of staying loyal
to true Chinese over traitors or foreigners. This theme became extremely important after
the Pacific War and during the Chinese Civil War. It seemed as if filmmakers were trying
to reconcile the two sides in some way - by focusing on the danger of the outsiders
(western powers and Japan) - filmmakers could help heal the gap between the CCP and
KMT. They emphasize the importance of a single vision of a prototypical Chinese
peasant and push for this interpretation to be the "official" interpretation in film.
A good example of this comes from This Life of Mine (1950), in which a retired Beijing
policeman recounts his life - which conveniently takes place in the early 20th century
during some of the most turbulent periods in Chinese history. The policeman recounts the
corruption of the various governments (Qing and KMT), the Japanese invasion, the,
ensuing civil war, and the necessity for a unified. China. At one point he is asked by a
friend "Are you Chinese or not? Do you want to be ruled by foreigners?" A CCP cadre
remarks, "The Chinese people have lived meaningless for 5,000 years, dying unmourned
and wronged," showing the frustration the laobaixing have with the treatment of the
lower classes throughout China's history. The film was released after the Communist
takeover, so there is an overt CCP political message toward the end of the film - but the
theme of a unified China in many respects unites CCP and KMT interests.
In addition to a unified China, there is a strong theme of an old China and a new China.
The old China represented China's feudal past; servants, slavery, foreign domination, the
monarchy, corruption, and the old ways. The new China represents liberty, democracy,
equality, and a chance for the poor to make their way in society without oppression from
the upper classes. An interesting film representing this idea is Spring in a Small Town
(1948) - portraying what appears to be a bucolic country village recovering from the
Pacific War. Mr. Dai lives in a crumbling home with his young wife for which there is
little mutual passion in the relationship. An old friend visits one day, who has journeyed
to the coast of China and become a doctor This man is young, educated, and forward-
thinking. The filmmaker has set up a clear dialectic - pitting the old, decrepit squire
living in his crumbling home against the new China - the young doctor coming to save
the day. The bored and listless wife - of course - becomes enchanted, but does not agree
to take him as her husband until the squire has died.
After the Communist takeover, themes became more tightly focused around Party ideals.
Mao is well-known for his belief in art as a political medium. With the explosion in the
number of screens in China after the Communist takeover, it is no wonder the Chinese
film industry grew; films were an easy way to push a propaganda message across a
ravaged country of uneducated peasants - newspapers, for example, would have been
hampered by low literacy rates. These films were oriented around convincing the people
that the CCP was justified in ruling the country, and that while there were other parties
who sought to control China, only the CCP could have united the common people and
thrown out the oppressors. Many of these films are in fact retellings of various episodes
in China's history. The East is Red (1965) is a musical telling the story of the rise of the
CCP and how it came to control China - focusing on the various armed conflicts it fought
in the first half century. The Burning of Yuan Ming Yuan (1983) is about the burning of
the Emperor's summer palace by rampaging foreign forces. The depictions of foreign
:
brutality are particularly severe, and there are the same common depictions of the people
rising up as one to destroy them. The Red Detachment of Women (1961) is unique for its
portrayal of women as main characters - but of course, these women are in a special PLA
unit of all women fighting against the Nationalists on the island of Hainan. The themes of
sacrifice and martyrdom are common in these histories, as are the need to protect the
common people from rapacious capitalists and landlords. Liu San Jie (1963) is another
musical describing a folk story from Guanxi region. In it, a beautiful young girl struggles
against evil landlords with her talent in singing; all Chinese, regardless of their skills,
have a duty to struggle however they can. These films push forward a narrative that China
is perpetually in danger of attack - from inside or outside - and that the Party is the best
way to protect a vulnerable country.
There was little deviation from these themes up through the Cultural Revolution. Party
censors were content to approve films that fit their set narrative. Given the political,
economic, and social turbulence in China arising out many of Mao's decisions, it
probably served the Party's interests to have its narrative repeated again and again. The
Chinese film industry arguably had wide thematic exploration during the golden ages of
film - primarily in the 1930s. It might appear film themes reverted to what the
propaganda masters in Beijing wanted to push; that the censors restricted themes heavily.
However, note that many of the earlier films actually had similar underlying themes - the
whole movement of leftist cinema was designed to agitate for the resolution of major
social problems - including exploitation by capitalists, foreigners, and landlords. It is as
if the Communists came in, took the pre-existing themes, changed them slightly to fit the
CCP narrative, and held themselves up as the answer to the calls for help Chinese
filmmakers put out in the 1930s. So what might look like the restriction of artistic
expression could be viewed in a different way - the culmination of decades of artistic
calls for attention finally satisfied.
After the Cultural Revolution and the downfall of the Gang of Four, censorship
restrictions loosened dramatically. There was again a blossoming of thematic exploration
- to coincide with China's economic and social opening. After the repression (and
effective shutdown) of the film industry during the Cultural Revolution, the first class of
filmmakers to graduate from the Beijing Film Academy - the class of 1981 - led the so-
called Fifth Generation in the exploration of more varied themes, at times even criticizing
the Party and its actions.
Prior to the 1980s, Chinese filmmakers focused on narrative. The structure of a story, the
themes, and the way the story is told were extremely important. At times, the
cinematography, sets, and costumes faded into the background - The East is Red is the
clearest example of this; a musical borrowing forms from Beijing opera, it presents its
story is an extremely stylized way, stripping down all distractions from the audience to
focus on storytelling through dialogue and action.
Fifth Generation filmmakers started to tell stories through their cinematography, such as
choice of color, and scene structure. In some films, dialogue is kept to a minimum so as
not to distract from the visual storytelling. For example, Yellow Earth (1984) has quite
little dialogue - preferring to conduct its story-telling through sweeping views of the
3 .
Chinese countryside. The dialogue the film does contain could be ironic; a PLA officer
visits a rural community to collect folk songs to inspire PLA soldiers. The peasant family
he stays with laments the difficulty of their lives and how hard it is to survive in the
"yellow earth." The PLA soldiers responds, reassuringly, that all will be better when the
CCP takes over - but with the backdrop of the yellow earth and panoramic, sweeping
views of a desolate countryside, it is hard to see how that could be possible. Here, the
story telling is conducted through the use of muted colors and an ambient sound track.
In Red Sorghum, Zhang Yimou uses colors to express emotions - in a film about passion,
love, and emotion, Zhang constructs shots of vibrant red and green to convey the
expression of emotion. After the same theme's and filmmaking styles of the Communist
era in the middle part of the century, seeing filmmakers willing to explore in such a way
is refreshing. It is as if the colors represent not just the themes the filmmakers want to
portray, but also their own feelings of relief - they could start to experiment, and they did
so as much as they could.
As storytelling changed in the 1980s, so did the stories themselves. Some films covered
familiar ground, like the Sino-Japanese War, the Chinese Civil War, or other periods of
political turmoil in which the CCP won. Red Sorghum and Yellow Earth were both set in
the 1940s. A new genre emerged during this period, called the scar film. These films were
primarily concerned with the excesses of the Cultural Revolution.
One example of this genre, Farewell My Concubine, is regarded as one of the first films
to attract wide attention outside of China, Farewell My Concubine is an epic that spans a
half century of Chinese history, examining some of the most turbulent historical episodes
of the 20th century. The film follows two main characters - two opera singers trained
from childhood to practice Beijing Opera. The film is at once an historical epic portraying
the Japanese invasion, the Chinese civil war, the rise of the Communist Party, and the
chaos of the Cultural Revolution - but at the same time a melodrama exploring the
relationship between the two actors. The film was at first banned in China - its themes of
preserving individualism and idiosyncrasy in the face of historical forces and fate did not
sit well with Party censors, nor did its portrayal of the Red Guards' actions during the
Cultural Revolution. This is one of the core works of the Fifth Generation of filmmakers -
and was one of the first Chinese films to attract wide attention outside of China. The film
actually begins with a denunciation of the Cultural Revolution - and it shows scenes of
Red Guard brutality and struggle sessions that must have been vivid in the memory of
audiences at the time.
This raises an interesting question - Chinese films portray a wide variety of historical
events. They reference classical Chinese literature, historical figures, and what are
seemingly obscure events. To what extent de viewers need to be aware of Chinese history
to understand these films? This would suggest a high degree of political and historical
education among ordinary Chinese, as many of the events here are not explained well in
the narratives themselves; they are set-pieces around which the filmmakers tell stories.
What may appear to be a cliched set of events from an outsider's perspective (ex: the
Sino-Japanese War) may have deeper meaning for the typical Chinese viewer. An
unanswered question is exactly what kinds of deeper meanings Chinese viewers draw
from these set-piece type plots. What does a Chinese viewer expect from a film about
World War Two? Are films sometimes designed to confound their expectations? Do they
get primed with certain feelings from set-pieces? Do they expect to see certain types of
characters? Does viewing such films satisfy an emotional need that comes from another
part of their life? Films are embedded in a larger social fabric; to understand them, it is
necessary to understand the social forces and cultural context from which they come.
How much do Chinese films draw from Beijing opera? Some of the earliest actors in the
film industry came from the operatic tradition. Several films reference, explicitly, operatic
forms - for example, The East is Red and its por
portrayal of the rise of the CCP, Lui San Jie
and its use of folk singing to combat landlords, and Farewell My Concubine with its
entire plot revolving around an opera troupe. Yet, it is interesting to see any relationships
at all - given that in the 1950s, the CCP began denouncing Beijing opera as a feudalistic
ritual and began to suppress opera. This is, in fact, depicted in Farewell My Concubine.
What are the relationships between the various types of Chinese creative expression? Are
there still relationships between film and opera? Or other forms of stage acting? Or
singing?
Chinese film is remarkable because it is at once dynamic and static. There is wide variety
in the themes expressed - and how they are expressed - between the different periods.
However, there is a great deal of continuity in some of the underlying themes. Even
recent films still cover familiar themes like the Sino-Japanese War, exploitation by
foreigners, and the plight of the poor before the CCP takeover. This continuity is
remarkable given the change in China over the 2011 century. Does this continuity reflect
desires by filmmakers and censors to send similar messages? Does it reflect demand from
a movie-going public for certain stories? Is it unique to China? Or do we see the same
types of themes in American, British, and French films too - themes core not just to a
national experience, but to the human experience writ large.
4. Modern Films, Selection
Film is a medium through which artists express the underlying passions, fears, and
thoughts of the society in which they are created. Harris and Sklar (1998) view cinema as
"a complex, historically situated, economically, grounded social and collective activity
with its own unique forms of life." (Harris and Sklar, 1998, 225). China's film industry is
growing at a fast pace and is now the second largest in the world. A new generation of
independent filmmakers using digital technology and the Internet has allowed for this
growth. An additional wrinkle that makes the Chinese film industry unique is the extent
of government control on the industry. Chinese censors review every film before release
to ensure that it conforms to the standards of the government and the Chinese Communist
Party. This implies that every film released at best demonstrates themes the Party would
like shown to the broader population, and at worst demonstrates themes that are neutral to
the goals of the Party. It is possible that films could show not only what the people are
thinking, but the government as well.
The selection of films is important if the themes taken from them are meant to be
meaningful. The films that resonate most with the broadest segment of society are
probably those films that were viewed the most. Popular films must appeal to many
people for some reason, possibly because their underlying themes and message resonate
at a very basic level. Therefore the themes that come out of these films will be simple, but
might apply to an entire society. As a proxy to measure attendance, this study uses
revenue.
After looking at lists of the top ten grossing films in China for several of the years
between 2006-2012, films were selected both in and out of those categories for popular
appeal as well as for more independent films that could reflect emerging themes. Also
discussed with people familiar with Chinese film industry to develop a section as
authentic as possible.
The modern films in the main sample were released between 1994-2012 and range in
genre, with the most representation in historical dramas about the imperial era, historical
dramas about the first half of the 20
th
century, and romantic films set in the modern era.
The following is a description of themes, characters, plots, and relationships that come
out of this selection. A note about this selection before continuing: since many of these
films are historical dramas set during China's, warring states era, many of them focus on
palace intrigue, conflict between states, and the relationships between members of the
imperial court. It is interesting to think about why these films are the most popular in
China, but it should be noted that many of the themes and characters described here
follow from the general theme of historical China.
How might one think about film? It might be useful to approach the topic from the
viewpoint of content analysis. Leites and de Sola Pool (1942) identify five criteria for
work that is appropriate for analysis:
"(1) It must refer either to syntactic characteristics of symbols ... or to semantic
characteristics"
"(2) It must indicate frequencies of occurrence of such characteristics with a high degree
of precision"
"(3) It must refer to these characteristics by terms which are general ... or by terms which
are by definition subsumable under such general terms"
"(4) It must refer to these characteristics which occur ... in universal propositions of social
science"
"(5) A high precision of the terms used to refer to the symbol characteristics
studied" (Leites and de Sola Pool, 1942, 1-2).
Higson (1989) describes some salient questions that might be asked about film -
particularly the concept of "national cinema," which focuses on how films might reflect
the underlying feelings of an entire country. He suggests asking several questions: "what
are these films about? Do they share a common style or world view? What sort of
projections of the national character do they offer? To what extent are they engaged in
'exploring, questioning, and constructing a notion of nationhood in the films themselves
and in the consciousness of the viewer?" (Higson, 1989, 36).
Finally, Mead, in her seminal article on visual anthropology, sums up the concept of
studying film as a means of understanding society - although in this context she refers to
visual technology in general:
"Many of the situations with which we deal, situations provided by thousands of
years of human history, can never be replicated in laboratory settings. But with
properly collected, annotated, and preserved visual and sound materials, we can
replicate over and over again and can painstakingly analyze the same materials.
As finer instruments have taught us more about the cosmos, so finer recording of
these precious materials can illuminate our growing knowledge and appreciation
of mankind" (Mead, 1975, 10).
This study explores Chinese film as a means of attempting to understand Chinese society.
Following Mead, Higson, and especially Leites' approaches, we examine how Chinese
film might evoke some themes or aspects of Chinese society that are otherwise unclear.
Film, as well as other media, is well documented to be an important part of Chinese
society. Even in 1988, "the typical Chinese youngster in Beijing has access to a moderate
media environment in their own home. There were one or two audio cassette units in the
respondents' homes, while radio, black and white TV sets, and colour TV sets were in
eight or nine of every 10 homes" (Greenberg, et. al., 1991, 126). This is compared to a
telephone in only one of every ten homes - media technology exceeding communications
technology shows the importance of such means of expression in the Chinese mind.
Moreover, the Chinese Communist Party has a long history of using media: "The CPC
began to tap the potentials of film as a tool of propaganda when it set up the Yan'an Film
Group in 1938" (Han and Chen, 2000, 37).
Chinese film has changed since China's economic opening. Reform has not, according to
Liu and McCormick (2011), been a government-directed project, but has come from
simple marketization. They argue the."Chinese government has somewhat relaxed
standards on films, but "this is not to say that censorship and political control has become
ineffective or irrelevant. But these controls; are different in scope and effectiveness from
the monopoly wielded by the party in the pre-reform period. This is because, first of all,
the enormous volume of media content is making close supervision increasingly difficult
and allowing for more commentary on a greater range of topics. Second, under the new
conditions of media production, the authorities have had to trade off political standards
for economic concerns to be some extent" (Liu and McCormick, 2011, 122). The
government is less able to control what people say and watch.
This tension might reveal more about Chinese society than if it were controlled totally by
the government. Film becomes more evocative of the latent proclivities of the Chinese
citizenry, while retaining some aspects of the government control that makes it interesting
to consider from a government perspective: The themes, characters, and plots described
here are an attempt to derive some broader!meaning out of a complex and fragmented
data set.
In addition to relaxing standards, film studios are now seen as part of industry. "Film
studios are now being treated as enterprises, as other industries are, in terms of their
management. The early reform in separating the party from the administration took place
in film studios in the form of letting the director rather than the party secretary oversee
the production .... " (Han and Chen, 2000, 39). Rather than being "state-financed cultural
institutions," they become "industrial enterprises." Ticket sales now drive studio budgets
rather than government subsidies.
Social effects are still considered when planning films, but "as a former director of
Beijing Film Studio remarked, the film industry must first and foremost survive before it
can talk about development because "you cannot sing the leitmotif with an empty
stomach." (Han and Chen, 2000, 39). Commercial interests play a primary role in film
development, focusing on themes close to the lives of viewers.
We might, therefore, temper our assessment of the extent to which the Chinese
government has a role in directing film content. In the case of major propaganda films
(such as The Founding of a Republic), the government heavily subsidized and promoted
the films, but in the case of independent films like Ocean Heaven, it probably has a
smaller role to play.
How does this affect an analyst's interpretation of the themes, characters, plots, and
relationships depicted? Beebe has written on the Jungian illumination of film (Beebe,
1996), and in particular the concept of a collective unconscious. He quotes Jung as
writing "One does not become enlightened by imagining figures of light, but by making
the darkness conscious" (Beebe, 1996, 579). Film might be a medium in which the
unconsciousness of a collective people could be portrayed - the hopes, fears, dreams that
they hold but do not say out loud might be evoked in film images, "as if it were a regular
part of reality" (Beebe, 1996, 579). The party still plays a role, as it created new forms of
film that cloak state ideology in social morals: "A second aspect of state-industry
collaboration was an effort to create a more attractive form of propaganda through a
peculiar hybrid of political authority and market forces: the updated major melody
film" (Wan and Kraus, 2002, 429).
As an interpretive framework, therefore, it could be illuminating to consider the concept
of a collective unconscious in tandem with aspects of state control. The state might,
through support of certain films.(concept of picking winning horses), signal its desire to
mold the interests of the Chinese people unconsciously. This approach also implies that
the films that perform the best or are talked about the most best represent some
underlying Chinese values.
5. Possible themes
Early films and relationships with Leites themes
In comparing the early Chinese films with the Leitesian categories, it is important to
make one caveat - the Leitesian categories were developed out of three film traditions
that did not have a history of government control, or perhaps even a desire to send
political messages through film. If one can make any generalizations about Chinese film
as a whole - they try to send a message, They are not stories about love or killers and
victims or families - they are stories about politics, society, or human nature that involve
families, love, and killers/victims. Leites an'd Wolfenstein, approaching films from a
psychocultural perspective, focused on psychological characteristics of film characters,
themes, and plots to understand the psychological background of the societies from
which these films came. It is not clear that films coming from a society that values state
control over media - and films with clear moral/political messages - could be analyzed in
the same way. We observe some similarities between some of the broad themes Leites
described, but the content of the themes differs.
Wolfenstein and Leites describe at length "lovers and loved ones." In particular, they
describe the good/bad girl, vamps, the phenomenon of loving twice, types of plots/scenes
where love can occur, and national variations. Early Chinese films, as prior authors and
we have described, do not emphasize love and lovers. Relationships and courting
certainly exist, but they serve as vehicles for describing socialist or communist lessons;
