films do not explore relationships for the sake of exploring relationships. For example, in
Spring in a Small Town, the primary storyline is a love triangle and the film describes its
evolution. However, it is apparent that the characters are stand-in representations of
China itself; old China, new China, and the Chinese people. The psychological
experience of the characters is subordinated to the social commentary role they fill.
Wolfenstein and Leites write that in American films, heroes and heroines are unbound by
family ties. They suggest the American emphasis is on "the family you make, as against
the family you come from." There is some mixed evidence from Chinese films.
.
Characters in early Chinese films are shown to care about the families from which they
come - for example, in one case (National Customs), two sisters travel to Shanghai for
education, and while they argue and disagree, in the end stick together. But yet, there is a
clear emphasis on the family made as well - for example, in Street Angel, the plot of the
film is around a budding relationship by neighbors. Towards the Communist Era, we see
less emphasis on families; they become vehicles, like relationships in general, for
promoting socialist/communist ideals. In some cases, they are not that important at all -
for example, in The Red Detachment of Women, where the primary protagonist's family is
barely mentioned. Communist cadres are rarely ever shown with families - they are
depicted as independent and intrepid, free of the ties that hold back the Chinese people
and ready to work - wholeheartedly, without any distractions, for the advancement of the
Chinese people. The omission of family ties here is symbolic of their commitment to the
greater family from which all Chinese people come: the Chinese motherland. Given the
emphasis on Confucian values in many films, one could read the omission of family as a
signal that Chinese characters focus on an overarching sense of family (or they are meant
to) as country.
There seems, according to Wolfenstein and Leites, to be an inordinate amount of killings
and murders in American, British, and French movies. There are few explorations of
violence in Chinese film in the early era; they would have never passed censors. When
violence does occur, it is to demonstrate some, social message (landlords shown abusing
peasants), to demonstrate the historical abuse of the Chinese people (foreign troops
massacring Chinese civilians), or the destruction of China's enemies (Nationalist troops
being slaughtered by PLA soldiers). There are no overt crime or mystery films like there
are in the western tradition. Violence, death, and murder all occur within the context of
the political and social stories the filmmakers seek to tell; they are, in the Leitesian sense,
"killing with a purpose." But the purpose is not to conceal a crime, or the discovery of
mysteries, or frustration in love - the purpose is the promotion of some political or
cultural or moral objective.
The perspectives shown in these films do not, like in American films, show events from
many viewpoints. While to some extent characters will spy on one another - or plot
against one another - there are few depictions of characters looking or being looked at,
except in the case of films that deal with professional performers (Farewell My
Concubine). This major theme described in the Movies book has little relevance here.
In all, the Leitesian themes described in the Wolfenstein and Leites book have general,
but limited application to early Chinese films. Early Chinese filmmakers drew inspiration
from traditional Chinese stories, Beijing opera, and a desire to portray leftist themes. This
is a widely different cultural and social tradition from the societies that produced the
films Wolfenstein and Leites studied. While Chinese filmmakers may have been inspired
by western films, the divergence in cultural values, political ideology, the role of the state,
and historical events when these films were produced make it challenging to identify the
psychocultural themes in the Chinese films that Wolfenstein and Leites found in the
western films.
What are some of the themes that come out of older Chinese films? Because older films
are so different from modern films, we describe some of the common themes among
older films separately from modern films, later, we will discuss elements of continuity
and their possible significance.
What interested older Chinese filmmakers and their Party patrons?
Revolution as a means of social change
The leftist films of the 1930s and the Communist-era films emphasized the necessity of
revolution to transform society. The films set up an image of the "old society" - explicitly
.......
referring to it as such - and demonstrate how building the "new society" requires
fundamental revolution at the level of the masses. The entire storyline of The Red
Detachment of Women argues to this point. While many of the leftist and communist
films portray revolution as unequivocally positive, the Fifth Generation filmmakers are
more ambivalent - as witnessed by the brutality of the Red Guards during the Cultural
Revolution portrayed in Farewell, My Concubine
Foreigners are not to be trusted
Foreigners, in earlier Chinese film, are portrayed as even more untrustworthy than in
modern film. In some modern films, we observe characters working for foreigners in
professional settings - while potentially rapacious and capitalist, they are not portrayed as
fundamentally evil people. For example, in the Flowers of War, the main character is a
westerner who takes on the duty of protecting a number of Chinese schoolgirls from the
Japanese during the Rape of Nanking. In the earlier films, they are portrayed as
universally evil. For example, a film from as late as the 1980s - The Burning of Yuan
Ming Yuan - portrays what to westerners would appear to be caricatures of foreigners,
intent only on the destruction of China and the Chinese people.
Authority is a stabilizing force for the world, but only when uncorrupted
Authority, as portrayed in modern film, relates to the ability of a central power to impose
stability on a chaotic China. China is portrayed as a large country, which at times was
lawless, that benefited from the imposition of CCP control. Certainly, there are portrayals
of corruption - any of the films dealing with landlords, such as Liu San Jie, portray
landlords are inherently corrupt and evil. Their authority may be stabilizing, but is bad for
the Chinese people (which is the most important thing). The leftist era films do not push
for authority to stabilize the country; their objectives seemed to be purely for some sort of
change from the prior system. The Communist era films, many of them retrospective
looks at the heroism of the Party and Party members, are more overt about the positive
aspects of CCP stability. For example, the Party-produced musical, The East is Red,
comments on this. However, there is less implication that China itself is a chaotic place
by nature; this may be a new theme in the modern film. There are also signals that
authority cannot maintain total control over anything - much of the subtext of Yellow
Earth revolves around how, no matter how hard the Party tries, it will be difficult to
control the earth itself (weather, the rivers, growing patterns, etc).
Sacrifice is noble and often necessary
Sacrifice is a theme that shows up often in earlier films. Sacrifice can be minor -
sacrificing happiness or pleasure for the sake of personal improvement, like in National
Customs, or it could be total, the sacrifice of one's life for a greater cause, like in The Big
Road. This theme has direct continuity throughout modern film.
Family remains the most important thing
Earlier films are ambivalent about the importance of family. In some cases, family
members are set up as foils - with one representing the old China and one representing
the new China, such as in Liu Hulan. Here loyalty to family members is expected - to an
extent - but the greater good is more importa
portant. In The Goddess, the main character
prostitutes herself to support her son's education - a sign that family remains extremely
important in some cases. But in Spring in a Small Town, a woman contemplates leaving
her husband when an old flame resurfaces. The films demonstrate far more variance on
how Chinese should react to family members.
Coping with a new world
Only one film in this sample, This Life of Mine, explicitly examines the experience of an
ordinary Chinese in response to the remarkable social change in China throughout the
early part of the 20" century. Rather than thinking about coping with a new world, the
Chinese, particularly in the Communist era films, are shown as enacting change and
embracing the newness. Since many of the leftist films were intended to create a new
world, the characters are less concerned with coping with the newness; if anything, they
are portrayed as coping with the old world.
Loyalty
Loyalty in Chinese film is a complex subject. One immediate question that arises is -
loyalty to whom? Are individual loyal to their country, their people, their family,
themselves, or to a combination of all of these? A full description of the many ways in
which loyalty manifests is beyond the scope of this analysis, but loyalty is clearly an
important aspect of Chinese culture - given how it shows up in every film in our sample.
One interesting research question might be to understand how perceptions of loyalty have
changed in Chinese media from the inception of the film industry to the present - to what
are people loyal, and why?
Capitalism as a double-edged sword
Early Chinese films portray one side of capitalism - the dangerous side. Landlords,
business owners, and those in power are portrayed as supporting a corrupt system to
enrich themselves and to bolster foreigners. This is evident in films as varied as Liu San
Jie, Street Angel, The Goddess, and Serfs. Capitalism, not surprisingly, is denounced
roundly by these films, which were created under leftist and Communist influence.
Changing social norms in China
Similar to adapting to a new society, the early Chines films focused not on actual
changing social norms in China, but the norms the filmmakers wanted to have changed.
Particularly the leftist films were concerned with the power of landlords and feudalism
and were designed not to show ongoing changes, but strong desires for change.
Interestingly, there are few depictions of the Chinese attempting to adapt to changes in
rule; this should be evident in a film like This Life of Mine, but it is absent.
The importance of duty
Similar to loyalty, an immediate question this raises is - duty to whom? Chinese in these
films are often faced with competing demands for duty. This theme is present in every
film in the sample, and like loyalty, a full description of the important of duty is beyond
this analysis. Suffice to say, duty is on the mind of the ordinary Chinese portrayed in
these films - and indeed even in the minds of the collaborators and landlords - but how it
manifests differs.
The effect of war and violence on people
Conflict is portrayed as debilitating to ordinary people - but only when violence is
committed by the enemy. There are depictions of brutality - such as in The Burning of
Yuan Ming Yuan - but the atrocities are perpetrated by foreigners, the Japanese, or
variously the Nationalists/Communists (depending on the perspective). Violence on
civilians perpetrated by PLA members is nonexistent; indeed the ordinary people
welcome them as saviors. On the whole, this theme shows up far less in the early films as
compared to modern films.
The balance between business and family
This theme is really not evident in the earlier films. In Red Sorghum, business and family
become blended - as the main character operates a liquor distillery with her husband -
but there is not the sense the business and family are separate entities. The same is true in
Yellow Earth, Farewell My Concubine, and The Red Detachment of Women. Business and
family are one in the same in many cases ...:
The point of marriage
Marriages in the earlier films are arranged (Red Sorghum, Yellow Earth); there is little
emotional exploration on the meaning of marriage.
The Chinese West
This theme does not really show up in the earlier films. The focus of the films is on the
coastal regions of China where the revolution occurred. There were certain depictions of
lawlessness/lack of government control - for example in Red Sorghum or Yellow Earth -
so there is some thematic link, but not as explicit as in the more modern films. Moreover,
the films that do have a thematic link only show up in the 1980s.
Fate
There seems to be quite a bit of agency in these films. While individuals may be under
the control of landlords or feudal lords (Serfs), they still have an inner desire to reject
their fate and to overthrow their oppressors. The Chinese people, in these revolutionary
type films, are portrayed as strong if they take agency over their lives. For example, in
The Red Detachment of Women, the main character is a revolutionary who chooses to
escape her feudal lifestyle and to obtain her own vision of China, of course led by the
CCP. In Queen of Sports, the main character is naturally gifted at sports and so expects to
win all competitions; but her fate can only be achieved by her own actions - by working
hard.
Acceptance
As mentioned in the discussion of fate, there is a denunciation of acceptance. Chinese
should not accept their place in life or the circumstances in which they live; they have the
power to change it for the better - preferably through revolution. This is contrast for
modern films, where Chinese tend to accept what happens to this - resignation.
Aimlessness of youth
This theme does not really show up in the earlier films. Chinese youth in early Chinese
film are determined to liberate the people from oppressors; they tend to take on character
.
-
:
roles of young revolutionary firebrands.
Relationship with modern Chinese film themes
There is a much stronger relationship between modern films and the Leitesian themes, as
relaxed censorship standards and less state control in the modern era has led to greater
thematic exploration. There are several, recurring themes seen in the modern films;
themes that cut across Leites' but seem characteristic of Chinese movies
Revolution as a means of social change
Revolution, revolutionary behavior, and rebellion are shown as positive forces for social
change against corrupt governments. Rebels need not have much strength; as long as their
cause is correct, they have the will, and their ideology morally sound, they will succeed
because the people will rise up to support them. In fact, revolution may be considered to
be better than peaceful change, for with revolution, enemies will be destroyed or
marginalized, while with political compromises or elections, that cannot be guaranteed.
Revolution will always be against corrupt officials, and it starts at a small scale but
rapidly gains support.
This is shown overtly in The Founding of a Republic and The Founding of a Party, two
films sponsored by the government to portray the revolution and change in China in a
favorable light. Films also show the benefits of revolution indirectly. Some films portray
gradual change in Chinese society, but 'invariably show that there are downsides to
graduate change: Cell Phone, The Road-Home, Break Up Club, and other films show
Chinese society's gradual westernization and all of the problems associated with it. At
some level, there is a message that a systemic shock would have been better.
Revolution and rebellion are themes as well in multiple films set in the Chinese historical
era. In Hero, The Emperor's Shadow, Curse of the Golden Flower, and White Vengeance
(among others), the plots revolve around "revolution as a means to change corrupt
governments.
Finally, in The Silent Holy Stones, we see another form of rebellion - more subtle. The
film follows a young child living at a Buddhist monastery and his attempts to rebel
against both Buddhist and traditional Tibetan culture to embrace the brave new world
China is entering; the film's message is ambiguous as to whether or not this will be
positive for China.
Foreigners are not to be trusted
Foreigners are represented in historical films to have caused the majority of the strife in
China. Japan and the United States are the big culprits. Foreigners are portrayed as using
China and the Chinese for their own purpose's to the detriment of the Chinese people,
primarily for economic gain. For example, in Bodyguards and Assassins, the ethnic-
Chinese police chief of Hong Kong, on the eve of a visit by Sun Yat Sen in the early
1900s, was specifically told by the British authorities to ignore any conflict between the
Chinese - to let them settle problems by themselves. They are portrayed as exploiting
Hong Kong and the Chinese.
Perhaps the epitome of this type of film is Autobiography of a World War 2 Orphan. This
is an untraditional film; it is a documentary describing the life of a girl orphaned by the
Second World War during the Japanese occupation; her parents were killed and she was
taken in by a peasant family. The resounding message is clear - foreigners in general are
not to be trusted, because the devastation they might wreak on China is immense and this
must be minimized. Unsurprisingly, this film fwas wholly produced by the Bureau for
External Cultural Relations of the Chinese Ministry of Culture.
However, it is somewhat surprising that in many films set in the modern era, Chinese
citizens are seen adopting many foreign habits. Foreign culture and people are sometimes
objects of veneration or desire:
Authority is a stabilizing force for the world, but only when uncorrupted
In some films, authority is found to be a stabilizing force. A strong authority with
hegemony over tianxia (heaven and earth) is one of the only things that brought China
peace - the Qin dynasty - and this fact is referenced in some films. On the other hand, it
is also shown that authority that has become corrupted by power or other influences will
lead to pain and suffering for the people, and this was the case with the KMT and
Japanese during the 1930s and 1940s, leading to the CCP taking power. Politics of this
sort is rarely ever treated in modern era films.
In The Lost Bladesman, for example, the general Guan Yu is separated from the forces of
Liu Bei and must contend with Liu Bei's sworn enemy - Cao Cao. Cao Cao is shown as
being interested in unifying China and imposing order because China (and the Chinese
people) are "messy." They are apt to fight one another and cause trouble, and only with a
strong authority can they be stopped. In cases of weak authority (A Woman a Gun and A
Noodle Shop; Xiao Wu) the Chinese make war, steal from, and generally abuse each other
until an uncorrupt official comes to straighten things out.
Sacrifice is noble and often necessary
Many of these films portray struggles between different groups, necessitating sacrifice for
success. Common people and leaders alike are expected to take risks, sacrifice
themselves, and possibly sacrifice those they love in the name of victory. This is viewed
as a noble and proper task, with the underlying message being that the Chinese people
may have an unusually high tolerance for sacrifice and are particularly resilient. Perhaps
the most evident example of this is the film Sacrifice, where the main character raises an
orphaned child until adulthood for the sole purpose of exacting revenge against a corrupt
King. He sacrifices himself to allow his "son" to land the killing blow.
But sacrifice is not just for at a national level; it can be personal as well. In Lost in
Thailand, a well-meaning (somewhat of a bumpkin) traveler takes great pains to help a
fellow traveler he finds lost in Thailand, although his help is more of a hindrance than
help. In The Flowers of War, both prostitutes and westerners sacrifice in the face of the
Japanese occupation - sacrificing even their lives. .
Family remains the most important thing
Despite some comments below about how the Confucian family is portrayed, generally
speaking, families are still shown to be the most important elements of a person's life.
There is the implication that family members are the only ones you can really trust, and
when you find that you cannot trust your family, there is nothing left for you in the world.
This value resonates in many of the films across all time periods.
In The Road Home, a son leaves his busy city life to return to his home village to bury his
father. In observance of tradition, he organizes a procession to carry his father's coffin
back from the city morgue to where he will be buried, learning more about his father's
courtship with his mother along the way. In a display of filial piety, to respect his
mother's wishes, for one day he teaches the children at the school where his father taught,
making his father's spirit happy that he is remembered.
In A Simple Life, we find that adopted family members matter just as much as true blood.
In this film, a man's maid/cook, who has been with his family since he was a child, passes
away. The film is a flashback of her deteriorating health and how he was steady in
spending time with her and helping her as she passed. The film shows many Chinese as
neglectful of the elderly, but that the right moral thing to do is to always take care of
them.
Coping with a new world
Some films explore the theme of how to cope with the changes coming from a new
world. In these films, the characters have to deal with the possibility of a new order
(societal, economic, etc) coming, how it will change their lives, and in one case, we see
how an individual copes with major changes in life.
For example, in Platform (2009), there are two scenes that highlight this concept. The
film portrays a government/party-run theatre group that travels around Shanxi province
putting on patriotic performances. It is set in the 1970s-1990s in a rapidly changing
China. In a scene early in the film, the troupe members have a party, dancing very
awkwardly with each other. Later on in the film, after troupe members have become more
exposed to the outside world, they are shown once again socializing - this time in a disco,
dancing in a very western manner. The film shows how the characters deal with the
changing world, with some doing well and others not.
In Sunflower, the characters are shown dealing with the changes in Chinese society - first
in the aftermath of the Cultural Revolution, and then after the subsequent economic
liberalization of the 1980s. Family tension is heightened, not lessened, by these changes.
Loyalty
Loyalty is a character trait that common in modern films. Loyalty is presented as a virtue,
and strong bonds of loyalty (e.g., not breaking under torture) are what make groups
successful in their missions. Loyalty to the people, as well as the government, is
emphasized. In contrast, disloyalty to China or the Chinese people is considered to be a
cardinal sin.
In If You are the One and If You Are the One 2, loyalty is not demonstrated in a political
:
or revolutionary context, but in an emotional context. In a man's search for a wife, he
finds that it is ultimately loyalty that means: the most when finding an appropriate
relationship - signaling that some values to: which the Chinese ascribe in the political
realm hold true in the personal realm as well ???
In The Emperor's Shadow, one of the main characters is a musician serving in the
Emperor's court. He owed his life to the Emperor, as when they were young children they
were both held captive by a rival state (prior to Chinese reunification, which was depicted
as a lawless period), and the Emperor pleaded with his captors to spare both of their lives
when they were to be executed. The Emperor's loyalty to him remained firm, but as the
musician realized the Emperor was sinking into tyranny and decadence, he began to
rebel.
Capitalism as a double-edged sword
Capitalism can be portrayed as a double-edged sword for Chinese society and citizens.
Almost all characters in these films agree that China was an incredibly poor country
twenty years ago, and they all agree that capitalism was one of the forces that helped
China grow into the economic powerhouse it is today. Mao even says in The Founding of
a Republic that China "need capitalists." However, they do not all agree that capitalism
has overall been a force for good in Chinese society. Characters working in the business
world are portrayed as greedy, concerned only for making money or their own career
advancement to the detriment of their family or personal lives. The loss of a social safety
net such as the one that existed pre-economic. liberalization is reflected in the struggles of
the main character in The Postmodern Life, of My Aunt to survive in old age, and multiple
characters across films comment on the importance of having sons to support oneself in
old age. And in many romantic films, there is concern over where to find the money to
have a house, or to start a business, complicating life where it used to be much easier.
In Million Dollar Crocodile, capitalism is depicted as having created greed. In the film,
the antagonists seek to kill a giant crocodile for profit - they and other characters
repeatedly risk their lives for it. Only a police officer trying to stop them is shown as
actually caring about something other than money - human life and justice.
Changing social norms in China
There are some signs that filmmakers recognize there are now changing social mores in
China. A couple films show homosexuality and its growing acceptance among the youth
of China; it is not portrayed as something to be shunned or a humiliation, but just as
another part of life that characters seem to accept as normal. Ocean Heaven is a film
about the plight of the developmentally disabled in China. They are portrayed as having
to be independent, with little support from the Chinese government and what little
support they have coming from friends and family. There are also signs of westernization.
In films with young professionals, they are not portrayed as drinking baijiu, the
traditional Chinese rice liquor, but western style red wine. These are all signs that
Chinese society could be shifting toward more of a western, open model, though these are
only preliminary signs and may not be representative of society as a whole.
In Cell Phone, the plot revolves around characters adapting to the introduction of cell
phones in China, which, according to the film-makers, have led to moral decline. The film
emphasizes how overconnection by cell phones and the Internet has made it easier for
spouses to deceive each other in Chinese society. Characters when using phones are
portrayed as perpetually lying, and this being socially acceptable, but emotionally
damaging for their partners.
Sunflower is concerned entirely with changing society. Set in three periods: just after the
Cultural Revolution, the opening of China in the 1980s, and finally the late 1990s when
China began to fully embrace capitalism. It shows how China began to heal from the
Cultural Revolution (the father in the film repeatedly references his time in a labor camp)
and also shows some aspects of Chinese culture that might not be evident to the west - in
one scene, the teenaged main character sneaks away from home to watch a film with his
community in the town center. But despite societal norms shifting over time, one aspect
that did not change was the importance of family in Chinese lives - there are continued
attention to family, marriage, and finding houses throughout the film.
And yet with change there remains continuity - structures social position and status in
China remain. This is evident in the master-student relationship demonstrated in many of
the films, but in business and family contexts as well. Bosses manage, workers work,
fathers lead families, while younger sons obey. Even among social acquaintances this is
true, in the Sorcerer and the White Snake the teacher in the film says to a junior, "You
shouldn't refuse a gift from a senior." In Don't Got Breaking My Heart the CEO of a
financial services firm throws a tantrum and exercises his managerial power to the
extreme after being spurned by a woman - power dynamics for Chinese men seem to
have to be exercised somehow, whether it is in family or public life.
The importance of duty
-
Repeatedly, the theme of duty comes up. There are many different kinds of duty in
Chinese culture, with duty to the Emperor, and crown paramount in the historical era, and
with duty to one's parents and family now paramount in the modern era. In films,
characters repeatedly stray from the path that duty would take them, only to return later
after realizing what truly matters to them. And when characters continue to not fulfill
their duty or even openly flout it, it is because the person to whom they must show that
respect is not fulfilling his or her own duty. This is often the case with rebellions against
emperors or other false leaders. Character's" duty to their families often takes the form of
taking care of their parents in old age, or going to visit them and showing that they care
about them. Duty for children is to provide an education and possibly a house for the
child and his or her spouse in which to live, a sort of reciprocal gift giving scheme that is
an interesting element of Chinese culture. Duty toward one's significant other is rarely
discussed.
The plot of A Railway in the Clouds revolves around the construction of a railway
between Qinghai and Tibet, the highest altitude railroad in the world. The movie opens
with depictions of the first two attempts to build railroads - first by hand in the 1950s and
then with more modern technology in the 1970s. The main character had family members
or friends die during both of those construction attempts, which failed, and leads a third
attempt with a military unit in the early 2000s out of a sense of duty. He even misses his
mother's funeral because he chose to stay at the work site at a critical moment; duty to
country surpasses duty to family.
In For There to be Love, it is portrayed that duty exists not just to one's team, but also
one's society. Four young people, through volunteer work, seek the meaning of love -
there are multiple scenes of the main characters in this movie helping others or coming to
the aid of strangers on the street. Duty extends to one's society as much as it does to one's
close associations.
The effect of war and violence on the Chinese people
Some films explore the effect of war and violence on ordinary people and seek to
understand what people become when exposed to conflict, and why. This is particularly
true for the films looking at Japanese involvement in China during the Second World War.
Japanese soldiers, particularly in The Flowers of War, are portrayed as brutal savages,
reduced to base instincts of human nature, while Chinese soldiers and civilians are
portrayed as innocent and morally upstanding. Several films considering the Chinese
historical period look at war in a similar fashion. The message here may be that war
drives humans to revert to a natural state, with some people morally correct becoming
better people through war and those with naturally "evil" tendencies becoming far worse.
The balance between business and family
With the rise of capitalism in Chinese society, there is a growing delicate balance
between the needs of one's work and the needs of one's family. To be sure, this was
certainly true to an extent even under the command economy, but the way it is
approached in film seems to imply that it is thought of with growing concern. This may
be related to the fact that many Chinese now work for firms with foreign ownership.
Regardless, this interplay is often shown in films, with almost all characters ultimately
choosing family over work. The idea seems to be that family will always be there, while
professional opportunities will come and go.
This is shown in Qin Jia Guo Nian, a film about hear-in-laws celebrating Chinese New
Year with their children, who are dating. The son is struggling in starting a business, and
his girlfriend has sold a house given to her by her mother to finance his idea. With the
parents coming to visit and the business struggling, the film examines the complex
relationship between family and business life, with the ultimate message that family
might not always be the most important; but it should be.
The point of marriage
Many films explore the thinking of characters are they explore the meaning and point of
marriage. Repeatedly, characters ask about the point of marriage, and whether it is better
to marry a friend or someone you love, or whether love at first sight truly exists, or why
people get married at all. All of these films have different answers to these questions, but
in the exploration of what is important, all the characters do agree that it is necessary to
get married no matter what, no matter to whom. Marriage is considered to be the only
way to get a family, which is crucial to Chinese society, and those without marriages are
shunned or considered to be outsiders.
Women get married to men not necessarily because of money or power, but because of
devotion. In I Do, a woman decides to, after the re-emergence of an old boyfriend, to
marry a divorcee who had been emotionally supporting her throughout the breakup rather
than the newly returned, wealthy, ex-boyfriend. In The Locked Door, a woman's returned
lover actively treats her poorly.
The Chinese West
A theme has emerged in Chinese film where they are set in a lawless "Chinese west."
These films are set during historical periods when Chinese emperors or governments had
much less sway over far-flung provinces, and they depict corruption, violence, and
negative human qualities being counteracted by men (or women) of the people who take
righteous stands. An Inaccurate Memoir is an example of this type of film.
In Let the Bullets Fly, Ashes of Time, Design of Death, and Wind Blast, the west is also
portrayed. There is no law - the characters have to make do by themselves, and when
authority figures (police/military) come to employ justice/law, they find resistance from
the residents. These films deal with the struggle between good and evil in lawlessness;
these films assume that in the absence of justice (imposed on an unwilling populace), the
Chinese will lose civilization.
Fate
Some films concern themselves with fate, For example, in The Law of Attraction, there
are short vignettes concerning love, where it appears that it is luck that brings people
together. In The Emperor's Shadow, the Emperor and his young friend are only saved at
the beginning of the film from execution by the intercedence of fate.
Fate works in the opposite direction as well. In Memory of Love, a woman gets into a car
accident with her lover and wakes up with amnesia - but the doctor helping her recover is
her incredibly stoic husband who was shocked to find not only that it was his wife who
was so seriously injured, but also that it was under such circumstances.
Acceptance
Related to the concept of fate is acceptance. Chinese people not only have things happen
to them - exogenous forces operating outside of their control - but when they do, they
deal with it. They are resigned to their fate and once it seems like something cannot
change; they no longer make any effort to escape their situations. In The Postmodern Life
of My Aunt, the main character was reduced to being a vegetable vendor on the street
after a series of calamities and stoically bore 'her fate.
A similar situation is depicted in. Lost in, Beijing - although this film was banned by the
Chinese government for its themes and content. Depicting the aftermath of a rape of a
migrant worker who became pregnant, the main character "Pingguo," who was raped, is
treated as a pawn by her rapist and her husband. Over the course of the film, she becomes
resigned to her fate. But in a move that most Chinese film characters would never
consider, at the close of the film, she collects her child from her rapist's house, a large
sum of money, and walked out the door into the rain. But note that while this film
enjoyed great success outside of China, the government first censored and then cut large
portions of it for its messaging.
Social mobility as a concept is rare. Characters who try for it, like in A Big Score succeed
based only on luck, and in countless. romantio movies ambitious characters are satiated by
love, not by success. Despite the explosions in entrepreneurship in Chinese society over
the last three decades, it remains unclear whether this has fully translated to film - or
what factors prevent it from becoming fully, translated.
Aimlessness of youth
Chinese youth are sometimes depicted as aimless and without direction. In Buddha
Mountain, the film follows three such youth who did not enter college and express their
frustrations over succeeding in a Chinese society where they might be left behind. They
cause trouble, interact with the underbelly of society, and annoy their elders - who view
them as embarrassments. Lost in Beijing has a similar message and has the same director.
Young men are also sometimes shown in film with lives utterly lacking meaning. In The
Breakup Club, the men in the film have little direction - their jobs are dead-ends and it is
hard for them to find relationships. Several other films portray young men in the same
way; women are rarely (except in the above cases and in Xiao Wu) portrayed in such a
fashion.
Fantasy
Fantasy is rare in Chinese film, as are dreams or hallucinations or things that might seem
more common in American or western films. The Sorcerer and the White Snake is the
only film in this group that could be considered purely fantasy, with depictions of magic
and demons. Demons are portrayed are tricksters trying to connive humans into doing
their bidding, while those fighting demons are portrayed as morally upstanding defenders
of the people.
The lack of dreams and fantasy in films other than this is an intriguing point that bears
further consideration from a psychocultural or' psychoanalytical perspective.
Continuity
Is there continuity in Chinese film? Many of these themes do seem to show up in both the
earlier (pre-1990s) and in the modern (post-1990s) films. Common trends include the
importance of revolution, authority, the dangers of capitalism, and sacrifice and duty as
important things Chinese must think about.
What is clear is that there is continuity; what is not clear is why there is continuity.
Continuity could arise from several different factors - there are a few possible reasons for
continuity (these are not all the possible reasons):
Cultural continuity - it is possible that Chinese culture has remained (in large
part) consistent over time, at least in terms of core societal values that might be
represented in mass media. It is possible that Chinese have an underlying national
collective unconscious, whereby they value things like authority, revolutionary
change, and sacrifice. Continuity in these films could be evidence for cultural
continuity - although further evidence could come from examinations of even
earlier Chinese media (classical Chinese literature, folk stories, etc).
Censorship - it may be that Chinese censors throughout the 20""century
supported consistent themes because of political expediency. At the times when
these films were released, it is possible both Nationalist and Communist censors
desired these themes to be shown to, the people - or at least were neutral to their
exposure. This may not always be true, for example, it is unclear why Nationalist
censors would support films agitating. for revolutionary change to fundamental
societal values at the time when they were in power. There is also the additional
question about intent; does it change anything about our interpretation of the films
if the censors intended for certain themes to be let through, versus if they only
allowed themes or were indifferent to them?
Universality - is it possible that the themes demonstrated in these films are
common across time periods because of some sense of universality? Are there
common themes, tropes, characters; and plots that all human cultures value?
Regardless of political situation, government type, economic status, or innate
cultural values, it could be that humans are engineered (adopting an evolutionary
psychology viewpoint) to be naturally predisposed to like authority, or to like
loyalty and duty, or to distrust outsiders, or to advocate for equality. It may be that
these themes are not inherently Chinese, Nationalist, or Communist, but
inherently human.
--
We do not offer firm answers, but only try to raise some questions about how to interpret
these films. All three of the possibilities mentioned above could be "true" - or it may be
possible that a different force entirely is at work
Where there is thematic change - from where does it come? Berry (2004) suggests scenes
of romantic love in modern-era films could be a western import. Chinese filmmakers
certainly observed foreign films after China's economic opening, if not before - whether
those channels were official or unofficial. Scholars remark that Western film theorists
visited China and popularized western ideas about film (particularly the relationship
between film and society/as a reflection of society). Understanding and identifying the
sources of thematic change could inform how Chinese film might change in the future.
If film has changed, this raises an important question - what is Chinese cinema? Should
Chinese cinema be analyzed as a cohesive body? Are there categories of Chinese cinema
that could be interpreted in different ways? If there are different categories of Chinese
cinema, would it then be important to assess the psychology of the characters portrayed
differently? What era of cinema would be most appropriate for certain kinds of analysis?
Chinese cinema is a rich collection of portrayals of China and the Chinese people, but it
is complex enough to warrant serious thinking about how to approach analysis.
-
6. Broader Character Types
Older Films
Out of the older films emerges a group of characters; some of these can also be found in
the modern films, but these seem to be the most prevalent. As described in the themes, we
see that in early Chinese films, the emphasis is on a dialectic - the fight between the old
society and the new. Particularly for films produced prior to the Cultural Revolution, we
see film plots designed in service of a political message. Due to the need to send a
political message in this way, these films have three main character archtypes: the
enemies, the heroes, and the rest.
Landlord/Capitalist
These characters represent the "old society" the revolutionaries in Chinese film seek to
destroy. These landlords are often portrayed as rapacious;, squeezing every last cent of
profit from their feudal charges, and living lives of luxury and idleness while the peasants
toil on their behalf. They are shown as corrupt and evil, and with so much power that a
single person alone cannot defeat them. Often a revolutionary hero will organize a
collective to overthrow the landlord and institute a new society of equality - economic
and social.
Films: Liu Sanjie, Liu Hulan, The Goddess, This life of Mine, Song of the Fishermen, The
Red Detachment of Women, Serfs, The East is Red
Young revolutionary
The young revolutionary is impressionable and passionate. He or she, for women can also
be freedom fighters, have personally experienced or witnessed injustice at the hands of
the oppressor, whether those oppressors were the landlords and feudal class, the Japanese,
or the reactionaries fighting against the CCP. These characters are always portrayed as the
heroes, and they may sometimes sacrifice themselves for the cause - or they may be the
objects of sacrifice (others sacrificing themselves for the hero). The revolutionary is the
standard-bearer for the construction of the new society, which is a theme that occupies so
many older films.
Films: Liu Sanjie, Liu Hulan, This Life of. Mine, Red Sorghum, The Big Road, Yellow
Earth, The Red Detachment of Women, The East is Red, Serfs
Older bystander
The older bystander is too old to take direct action, and filmmakers use this character as a
tool for exposition. In one film, This Life of Mine, the entire premise of the film is that the
main character has been a bystander for several decades of Chinese history, allowing
viewers to observe history from his perspective. Films are ambivalent about these
characters; sometimes, they may provide advice to main characters, while in other cases
they are only bystanders or servants. As they are used to move plots forward, they do not
occupy much attention.
Films: Queen of Spots, Liu San Jie, Liu Hulan, The Goddess, This Life of Mine, Red
Sorghum, Spring in a Small Town, Farewell 'My Concubine, Yellow Earth, The Red
Detachment of Women, Serfs
Modern Films
Out of the modern films emerges a group of character archetypes that could be reflections
on a broader Chinese society:
Charismatic older leader, morally upstanding
This character is often portrayed as a leader of whatever group is depicted in the movie.
He is older, always male, and speaks from years of experience. He is morally
incorruptible and all who follow him obey orders without question. He is guided by
strong feelings of ideology. He is rarely portrayed as being distracted by things like
women, liquor, or a family. When he indulges in those things, it is as a way of bonding
closer with his comrades, not as a means of release or a distraction. This man is able to
speak in front of a rally of thousands just as easily as he is able to speak to a small group
of intimates. This character, as described in the W+L book, often rescues women -
sexually unawakened.
Films: A Battle of Wits, Bodyguards and Assassins, City of Life and Death, Founding of a
Republic, Lust Caution, The Message, The Founding of a Party, Demon 2, Overheard 2,
Let the Bullets Fly, Kidnap, A Railway in the. Clouds, The Road Home, Flying Swords of
Dragon Gate, The Emperor's Shadow, 1911.
Young revolutionary/firebrand
This character can be male or female but is always young, often a student, an actor, or an
intellectual. He or she is motivated by strong passions of ideology and is always ready to
take direct action on issues that concern them, over the wise counseling of their superiors.
Often, they are portrayed as having some sort of downfall or ruin due to their
hotheadedness, where more careful planning and thinking could have given a better
solution. Unlike the seducer described in the W.+L book, this character accepts sex when
it comes but is more concerned with mission success; seducing young women is not his
priority.
Films: A Battle of Wits, Legend of the Black. Scorpion, Bodyguards and Assassins,
Founding of a Republic, The Message, The Founding of a Party, Platform, The Underdog
Knight, An Inaccurate Memoir, Flying Swords of Dragon Gate
..
Decadent, corrupt official
In many films there are portrayed corrupt government officials, at any level. The corrupt
official could be the Emperor, it could be a local leader, or it could be a military officer.
Their deceptions and corruption often impede protagonist characters, and it is a mix in
terms of whether their corruption is exposed and they are removed.
Films: A Battle of Wits, Legend of the Black Scorpion, Bodyguards and Assassins, City of
Life and Death, Curse of the Golden Flowers Founding of a Republic, Lust Caution, The
Message, The Postmodern Life of My Aunt, A Woman A Gun and a Noodle Shop, The
Founding of a Party, Demon 2, Overheard 2, Let the Bullets Fly, An Inaccurate Memoir,
Flying Swords of Dragon Gate, The Emperor's Shadow
Turncoat
Out of the main group of protagonists there will sometimes be a turncoat, a traitor who
sells the group to the other side (because there is always another side they are fighting,
showing the idea of Communist dialectic from the Hegelian tradition). The traitor is
motivated by selfish reasons and never totally buys into the ideology of the group he is
with, and his treason hurts the group but is prevented from doing severe damage because
of the strength of the bonds of the rest of the group members and the extent to which they
truly believe in their mission.
Films: A Battle of Wits, Bodyguards and Assassins, City of Life and Death, Curse of the
Golden Flower, Founding of a Republic, Lust Caution, The Message, Demon 2,
Overheard 2, Let the Bullets Fly, The Emperor's Shadow, Million Dollar Crocodile
Strong woman who meets her demise
Strong female characters are sometimes shown in these films, but they are often shown as
meeting some sort of demise due to their strength. It appears that by being headstrong,
women are more likely to have to, deal with negative consequences and it is implied that
such outspokenness is not a preferred quality among women. These characters are
sometimes vampish - they seek to seduce. men. In some cases they may appear as a good-
bad girl with a veneer of badness. Types like the bad girl or the home-digger are rarely
seen.
Films: A Battle of Wits, Legend of the Black Scorpion, City of Life and Death, Curse of
the Golden Flower, Founding of a Republic, Lust Caution, The Message, The Postmodern
Life of My Aunt, A Woman A Gun and A Noodle Shop, Let the Bullets Fly, The Flowers of
War.
Untrustworthy foreigner
Foreigners are never portrayed as trustworthy. They are often shown as being incredibly
disrespectful, uncaring about the plight of suffering Chinese, and interested purely in
their own ends. When Chinese characters approach them for help, they will at first agree,
but then later renege on their promises in a manner that makes them more powerful or
wealthy.
Films: City of Life and Death, Founding of a Republic, Lust Caution, A Big Deal.
Yuppie
In many films about modern China, young professionals take leading roles. They are all
shown in the same fashion - well-dressed, attractive, with budding careers, and with
money to spend. They are all also shown in turmoil about how their devotion to work
causes problems with their family and friends. They all come back to the fold, dedicating
themselves anew to their families and wives (or husbands), but it takes a great deal of
soul-searching and personal trauma. They are tech-savvy, using the Internet and text-
messaging to communicate, and they adopt western standards of beauty and habits. They
are the new generation of China, but films show them as much for their flaws as for their
promise.
Films: If You Are the One, If You Are the One 2; Break Up Club, A Big Deal, Love is Not
Blind, Qin Jia Guo Nian, Stand by Me, Great Wall My Love, A Place of One's Own, I Do,
Summer Love, Eternal Moment
Good employees
In several films, upstart employees try to prove themselves to their bosses, who trust in
them. They could also work for themselves and try to be making it big in the business or
retail world. With the exception of one character in a romantic film who serves as sort of
foil, all Chinese men and women are very good at their jobs, willing to speak out to their
bosses and clients, and willing to work hard to succeed, to the consternation of their
significant others and parents. Bosses take on the role of surrogate parents in the
workplace, serving as mentors, often where their own parents are absent. When bad
coworkers are encountered, good employees empower and inspire them to do better
work.
Films: The Postmodern Life of My Aunt, If You Are the One, If You Are the One 2, Ocean
Heaven, Break Up Club, A Big Deal, Overheard 2, Dear Enemy, Demon 2, Kidnap, The
Road Home, Eternal Moment.
Confused, lovesick young woman
Confused, lovesick young women populate Chinese romantic films, comedy or drama.
They are universally confused about not only what they want out of life, but also out of a
relationship. They have significant others who they cast aside sometime during the film,
only to return to them toward the end of the film. They spend films thinking about what
life would be like with others, or upset about small slights their boyfriends have
committed while wanting them back at the same time. They ultimately figure out what
the want through a great deal of independent thinking, counsel from family and friends,
and often by a large gesture from the ex-boyfriend who she then takes back. This woman
is not trying to seduce a man (like a vamp), but is looking for true love.
Films: If You Are the One, If You Are the One 2, Break Up Club, Love is Not Blind, Qin
Jia Guo Nian, Stand by Me, Dear Enemy, Great Wall My Love, Platform, A Place of
One's Own, Cell Phone, I Do, Eternal Moment.
Earnest, not so impressive young man
A new phenomenon in China has been the glut of young men, some educated, with few
career prospects and being content lounging around all day not accomplishing anything.
Or they work odd jobs (often janitorial) and quit at will, revolving through a series of
non-career positions to the dismay of their girlfriends. A couple characters in this vein are
depicted in the films in this sample, and they typically do not redeem themselves except
through hard work and giving in to the interests of their girlfriends and families. This
character might be compared to the standing-offer man from the W+L book - who is
always around to help but regularly loses the girl. He is close to the man-as-child as
described by W+L.
Films: A Big Deal, Love is Not Blind, Qin Jia Guo Nian, Stand by Me, A Place of One's
Own.
Woman who needs rescuing
In many films, women are shown as needing rescue. Whether this rescue is from
themselves or from others, women are often seemingly incapable of taking care of
themselves. In one particular example, a woman tries to commit suicide by jumping into
the ocean, only to be rescued by some fisherman and awaking to see her suitor in front of
her, who spends the rest of the film (and the sequel) trying to help her heal and to win her
heart. Of course, he ends up succeeding. She is purely a good girl - unlike the good-bad
girl in the W+L book. She actually, is an idealized representation of a Chinese woman.
Films: Curse of the Golden Flower, A Battle of Wits, The Postmodern Life of My Aunt,
The Legend of the Black Scorpion, Lust, Caution, If You Are the One, Red Cliff Part I, A
Woman A Gun and a Noodle Shop, If You are the One 2, A Big Deal, Love is Not Blind,
The Flowers of War, Stand by Me, Great Wall My Love, A Railway in the Cloud, Buddha
Mountain, Eat Drink Man Woman, A Beautiful Live, Love for Life.
Caring outsider
Caring outsiders often help main characters in need of aid. These outsiders may be a
neighbor, a distant relative, a coworker, or even a significant other, but there is a clear
sense of community in Chinese film. Main characters do not live by themselves in a
vacuum, they inhabit a world where they can call upon others for help, and they will
respond even when the government does not. They serve as deus ex machinas in the films
themselves - plot devices - but their presence may signal a broader existence in Chinese
society.
Films: The Postmodern Life of My Aunt, If You Are the One, City of Life and Death, If You
Are the One 2, Ocean Heaven, The Flowers of War, Love is Not Blind, Break Up Club,
Great Wall My Love, Cell Phone, Kidnap, A Simple Life
Plot devices
There are several common plot devices in older Chinese films that help filmmakers tell
their stories. These include:
Persecution
Characters in many of the older films are persecuted, typically by members of the "old
society," government officials, foreigners, or invaders. It is common to show heroes as
persecuted, by their enemies - they do not always come back and defeat them.
Films: Queen of Sports, Liu San Jie, Liu Hulan, The Goddess, This Life of Mine, The
Burning of Yuan Ming Yuan, The Horse ThiefinRed Sorghum, Farewell My Concubine,
Yellow Earth, The Red Detachment of Women, Street Angel, Serfs, The Red Detachment
of Women
Overt political statements
There are scenes where characters do not quite break the fourth wall, but deliver what
appear to be very direct political messages, in particular arguing for the destruction of the
old regime and the institution of the new society. These are sometimes jarring - they may
not always "flow" with the film, and it is obvious when the political message has been
shoehorned in.
Films: Queen of Sports, Liu San Jie, Liu Hulan, National Customs, Song at Midnight, The
Goddess, This Life of Mine, The Burning of Yuan Ming Yuan, Red Sorghum, Spring in a
Small Town, Farewell My Concubine, The Big Road, Song of the Fishermen, Yellow
Earth, Street Angel, The Red Detachment of Women, The East is Red, Serfs
Battling China's enemies
Given the political message in these films, there are repeated scenes and plot devices
revolving around fighting China's enemies, After initial defeat, the revolutionaries who
fight for China's new future are invariably\ successful. There will typically be a
triumphant scene where the revolutionaries win a major battle - or in another style, there
will be a major scene of sacrifice, followed by a follow up describing how the
revolutionaries went on to win ..:
Films: Liu San Jie, Liu Hulan, This Life of Mine, The Burning of Yuan Ming Yuan, Red
Sorghum, Farewell My Concubine, The Big Road, The Red Detachment of Women, The
East is Red
There are a number of common plot devices in modern films that help filmmakers tell
their stories:
Catching a significant other in the act
Some amount of voyeurism and watching from afar is evident in many romantic films.
Often, a boyfriend or girlfriend will watch from afar as their significant other cheats on
them or engages in behavior they do not like. Sometimes they stumble on this on their
own volition, other times they are led there by friends. There is sometimes an angry
confrontation after catching a significant other in the act, leading to the couple splitting
for a period before recognizing that both had made mistakes and returning to one another.
Imperial proclamation
One way that Chinese directors and writers of historical films move plots along is by
using imperial proclamations. In these scenes, the Emperor, or an equivalent male
authority figure, addresses a large body of his retainers, directing them toward some task.
Often there will be a retainer who objects to the order, resulting in his expulsion or
execution. The Emperor will stay calm throughout most of the session, but will
occasionally fly into a rage at the reticence of his retainers in carrying out his orders.
Scene of sacrifice
Sacrifice is a recurring theme in Chinese film, so it is no surprise that scenes of sacrifice
routinely show up in films across genres. It can happen in a tense moment where a
character must sacrifice himself (to the death) for success in battle (like in Red Cliff), or it
can happen over the course of a film where a character spends an entire lifetime
sacrificing for the good of someone else (like in Ocean Heaven). No matter the form, the
intent is always the same: by giving up one's own life or happiness, one can ensure the
happiness, success, or survival or another.
Activities done in "new China"
In the "new China," characters drink western wine, enjoy electronic media, use the
Internet, eat western food in restaurants, and travel abroad. In films set in the modern era,
nearly all characters do this to some extent, and these activities seem to have supplanted
more traditionally Chinese activities depicted in historical epics (reading, bettering
oneself, drinking rice wine, etc).
The problem is that these scenes are probably utterly unrelateable for most Chinese, even
young professionals. Ordinary Chinese cannot book flights across the ocean on a whim,
nor could they necessarily afford the kinds of meals or products these characters enjoy.
While these are supposed to portray "ordinary life" in a richer China, the reality is far
from what is depicted. What then, would be the draw of showing these scenes over and
over again in so many films?
The significant other making a serious mistake.
Somewhat related to the discussion of; watching a significant other with someone else,
lovers in Chinese romantic films often make egregious mistakes in the eyes of their
significant others. These cause the couple to split, only to be reunited later after
apologizing for the mistake.
Suicide
Suicide is sometimes depicted in Chinese film, with main characters attempting, but
never succeeding, at committing suicide. Their motives are for love, in the two cases in
this sample because they love someone too much. They are always rescued by someone
or something outside of their control, and they spend the remainder of the film healing
and vowing not to try that again. It is somewhat surprising that Chinese films are so blunt
about the topic of suicide, although the fact that none of the characters ever actually die
from it is probably indicative of just how accepting they actually are.
Finding self by going home
There is an obsession with returning to one's laojia, one's hometown in the Chinese
countryside, or with having the laojia come back to them. Reconnection with the place of
one's birth, the family still living there, and the memories had there help characters in
these films to find themselves and to overcome problems they have in life. They become
reinvigorated through their travel to their laojia and better understand who they are. This
plot device, of a character returning home, appears often.
Airport scene
Another curious phenomenon is the proliferation of scenes of people running to and from
airports, almost always chasing after a significant other who is about to depart. It is
typically the male chasing after the female in the relationship, breathlessly catching up
with her at the very last minute as she departs for a far-off destination that would take her
away from him for an extended period. She almost always agrees to stay with him, or at
least, to remember him while she is gone.
The drunk woman
There seems to be a common scene where a young woman, distraught over losing a
boyfriend or husband, will drink an incredibly quantity of alcohol in a plea for attention.
When in public she will find a man nearby to take care of her and to take her home - in
these films Chinese men are invariably honorable to the point of taking her home without
problems. When in private, she will call over a close male friend to commiserate about
the love. These are used as scenes to introduce the woman to an eligible man, who will
then start courting her.
Family dinner
Food is central to Chinese culture and social interaction, so it is no surprise that many
films include scenes of communal dining - family dinners or outings to restaurants are
very common. Even the act of purchasing food leads to human connections - as the
characters remark in Eat Drink, Man Woman "man, woman, food, sex" are basic facts of
humanity that they cannot escape. These scenes are used as settings for important
announcements or for drama to occur between participants.
Relationships
There are few recurring relationships showing up in the older films. This is not to say
there are not significant relationships - the mother's relationship with her child in The
Goddess is quite important, as is the young revolutionary's relationship with her mentor
in The Red Detachment of Women. These relationships are meaningful to the characters
and they matter for the messages the films try to tell. However, there do not seem to be
systematic relationships that appear across many films. The characters in these films have
relationships idiosyncratic to the film they are in.
This may seem strange, given that the early films do not exhibit thematic variation. Why
would there not be recurring relationships when there are recurring stories? While there is
thematic invariance, the stories are told in different ways. With the variety of means
filmmakers tell stories, we may not expect the same characters to show up.
Moreover, there is a far more explicitly political angle to many of the earlier films. These
films tell a story not about the relationships between the characters, but about how the
characters further the political message of the film. Relationships are arguably less
important; they serve a plot purpose, but in themselves may not have significance. One
might conceive of the main characters - especially the protagonists - having relationships
not with other characters, but with the broader cause with which they identify, or with the
Chinese nation generally.
There are some recurring relationships that show up in the modern films as well:
Father-son
The father-son relationship is actually rarely shown in this sample of films. Among films
set in the modern era, it is shown in Qin Jia Guo Nian, a film about two sets of in-laws
meeting during Chinese New Year. There, the relationship is akin to a emperor and
subject, with the father reminding the son of his duty to the family and his wife, and
taking charge during an awkward moment during the week. In historical films, the father-
son relationship is also shown as one of duty, with several examples of the Emperor's
sons obeying (or disobeying) him as duty commands.
Father-daughter
The father-daughter relationship is shown as primarily being one of protection. Fathers
are supposed to look out for their daughters even after they grow up and get married, with
the understanding that the daughters will continue to cherish and love them well into old
age. Surprisingly, fathers are also often absent in Chinese films, having died or been
divorced, leaving only the mother to raise the daughter. It is the father's responsibility to
approve of or to give advice on the choice of husband.
Husband-wife
Husbands and wives have varied relationships in Chinese film, but all relationships
revolve around the central question of the point of marriage. Why get married? Who to
marry? These questions are recurring issues that plague relationships in these films. There
is also the question of lost loves; in some films, husbands or wives go out to seek their
lost loves upon reaching old age, to wonder about what could have been. But these are
distractions or interludes, as the vast majority of the time husbands and wives are
:
:
characterized by devotion to one another over all else. The same cannot be said for
people in just regular relationships; it seems the label of marriage in film adds some
seriousness to relationships even though this seriousness may not always be practiced in
reality.
Mother-son
Mothers are protective toward sons, often thinking their son's wives or girlfriends are
unacceptable or not good enough for them. Mothers also are greatly concerned about
their sons' safety and future, willing to; sacrifice to ensure a happy future and always
worrying about their safety wherever theyare: In return, sons are devoted to their
mothers, and when they make mistakes that would make their mothers upset, they always
return to apologize and continue doing their duty. In one case, a son (a criminal) was so
concerned for his mother that he went to visit her every day in the nursing home when his
father (and her husband) died. He ultimately hatched a scheme that would enrich the
nursing home to provide his mother with a steady and happy future.
Mother-daughter
The mother-daughter relationship is also protective, but mothers are more willing to take
drastic action to ensure their daughters will have good lives to the tune of buying houses
or negotiating directly with boyfriends. They are overly concerned with their daughters'
relationships and want her to find the man who would provide her the best life possible.
In other respects, mothers' relationships with their daughters are similar to the mother-son
relationship referenced above.
Boss-worker
Bosses are often protective of those who work for them, mentoring and helping them out
whenever they can. The idea of the boss or manager asking workers to do too much or
being not nice in really any way is unheard-of, and workers repay their bosses' kindness
with work that is high in both quality and volume. There is some element of a parental
relationship with this, as bosses will step in as father (nearly always male) figures to help
take care of their workers when parents are not near.
Confucian family relationship commonly shown as flawed
There are also some relationship archetypes shown in these films. One of these is the
unhappy marriage - unhappy husbands and wives are shown fairly often. This is related
to the idea of the Confucian family structure in general, with the husband/father in charge
of a family unit and all others within it needing to obey his words. The Confucian type is
shown often in the historical films, with the father lording over his children, his wife, and
his other subordinates. This is shown as being a negative thing, and the father is
chastened or has to learn how to deal with a more reasonable structure that treats
members more as equals.
Boy-Girl
Apart from traditional family relationships, Chinese films often portray romantic
.......
relationships. I refer to these as "boy-girl relationships," because they are almost always
between an unmarried couple acting in immature ways. When married couples are
shown, they are somewhat troubled (ex: Cell Phone, with the husband's incessant lying
and cheating). Unmarried couples are often portrayed as having met long before the film
began. In established relationships, they are often challenged as the film goes on - by
events outside of their control, by new emotions, by new people. There is a strong
deterministic message, that people do not choose who their partner will be, it will be a
natural match or not. Couples may not seem like innate matches at first, and films often
trace their path of discovering that one way or the other.
Mentor-Student
The mentor-student relationship comes up in a few films. Students may not always be
students in the traditional sense. In Ocean Heaven, the relationship is between a father
and a son, where the father is trying to teach his son about being a good person. In
Together, there is a more traditional relationship between a violin student and teacher.
Students are expected to respect and obey their mentors at all times, but if their mentors
take advantage of them or lead them down a path that would violate any Chinese morals
(for example, neglecting one's family), they are fully justified in leaving and disobeying
them.
7. Characters and Themes fitting Leites
Characters
Older Films
Woman rising due to own abilities - The Red Detachment of Women
The entire premise of The Red Detachment of Women is that women can be just as
effective revolutionary fighters. The women in this film, particularly the protagonist,
have all been treated poorly by the Nationalists and the landlords they support. They
spend large amounts of time training, drilling, and planning for attacks against Nationalist
columns, in part drawing knowledge from male CCP members, but primarily working on
their own. They become an effective fighting unit based almost entirely on their own
innate abilities and determination.".
The heroine as mother - The Goddess"
The heroine is shown as a mother in The Goddess; similar to American movies, the father
is absent. While the son is not shown to grow up in this film, we see the heroine sacrifice
everything - even her freedom, as she is imprisoned for prostituting herself to support
him. The son's father is never mentioned in the film, nor is any other of the heroine's
family; she is entirely alone and her story revolves around her struggles to support him.
Suffering stars - Farewell My Concubine
!
In Farewell My Concubine, the plot revolves around two professional performers. Leites
describes the characters in their sample as having careers fraught with danger; this is
certainly the case with the two Beijing opera stars in Farewell, who suffer persecution at
the hands of the Japanese and the Communists. In Leites' world, the preference was for
those who look - rather than act. This leads to catastrophic results when they act, much
like the results we see in Farewell .:
Modern Films
There are also some examples of characters and themes fitting Leites' original archetypes
from his Movies volume, as well as from his paper On Violence in China. While not all of
the original examples fit, due to vast differences in culture and setting, there are still some
persistent characters that could reflect some underlying aspects of the human experience.
Woman rising due to own abilities - The Postmodern Life of My Aunt
A trope Leites described in his book was the example of a woman rising in stature or
ability due to her own abilities and not due to marriage or favor from someone else. The
independent woman does not need a man or benefactors to be successful at her career.
She pulls herself up by her bootstraps and uses her innate intelligence, cunning, and
dedication to make it in the world without the assistance of men. This is true in The
Postmodern Life of My Aunt, the main character is an older woman who lives alone in
Shanghai, who has never married and supports herself through teaching English to the
children of wealthy Shanghai residents. She came from poverty in northern China and
was able to be successful in the world by virtue of her education and abilities in working
with others around her. Repeatedly in the film, she demonstrates how well she is able to
succeed on her own, though the last half of the film deals with her ultimate downfall.
Man rising due to fluke - Bodyguards and Assassins
While women are portrayed as rising due to, their own innate abilities, Leites noted that
sometimes men are portrayed as having succeeded due to fluke or chance. In Chinese
cinema this is mostly shown with worthless sons being raised to power or status by virtue
of their fathers. In Bodyguards and Assassins, one of the main male characters is the son
of the wise leader figure, who happens to be very wealthy. At the beginning of the film,
he was accepted to Yale University, but it seems that he was accepted more due to his
father's influence, wealth, and ability to hire tutors rather than innate desire or ability.
Portrayed as a privileged son of wealth, he redeems himself at the end of the film by
sacrificing himself for the sake of a revolutionary cause.
Masculine girl - Bodyguards and Assassins
Leites noted that female characters were sometimes portrayed with masculine tendencies
or characteristics. This happens in Chinese film occasionally. In Bodyguards and
Assassins, the main female character was a tomboy, learning martial arts and unwilling to
become emotionally close to any other characters. In Chinese film the masculine girl is
not a love interest, but becomes like a stand-in for another male. She is a novelty, not a
central player.
Suicide due to frustration in love -Legend of the Black Scorpion
Suicide due to frustration in love was a theme in the films Leites watched. When one or
more major characters were continually frustrated by their relationships, they would kill
themselves to avoid dealing with the trauma. In this sample, it happens in Legend of the
Black Scorpion, which was based on Shakespeare's Hamlet. In this film, the sitting king
had just killed his brother to take both the kingdom and his brother's wife for himself.
Through a series of machinations, his wife, his son, and others in the court conspired to
kill him, but he had by that point truly fallen in love with his new wife. Upon discovering
the plot and his wife's desire to kill him, he became distraught and killed himself rather
than live with the knowledge that the woman he loved felt so negatively toward him.
Private eye - A Woman, A Gun, and A Noodle Shop
The private eye character is noted by Leites as one who is contracted to solve a mystery.
There are relatively few examples of this in Chinese film, but the closest one appears in A
Woman, A Gun, and A Noodle Shop. This film is set in the Chinese imperial era, and the
main antagonist, the owner of a noodle shop, hires a local police official to murder his
wife and her lover. In this course of this mission, the police official undertakes sort of a
private investigation of his own in an attempt to further enrich himself. He thinks about
the best way to steal money from the antagonist, discovering through investigation where
he keeps his money and creating a plan for taking it. Private eyes are a character
archetype that may have been popular in the 1940s, when Leites watched film noir films
from American cinema, but it does not appear they are too popular in modern Chinese
cinema.
On Violence in China
Leites' On Violence in China is a collection of quotes from various Chinese sources that
seeks to illustrate the Chinese mindset toward violence - in the four chapters completed,
Leites examines the phenomenon of taking oneself as a target, making oneself as a target
for others, pursuance, and yielding to an enemy (and enemies yielding to oneself). While
in modern Chinese films, overt violence exists primarily in war films (street violence or
interpersonal violence is rare) there are still a number of quotes that correlate with some
themes from the films - even working in the 1960s-1970s, there are some areas where
Leites was able to identify some (seemingly) enduring features of Chinese culture.
Early Films
There are several areas where Leites' themes from the piece are exhibited in older films.
Leites discusses Communist feelings on self-destructiveness, namely, he argues there are
four rules the Communists apply to damaging oneself:
"(1) that the individual should be willing to let the Party's (instead of the family's
or the ruler's) enemy inflict damage on him, as the price to be paid for his hurting
the enemy on the Party's behalf;
(2) that he should avoid any damage to himself not profitable to the Party-the
theme corresponding to the classical one stated above and, as I just noted, now
muted;
(3) that the (pen-)ultimate objective is not, as of old, avoidance of destruction, but
rather its accomplishment with regard to the enemy;
and (4) that the Party knows how to reduce the cost to itself of attaining this
objective." (Leites, 1970, 219):
There are many examples of these themes in film - as these are actually the central stories
behind the revolutionary films the Communist Party promoted about itself and its conduct
during the Sino-Japanese War and the Chinese Civil War. Certainly in films like The Red
Detachment of Women, The East is Red, Liu Hulan, and other films set during the
Communist Era.
Violence need now always be violence. Violence can come not just from overt attacks on
others, but from deprivation and exploitation. Leites cites the example of landlords:
"The bloody facts are that the paradise of landlords, rich peasants and a fortune
without murdering the poor people." This is the catchword for making a fortune
among the landlords, rich peasants and capitalists" (Leites, 1970, 223)
This is, of course, a major theme in films like Liu Sanjie, which attribute much of the
Chinese people's dissatisfaction with their state in lives to the exploitation and rapacity of
the landlords, the upper classes, and the traitors who support them. Toiling for another
without reaping the benefits of that labor is akin to having violence inflected on oneself.
