A theme Leites cites - namely, the Chinese fear of a dangerous and destructive world - is
prevalent in film. Leites suggests "[i]n traditional China this feeling was strong: almost
anything I do may provoke somebody's rage and induce him to strike at me" (Leites,
1970, 221), and "[w]hen another harms me, I might with notable ease not consider the
possibility that he is doing it without awareness or with indifference: it is with
intent" (Leites, 1970, 222). In films, characters'are generally concerned about the world -
they are concerned for the number of enemies that appear, seemingly out of nowhere, to
challenge the broad masses of the Chinese people. These enemies, like the landlords, hate
the Chinese - "" 'Hatred' and 'revilement represented the common language of all the
landlords, rich peasants, counterrevolutionaries; undesirable characters, and rightists ... in
China at that time," recalls People's Daily about 1962 in the summer of 1966. "They
really harbored 'deep hatred' towards the Communist Party ... gnashed their teeth and
poured out abuses." (Leites, 1970, 223): This is a basic fact: that China's enemies are
numerous, the world exists in a dangerous condition, Chinese enemies are opposed to the
very existence of the Chinese people, and that the only way to defeat the enemy is to
inflict violence.
:
Is there ambivalence about the destruction of one's enemy? How far should the fight
against the enemy be taken? In early Chinese film, there is a strong emphasis in the utter
destruction of the enemy - again and again film characters are seen chanting about the
destruction of the old society, and that the only way for the new society to be built - a
new society built on equality and freedom Y'is for the old to be utterly smashed. We see
this not just in conflict against landlords; but also against the Japanese and the
Nationalists. In the Communists' view - at least in film - the dialectic was established
such that failure to utterly destroy would lead to the enemy's reemergence.
Leites suggests there is ambivalence about the Communist desire to destroy the enemy
utterly. Even Mao tended towards "the classical Chinese belief: sometimes he implicitly
denies the desirability, and even the feasibility, of annihilating the enemy" (Leites, 1970,
228). The films do not follow this line; they follow the argument that "[t]he existence of
some badness fosters the very combat of the good against it" (Leites, 1970, 228). To
combat evil is to destroy it - there is no need, in the view of the film characters, to hold
back.
Leites also discusses how enemies may be hidden - of course, tying back in to the idea of
deception as an underlying current in Chinese society. He suggests, "enemies, usually
protected by false appearances, should be given time and facilities to reveal their 'true
form" (Leites, 1970, 237). This is not the case in the films. In the films - particularly any
films related to struggle against enemies the enemies are clear. The enemies are the
landlords and oppressive classes or the foreign invaders rampaging around China; there is
no need to let the enemies show themselves because they are so obviously present.
Moreover, Leites cites evidence that traditional Chinese do not pursue to the extreme.
Rather, when they respond to enemy attacks, their tit "should not exceed a fair proportion
to the enemy's "tat" -for reasons which are not given; as if the fear of irritating the
enemy were making itself felt. "After repulsing one . . . attack," Mao requires in the
situation just evoked, "we should know when to stop. . . . " (Leites, 1970, 239). Liberation
fighters in Chinese films only stop when they cannot proceed any further - they stop
because circumstances require it (not enough force), but they always want to continue to
attain the complete destruction of the enemy. There is no desire for there to be balance -
the presence of the dialectic means balance between the opposing sides would go against
nature; the natural, correct state is for the Chinese side to prevail. Any kind of tit for tat or
proportional response would prevent nature from rebalancing itself and go against the
natural order.
While there does appear to be some alignment with the Leitesian themes - there seems to
be difference as well. Namely, a number of themes of violence Leites cites - for example,
not pursuing one's enemy to total destruction - seem to be supported by the evidence
Leites cites and by contemporaneous quotes, by Mao and other party members. Why then,
would we not see those similar themes in the films - particularly films from this era,
which are supposed to be representations of Party messages to be sent to the people?
There might be multiple reasons. First, there could be an error in interpretation at three
levels: errors in interpreting the older films, Leites committing an error in making
:
1
:
statements about Chinese society from a limited data sample, or an error in the author
interpreting Leites.
Conditional on correct interpretations (on all sides), what might be other reasons for the
divergence? It is possible that Party members have private or public thoughts - as
expressed through their speeches or interviews. It is also possible that they hold different
views that they wish the people (on the whole) to believe. That is, while Party leaders
may privately believe (and be influenced by traditional literature in believing) that China
should not overreach with its enemies. At the same time, it may be convenient for them
(and the Party generally) for the people to believe the opposite - that the common people
need to unite and ensure that China's enemies are unilaterally destroyed.
Modern Films
There are also themes that are exhibited in the modern films.
Leites' On Violence in China is a collection of quotes from various Chinese sources that
seeks to illustrate the Chinese mindset toward violence - in the four chapters completed,
Leites examines the phenomenon of taking oneself as a target, making oneself as a target
for others, pursuance, and yielding to an enemy (and enemies yielding to oneself). While
in modern Chinese films, overt violence exists primarily in war films (street violence or
interpersonal violence is rare) there are still a number of quotes that correlate with some
themes from the films - even working in the 1960s-1970s, there are some areas where
Leites was able to identify some (seemingly) enduring features of Chinese culture.
Leites emphasizes violence against oneself: a" prevailing theme in many of the quotes
from his sources as well as in modern fine
Im. Leites describes a Chinese culture oriented
around rage and violence that does not exist in modern film. "Suddenly," in The Dream of
the Red Chamber, the central character (young Pao-yu), possessed, "leaped three or four
feet into the air and began to jabber words that no one could understand .... He dashed
about wildly ... apparently bent on self-destruction ...
Soon the news spread throughout
both mansions .... " (Leites, 1970,4). This would be utterly unheard of in modern Chinese
film - while rage does occur (for example in Go LaLa Go!), fighting is somewhat
symbolic and does not yield a resolution - one resolves (interpersonal) conflicts through
talk, not through violence. Fighting as described by Leites here yields desired outcomes -
or at least some outcomes. Leites focuses on destruction and rage in many of the quotes,
but their applicability to modern Chinese film is dubious in many cases.
1
But violence as manifested in self-harm is a common feature in Chinese film (ex: If You
Are the One 2). Leites describes self-mutilation: "Before Wang could interfere," imagines
a T'ang writer, "Ku raised his sword and cut off his own head" (Leites, 1970, 5). In
Chinese film, self-harm is less physical - but comes from substances, emotions, mental
health, and trust. Self-harm does not result in physical death but emotional. And as Leites
describes self-punishment - for example: "Strike the wretch for lying!" she commanded.
Wang-erh, one of her favorite servants, came up to carry out the command, but Phoenix
stopped him, saying, 'Let him'strike himself. "Your turn will come next." (op. cit., pp.
422-423)" (Leites, 1970, 7) - this is less prevalent in modern Chinese film.
Marriage is a concern. Leites reports that a farmer's son having, in the 1920's, refused the
wife selected for him, "his father told him that unless he married, he would kill him, or
kill himself" (Agnes Smedley, Chinese destinies. New York, 1933, p. 6)" (Leites, 1970,
2). That is to say, marriage is a concern both in the sense that parents continue (to this
day) to want to have their children married, while children sometimes push back - or
acquiesce under duress. But in modern Chinese film, there is more passion for marriage
than duress - unlike this quote (see any romantic film produced in the last decade). Of
course, with respect to cheating in a relationship, the feelings expressed in the following
quote still exist in film: "Phoenix ... threw herself at Chia Lien, crying that he might as
well kill her then and there, since he wanted to get rid of her," (Whereupon, unusually,
"Chia Lien ... seized a sword from. the wall, and said he would gladly oblige if she
insisted.")" (Leites, 1970, 9).
Family is also important, to the point where Leites finds that brothers are unwilling to
report on each other: "The elder brother," discloses another such story, "knowing that he
was in the wrong and could not report the case (against his younger brother, never mind
which-NL), was very angry." Then, though "Chuan saw that his brother was very
angry" and - probably sensing the danger not to the target, but to the bearer of that
feeling - "kowtowed and apologized," "the brother hanged himself" (T'ung-tsu Ch'u:
Law and Society in Traditional China, Paris, 1965, p. 59)" (Leites, 1970, 2-3). Here, the
sense of duty to one's family is similar but still different. Chinese films actually rarely
how siblings (Echoes of a Rainbow is a notable exception); but when families are shown,
they have a sense of duty and obligation that help to keep them together - but they are
rarely so upset about it as in this case.
Leites' comments on political aspects tend to be true. Although modern Chinese film is
oriented heavily around the theme of sacrifice (ex: Sacrifice), Leites notes that at least in
1970, he identified the Communists as trying to reducing the cost of attaining objectives:
""Local ... guerrillas," reports Agnes Smedley about the war against the Japanese, "who
had formerly fought under the slogan of 'Kill Japanese, then die,' were taught by the New
Fourth Array training camp how to 'kill Japanese and live" (Battle Hymn of China, New
York, 1943, p. 261)" (Leites, 1970, 13). He-finds Communists as "chary" in mentioning
the penchant to do self damage.
However, with respect to overall mindset toward the world, the Communists seem to
have the same general mindset as modern Chinese films. In film, characters view the
world as messy and dangerous, that not only will fellow citizens/people seek to do harm,
but that this hostility will be reflected in the broader international geopolitical system.
This led, Leites argues, to conservatism: "don't do anything to cause men to frown at you,
and there will not be a man to grind his teeth at you" (a proverb quoted in Scarborough's
collection, op. cit., p. 315)" (Leites, 1970, 16). The feeling at the time seemed to be
conservative and wary of provoking others. "if you want your dinner, don't offend the
cook"(loc. cit., p. 93)" (Leites, 1970, 16). Also: "Only fools catch flies on a tiger's head,"
opines a character in The Scholars. "Much better to say nothing and offend nobody" (Wu
Ching-tzu, The Scholars, Peking, 1959, p. 116)" (Leites, 1970, 38).
The world, in this world, is murderous: "The power," says Ou-yang Hsiu (11th century)
in a famous passage about the season of autumn, "by which it lays waste and scatters far
and wide is the unexpended fury of the breath of heaven and earth. For autumn is the
minister of punishments .... Its purpose is .:. execution" (Anthology of Chinese literature,
compiled and edited by Cyril Birch and Donald Keene, New York, 1965, p. 373)" (Leites,
1970, 17).
With respect to interpersonal relationships; Leites found that even when people try to
help, it could have been construed as hurting: "Black Jade threw up all the medicine she
had just taken and much more besides ... coughed until it seemed that her lungs would
burst," her devoted maid "Purple Cuckoo hurried to her and began to pound her back to
relieve her"; but Black Jade pushed her away saying, ' ... Go get a rope and strangle me. It
will suit your purpose better" (The Dream of the Red Chamber, pp. 351-352)" (Leites,
1970, 18). This shows up in Lost on Travel and Lost in Thailand - where well-meaning
attempts by a character to provide help as construed as materially dangerous by another.
But because of the hateful world, Leites finds that when Chinese think about their
enemies, they show only hatred. "With hatred carved in his bones ... ," a "rebel"
publication says in standard fashion about Peking's deposed boss (P'eng Chen) (Current
Background, No. 874, p. 16); "the faces and eyes of these ... bandits were burning
with ... hate against the revolutionary rebels," one such group may say about its rivals (loc.
cit., No. 861, p. 25)" (Leites, 1970, 19). This is consistent with how the enemy - typically
the Japanese - are portrayed in Chinese film, with the oppressed Chinese truly showing
them hate (Shanghai, The Flowers of War); the enemy is portrayed as inhuman: "when
you want to attack someone, is to give him a label or 'nickname.' This is the method
used ... since the Ming and Ch'ing dynasties. If for instance you want to accuse Chang or
Li, they will seem too normal if you just use their names; but if you call them 'six-armed
demon Chang' and 'Li the white-browed tiger" the magistrate need not examine the facts
to be convinced they are scoundrels" (Leites, 1970, 25). Or: "She was not a human being;
she was a wicked infamous beast! Why let such a creature survive?" (Wen Kangs Die
schwarze Reiterin, Zurich, 1954, p. 250)" (Leites, 1970, 28). Leites, however, notes that
the Communists "deny, in opposition to the Confucians, that a "human nature" exists:
only class natures do" (Leites, 1970, 25).
Leites expends considerable attention on pursuit - the question of whether, upon
defeating or routing an enemy, to pursue him and to annihilate him. His evidence is
mixed; certainly, there is a strain of quotes arguing not to destroy: "the altars of the fallen
dynasty were not destroyed, but merely walled in ... [for] one foresaw that the type of
civilization
whose memory ... and " seed they preserved, would return"
(pp.
99-100)" (Leites, 1970, 30). Leites notes that even Mao implicitly denies that destroying
the enemy is desirable. The goal is struggle is not the same as the goal of destruction. The
dialectic requires the opposition to exist in order to be strengthened: "That erroneous
things ... exist," explains Mao, "will help people learn to struggle against them
better" (March 12, 1957, Speech at the Chinese Communist Party's National Conference
on Propaganda Work, Peking, 1966, p. 26)" (Leites, 1970, 31). This is in line with the
thinking on film; attackers seek to co-opt their enemies rather than to utterly destroy them
- for example in Red Cliff, the opposing forces look for opportunities to encourage
defection that might serve to strengthen. During the Japanese occupation and ensuing
civil war, while sentiments toward the Japanese were high - there was never talk of
pursuing the Japanese back to the home islands, only to get them out of China. Mercy/
chivalry, as in film, were major features: "some comrades consider it too much to trample
on enemies who have already been knocked down .... They show mercy to
them" (Chekiang Radio, July 20, 1967¿ Union Research Service, August 25, 1967, p.
219)" (Leites, 1970, 33).
However, Leites also presents evidence to show that Maoists are in favor of annihilation
- because of fear. In response the Duke said:"In one morning I exterminated three of the
six nobles. I cannot bear exterminating all of them.' 'Your Highness cannot bear
exterminating them, but they will bear causing Your Highness harm,' said Ch'ang Yu-
chiao. To this the Duke would not listen. In the course of three months, the remaining
nobles started a rebellion and ... killed Duke Li ... " (Chapter 31, op. cit., Vol. 2, pp.
6-7)" (Leites, 1970, 44). If one does not annihilate enemies, they will come back to cause
harm - this theme is not apparent in the films.
Finally, Leites describes the prospect of yielding. Mao, in describing his upbringing,
learned: "I learned that when I defended my rights by open rebellion, my father relented,
but when I remained meek and submissive, he only cursed and beat me the more" (loc.
cit., p. 126)" (Leites, 1970, 50). Yielding denies one of the rights he might otherwise
have; in China film, heroes rarely ever surrender. In Hero, rather than surrendering, the
main character chooses death after rebellion. But when Leites goes on to describe the tit-
for-tat strategy the taken by the Communists this is not seen in the films - in films
characters are always on the aggression (in romance, in war, in deception), tit-for-tat like
the following is unheard of: "Since the pro-British and pro-American big landlords and
big bourgeoisie," he had noted years before, "are still using the stick and carrot in dealing
with our Party, the policy of our, Party is to 'do unto them as they do unto us,' stick for
stick and carrot for carrot" (Leites, 1970, 51).
8. Relation to Chinese Society
Does any of this relate to the earlier and current state of Chinese society? Where are there
possible avenues for future research? There are a few broad areas where analysis films
could broaden understanding of Chinese society as a whole, including social changes,
social mood, and core values.
Early Films
How do the themes in earlier films relate to: Chinese society? There are potentially
different ways of thinking about this. First, one could think about the themes in relation
to the society that produced themo relationships with the Chinese society of the
1930s-1980s. Second, one could think about whether there are any enduring themes that
would suggest consistency over time.».
What is the importance of the older movie themes as they relate to our understanding of
modern China? Older films have themes that may differ widely from modern films, and it
may not be clear how they relate to understanding of modern society. And yet, these films
still shed light on our questions - how has China adapted in the 20th
century, and what
might be the direction of China's rise in the 21st.
Older films reflect the spirit of their period. Examining the themes, characters, and plots
from these films helps modern analysts think about the social, political, and economic
conditions that shaped the founding of the People's Republic. Examining themes over
time helps us to understand whether there was change - whether Chinese viewers were
still grappling with the same issues as the PRC matured. And they help us understand the
psychological mindset of Chinese citizens during the 20th
century, some parts of which
may still persist today. We may expect some of these themes to be enacted throughout the
20th century, resolved (or not), and some may still persist as issues lingering in minds
today. Although certainly some facets will change, some of the core psychological
features that we see in early film themes remain in both modern films and in modern
Chinese society.
By illuminating the past and by showing the beliefs, trials, worries, and victories of the
men and women in early 200
century China, we will be able to better reflect on the same
topics in modern China. Although society is dynamic, people are fundamentally the same
- they have the same motivations, fears, and interactions, and we may aggregate these to
the level of society by examining these themes.
What interested older Chinese filmmakers, and what does that mean for modern China?
Older films were often concerned with social trequality, revolution, colonialism,
authority, sacrifice and duty, social change, the influence of capitalism, and loyalty.
-
Social Inequality
China was in turmoil in the early 20
th"
: century, with leftist political parties railing against
the influence of landowners and the last vestiges of the imperial government in
suppressing the landless and poor. This was reflected in film, with many films examining
the plight of the poor and their attempts to fight back against landlords and other
oppressive figures.
This climate deeply influenced political parties during the period, with a variety of leftist
parties vying for control over the country in their attempts to right these wrongs
according to their own political ideology. And this great desire to increase the quality of
life of the masses was a key plank of the Communist Party platform, which has persisted
to the present. Even though the CCP has embraced capitalist ideals, it is still bound to
improving quality of life for the broad masses of China's population, and this need has
become more urgent with China's swelling population. In the social and economic
1
climate portrayed in these early films, we can see some of the beginnings of why the
Communist Party behaves the way it does with respect to social welfare programs,
protectionist economic policies, and maintaining its purported ideology while still
embracing capitalism.
Revolution
Revolution was seen as a means for change. Indeed, it was seen as one of the best means
for change, a way to wipe the slate clean and to take total power for the masses. Of
course, the Chinese Civil War enacted the revolution that films called for, and this
revolutionary fervor was repeated throughout the 20th
century (ex: Cultural Revolution).
What these films show is that revolution is not unique to the Communist Party. Although
the Communists were the most successful practitioners of revolution, many other parties
believed this to be the best course of action (including the Nationalists). We should
expect this ideology - the ideology of revolution as a means for change - to remain in
Chinese society to this day, and when we view the martial language that some business
leaders use when approaching internationalization, we can see some influence of
revolutionary thought.
Colonialism
Colonialism and foreign influence acting to the detriment of the interests of the Chinese
people is a key aspect of early films. Foreigners acting in a colonialist fashion colluding
with corrupt Chinese was blamed as a reason for China's poverty and inequality in the
early 20th century.
This idea, like revolution, was formative in the minds of early 20th
century Chinese
political and social leaders. It engendered a deep distrust of outside influence that
persisted throughout the 20
th
century and to today - collaboration with foreigners was
seen as an invitation for collusion, corruption, and the degradation of the Chinese people.
Although modern China now has far more interaction with foreigners, it is important to
note that they are often monitored - foreign businesses entering the Chinese market must
establish a joint-venture for the operation to be legal. Given the persistence of this
thought in the Chinese psyche, it is unlikely that this prejudice will disappear in the 21st
century as China opens up.
Authority
Strong authority was viewed as extremely important for China's stability. China is such a
large country with so many people, and with unruly, violent people, that it needed a
strong and firm hand to guide it. Leftists were not so much concerned with authority
itself, but the type of authority that ruled - they would have preferred a people's
government ruling with a firm hand rather than an imperial government with foreign
influences.
And this has persisted. The turmoil of the 20th
century was, in the view of filmmakers
:
and viewers, dealt with only by a firm party, hand guiding government. The modern
government has taken many steps to increase its authority over the populace, which has
met with some moderate resistance from local citizens. But this resistance is often geared
toward corruption on the part of local officials, which we observe in early film, not the
very idea of strong authority itself. We may see history repeat itself with revolutionary
upheaval leading to a strong national government.
Sacrifice and Duty
Sacrifice is a key idea, and sacrifice as one's duty to a greater cause is a noble idea. The
Chinese are expected to uphold their duty first to family, second to community (both local
and writ large). In accomplishing community acts, they are expected at times to sacrifice
themselves for the good of the collective. Although they are portrayed (always) as doing
so in the films, it is not entirely clear how much this will extent to actual interactions.
Social Change
China in the 20
th
century was a time of incredible social change. China went through
multiple revolutions and experienced a transformation from a feudal empire to a modern
capitalist economy. This brought with it a great deal of social change, and the expectation
of change was a main theme of films in the early part of the 20" century. Chinese films
examined how characters reacted to societal changes, generally taking them in stride but
modifying them to fit Chinese societal characteristics. We see this in modern day film;
Chinese characters carouse in the traditional Chinese way, but red wine has begun to
replace baijiu in dinner parties.
Signs for the future point to Chinese citizens reacting to changes, not entirely assimilating
them, but pragmatically choosing which aspects of societal changes they accept in
bettering China.
Capitalism
Chinese film is ambivalent about capitalism, generally, but the far more negative opinions
about the detrimental effects of capitalism show up in early film. Related to the themes
about landowners and social inequality, Chinese films pointed to capitalism, and
associated greed, as a primary cause of inequality and oppression in early China.
Ordinary Chinese would be seduced by the allure of money or power coming from
capitalist powers and betray their countrymen, violating the fundamental tenets of loyalty
and sacrifice Chinese citizens were expected to uphold.
In the present day, this suspicion toward capitalism persists. Capitalism is practiced with
"Chinese characteristics," with the party not willing to fully embrace capitalist ideals,
attempting instead to remain cautious about China's opening so as to avoid the negative
effects portrayed in early film.
Loyalty
Loyalty is an important factor in decision-making in early Chinese film. Individuals are
expected to be loyal to the collectives to which they are a part - particularly their family,
work groups (danwei), and political organizations. These are key relationships to which
individuals are bound. Loyalty to motherland and to the idea of unified China is
paramount among these collectives.
-
Modern Films
Social Changes
Some of the themes and characters above point to changes in Chinese society that are
worth considering. Chinese society continues to undergo rapid change, partly due to
globalization. This includes exposure, both economic and personal, to western society.
Scholars debate the extent of these changes and implications such as US-China relations
and the extent to which China is a rising global or partial power (Friedberg, 2011;
Schamburg, 2013).
One can also see manifestation of social changes in Chinese films. Chinese citizens, on
the basis of these films, appear to be taking on some of the habits of the west, but they do
not seem to be accepting core western political values of individual liberty or democracy.
Rather, they accept superficial, quality-of-life improvements that reflect China's new
economic standing. It would be interesting to consider, through survey data or other
sources, the extent to which the reality portrayed in film is the reality for most middle-
class Chinese citizens. What elements of western culture do they actually embrace, and
what elements of Chinese culture do they wish to keep? A changing national culture over
time could lead to changes in decision-making patterns, making this an interesting
exercise from a strategic and business perspective.
Another element of social change is in, acceptance of things that Chinese society may not
have traditionally discussed. Things treated in these films, like suicide, mental handicap,
depression, and homosexuality, could, point to growing social and governmental
acceptance and recognition. This could mean that these issues now have a place in
Chinese society, and that film could be used as a medium to discuss these issues where
other forums are not suitable. Presence of such issues in film could point to their growing
presence in society, although that may be somewhat of a stretch.
Considering social changes could also help. chart the direction of Chinese society in the
future. The areas of change, their scale, and their speed are things of note that could be
the seeds for social revolution in China in the near future. Considering these elements
from film, and then considering government reactions to change, could help to identify
the most likely areas where change will endure.
Social Mood
Following the Leites perspective, one, can also think of films as reflecting a kind of
'social mood' in a culture. Are the people of a country relatively happy with their lives or
fairly upset with their lives? How does this relate to how they perceive their government?
This could be done by identifying areas of film's where characters experience discontent,
whether set in historical or modern times, and trying to relate those sources of discontent
to the modern Chinese experience. Cross-checking with Chinese social media and news
outlets could verify this discontent, however much it is expressed.
In this way, film serves as another way to keep a finger on the pulse of Chinese society.
Understanding what ordinary Chinese citizens think about could be very interesting,
particularly with regard to perceptions of the west.
Core Values
One can also identify certain Chinese core values from films.
The values characters
espouse are those the Chinese government could have identified as being important for
the Chinese people to view. Identifying the values of a society and culture can be
challenging; sources of culture like film, radio, television, and literature are all ways to
see what things those in a culture care about. Because no films are released that the
government has not endorsed, the implication is that the government at the very least
tacitly approves of the values in question.
With respect to the films that the government has sponsored (or has heavily promoted) -
these include Founding of a Republic, Founding of a Party, and Confucius - these films
have some common themes and values. While these values exist in plenty of other films,
they are particularly evident here - they include: sacrifice, loyalty to the state, humility,
love for family, desire for order, close connection to the common people, and love for
China. Absent are any problems innate to China - any problems in these films come
externally. When sadness and distress comes in these films, it comes from external
sources (foreign interlopers), or from distress over inability to properly serve the state and
party. Corruption is not a concern in these films, nor is romance - the characters eschew
any romance for the sake of duty.
A host of questions arises from this sort of analysis - what do the Chinese believe to be
moral? When do they believe that it is right to, step forward or when it is right to back
down? How do they believe confrontations arise,, and how do they end? What do they
think about the natural order of the world and how states and people interact? These are
all core values of Chinese society that; would, be worth knowing for anyone studying
China seriously, and while spending time in China and talking with the Chinese may be a
good way of doing this, viewing films would be a way of doing so with the CCP's stamp
of approval. If the CCP wishes to shape.Chinese society in a way that benefits the regime
and its future, it would be good to understand the core values it wants the Chinese people
to have.
Strategic Mindset
These data demonstrate some changes in Chinese society as a whole, but what about in
terms of decision-making style or operational capabilities? Is it indicative of changes in
Chinese thinking at the government level? Where could one identify areas where lessons
from these films could speak to not just society?
There are several areas where there
could be potential linkages.
There are two major reasons why it may be possible to understand some implications
about government from movies. First, films are consumed not just by the Chinese public,
but by government employees as well. There is no reason to believe they do not affect
them in the same way. Second, films ostensibly promote a government message, which
members of government help craft. The government as a whole should support the
messages they send and some elements of operations should follow them.
There is a class of films that are heavily promoted by the government. These films
are subsidized by the government, they enjoy fast-tracked review, and the government
might even subsidize tickets for Chinese citizens. The three best examples in this sample
are Confucius, The Founding of a Republic, and The Founding of a Party. These films do
not promote uniquely different values from the rest of Chinese film - the message is in
line with what else exists, but there is certainly more emphasis on certain aspects.
Specifically, these films highlight sacrifice, duty, authority, the primacy of China, and
stability as the biggest themes. The message sent is that China suffers from both internal
and external threats, and that all Chinese, are duty-bound to sacrifice what they can to
support stability through the authority of the Communist Party.
Government as family
Portrayal of the Chinese government in relation to society is paternalistic (or
maternalistic). In particular, the Chinese military is almost always shown as taking great
care of the people and making sacrifices to ensure the people's safety and comfort. For
example, in A Railway in the Clouds, a PLA engineering unit undergoes great hardship in
building a railway to Tibet. Soldiers suffer, die, but ultimately prevail in their desire to
serve the people. Other government entities are also shown as being effective at their
jobs, but also caring about the people as well.
A more cynical perspective would say this is pure propaganda, that the ideology
expressed is only for the benefit of the average Chinese and not indicative of the true
feelings of the Chinese military or government. But the government draws much of its
legitimacy from its ability to provide for the people, and it stands to reason there could be
significant elements within the military and government who believe genuinely that their
role is to safeguard the well-being of the people at all costs.
Propaganda is effective if the people believe it "- if the Chinese people buy into this
concept of a paternalistic government, government as family, there could be significant
loyalty to the government under most circumstances. In film, families are portrayed as
unbreakable, with bonds lasting even after death. Civilian loyalty to the government as a
whole would then be high, and civilians would be willing to overlook some government
transgressions in favor of the bigger picture ..
General antipathy toward foreigners (or ambivalence)
Another area where there could be a linkage is in general government feelings toward
foreigners. Both as individual government workers and as a collective whole, it is
possible the government harbors distrust of foreigners - this is the message they repeat in
film. There is general antipathy toward foreigners or the idea of Chinese moving abroad
or interacting with them.
Doctrine
Could Chinese military doctrine change as its society changes? The PLA has relied on the
people's war concept in the past, and edited volumes have considered how China could
think about the future security. environment: According to Pillsbury, "China's military
authors have called the future multipolar world :"amazingly" similar to the Warring States
era and declare that China's future security environment resembles the Warring States era
in several ways" (Pillsbury, 2000, xxxvi). Chinese strategists seem to draw from Sun Tzu
and his contemporaries when thinking about security issues. However, Pillsbury also
reports that the people's war concept has begun to be supplanted by the "Local War"
school of thought, which argues that China's next war will be limited, will not be a war of
survival requiring the people, and will be quick (Pillsbury, 2000, 262). And there is also
an RMA school of thought that sees war scenarios with Japan or the United States -
networked warfare in high-tech environments (Pillsbury, 2000, 265). As films have
described a changing China, some of this (the networked aspects) might be expressed in
the concept of people's war.
Chinese films depict a network, cosmopolitan society that has adopted many western
habits and thinking - how does this relate to the military? If young people entering the
military bring with them this new way of thinking, perhaps the military itself will adapt to
take advantage of all the benefits (and weaknesses) that creates. This would imply a
networked military, quick to adopt new technology, but prone to overcommunication and
deception within the ranks. It also implies a military that is undergoing significant flux
about the role of duty in relation to family -" to whom does the military answer, the
people or the Party? Ordinary Chinese ask themselves this in relation to their professional
and family lives, perhaps similar grappling goes on in the military. So far, it seems loyalty
to the family wins out.
However, one of the biggest themes in Chinese film is also the relative lack of trust
among members of Chinese society - characters in films cannot bring themselves to trust
each other, and there are numerous examples of scammers stealing money, property, or
just seeking to profit from someone else's misfortune. Ordinary Chinese citizens are often
depicted as rapacious (when it comes to money) - and social cohesion is not a big theme
in film. That is to say, in Chinese film it is rare to see communities banding together to
fight off an enemy or to solve a problem; they wait for an authority to solve it for them.
This lack of trust extends to leadership feelings (in the films), particularly toward
foreigners. This could have several effects on military doctrine; an underlying feeling of
mistrust in a society - and leadership concurrence with that feeling - could result in a
country that views the outside world with distrust as well, potentially making China more
hostile. But yet, a lack of trust among Chinese - therefore extending to the level of the
military unit - may affect how Chinese commanders view their forces.
Resiliency
One of the biggest recurring themes in film is that China is a resilient country full of
resilient people; there are no challenges China cannot overcome, whether those be
internal, external, or natural. This has been instilled in the Chinese way of thinking -
even Mao thought it was possible for China to win a nuclear war simply because of
population.
This could mean the Chinese are less risk-averse than the west. In the US, it is national
news when a single US soldier dies in Afghanistan or Iraq. Would this be the same in
China? Would the Chinese be more willing to accept losses, whether those be financial or
human, in the name of progress? This could play a role in shaping Chinese doctrine and
make Chinese leaders (and the people). more aggressive.
Chain of Command
How could this relate to chain of command in Chinese government organizations? Chain
of command as portrayed in film, both in military and private realms, is very top-down.
There is little room for individual initiative; one follows the orders of the commanding
officer. This holds true in family life just as much as it does in professional life - Chinese
families follow the directives of the most senior,, the father, grandfather, or otherwise.
This could lead to Chinese organizations being prone to conservative thinking and
relative lack of innovation. An organization that is bottom-down to the point of not
entertaining courses outside of what the leader says has significant vulnerabilities. It is
personality-driven, does not have consistent decision-making patterns, and is slow to
come up with new ideas.
9. Closing
Study of a nation's films can help to shed light on underlying patterns of thought,
relationship types, and fears. Nation is used not to describe a political unit, but a social
unit - a nation can transcend state boundaries - the study here depicts some elements of
the Chinese nation as it exists both in the state of China and abroad. China's ex-patriate
community is tightly linked to the mainland, through both official ties and unofficial;
foreign Chinese remittances and visits to the mainland keep the groups close. Ex-patriate
Chinese watch films just like native Chinese, so viewing film could be helpful in better
understanding both groups.
Chinese film subjects have evolved in the century. From a cinema dominated by state-
controlled films about Communist propaganda, Chinese film has blossomed into dozens
of sub-genres, with a heavy prevalence of romantic comedy films and historical epics.
And yet some enduring themes remain in Chinese film - films emphasize sacrifice,
togetherness, and family. Emerging themes tackle some of the challenges facing Chinese
society - social issue, loneliness, care for the elderly or sick.
The approach taken in this report, with an emphasis on a psycho-cultural approach to film
like in the work of Leites and Wolfenstein, shows that a researcher might be able to glean
understanding of a broader nation from representation of visual culture - a finding
supported by the sociology, anthropology, and psychology literature.
While this study has identified some key themes, characters, relationships, and plots in
Chinese film, there is much room for further analysis. One area where work might be
useful is in reconciling the focus on community and the apparent lack of empathy in
modern China. The most famous example of the Chinese lack of empathy came earlier in
2011, when a 2-year old girl in Foshan was struck by two vans and subsequently ignored
by 18 passerby (caught on security tape). She was later taken to a hospital and passed
away from her injuries several days later. The reluctance of ordinary Chinese to help each
other, as well as the predilection for transactional, rather than emotional, relationships (cf.
guanxi practices) is hard to distinguish in most film.
If a lack of empathy really does exist, this would mean that Chinese films are still meant
to be educational - promoting values the government thinks the Chinese people need to
improve. If we accept characters in these films are Platonic ideals (in both positive and
negative senses), we can see what kind of "harmonious society" elements of the Party
might want.

11. Introduction to the Summaries
This appendix of Chinese film draws from the viewing of (so far) 121 films produced
between 1934 and 2012. These films were produced in mainland China, Hong Kong,
Taiwan, or a joint production between some mix of the three. China's film industry has
expanded since its economic opening, and co-productions are now the norm - in
2012-2013, a Chinese studio even collaborated with American studio Marvel in the
production of Iron Man 3. The mainland's' appetite for film has grown as well, although
the majority of ticket receipts still comes from foreign productions, typically American
action films. Regardless, the number of films in part produced by and released on the
mainland has grown to hundreds a year.
These summaries describe the films and highlight some interesting aspects of characters,
relationships, plots, or themes that might relate to the foundational work in the
psychology of film conducted by Martha Wolfenstein and Nathan Leites in their book
Movies: A Psychological Study.
Apart from descriptions of relations with the Wolfenstein and Leites work, the summaries
also describe some elements of the films that may point to uniquely Chinese cinema
characteristics that could speak to national culture or social norms. These elements are
guided by four broad societal generalizations and fall into four categories. For context,
we describe these to introduce the summaries.
> From The
There are three primary genres in these films. First is the historical film - these films look
into China's imperial or feudal period and focus on court intrigue and warfare. Second
are films about the Second World War and Chinese Civil War; these films describe the
Japanese occupation and the conflict with; the KMT. Finally, there are 'modern films' -
this broad category encompasses romantic films and dramas about modern Chinese life.
-
These films are interesting from the perspective of how they might reflect society.
Modern films, owing to the fact that they are set in every day life for many Chinese,
might serve as a 'mirror' on society - they reflect society for all its good and bad. Films
set in the historical period or during the war might show how society ought to be - or at
least how characters ought to behave. More' moralistic stories, they depict 'good'
characters struggling against 'bad,' with. implications for which one the filmmakers
prefer.
Societal Issues
These broad issues reach across all of the 'buckets' into which we might categorize some
of the trends we see in film. These issues serve as a backdrop for character interactions
and frameworks for plots - since they pervade almost every aspect of Chinese film they
are the context for the context.
Ambivalence about capitalism
The transition to full-blown capitalism in Chinese society has been treated with mixed
feelings. While characters are shown as reaping the benefits of capitalism - professional
jobs, experiencing western social rituals (red wine vs. rice wine at dinner), and general
increases in quality of life - they are just as often shown consumed by greed. Greed
causes families and friendships to break upę as characters see opportunities to personally
profit from economic freedom and forsake their past relationships or loyalties in doing so.
Harmonious society
Characters in Chinese films desire a' harmonious society - the term the Chinese
government uses to describe the kind of country it wants when censoring social media.
The harmonious society includes filial piety, respect for others, strong collective action,
adherence to moral standards, and loyalty to one's nation (nation in this sense being used
to describe one's national identity rather than de jure state affiliation). Chinese films vary
in their depictions of the level of harmony in society; historical films looking at China's
past (both imperial era and during the Japanese occupation) show little harmony in
society. There are many examples of the Chinese 'west' - a part of China either in the
past or present that is far from government control and therefore lawless; characters in
many films use the word "chaotic" to describe China in the absence of such a
government. To ensure harmony in a large country whose state of nature is chaos, China
(the implication goes) needs a strong government.
Deception
In many Chinese films, characters relate to each other through deception. This is most
common in films set during China's historical era that focus on court intrigue or grand
strategy as related to warfare. Characters enact incredibly intricate plans to gain the upper
hand over their adversaries and victory goes to the ones who are most clever. In films set
during the modern era, deception occurs within relationships - husbands have mistresses,
boys sneak out to visit girls, etc. In the film's deception is part of the fabric of Chinese
-
life.
What leads to victory?
What leads to victory? What can one call a victory? Chinese films often consider the
meaning of victory and how to achieve it; will deception tactics lead to victory? Or is it
better to confront an opponent directly? Can one 'win' in a relationship? Characters
desire victory; they reference that they want to win, but question how to do it.
Broad Themes
In the context of these societal issues, there are some themes that come out of the
summaries.
Romantic Relationships
A large number of Chinese films are concerned with romantic relationships. Within these
films, a number of topics stand out. Many films are concerned with falling in love -
finding love, falling in love, and sustaining love are primary plots points in many films
[11]. But the big emphasis is on how why one falls in love - what about another person is
attractive [28]? And does that attraction sustain over the long term [55]? Can that change
over time?
In Chinese films, characters marry only once. While they may get divorced (but not
onscreen) or become widowed, they do not remarry after losing someone [41]. They stay
alone. When they do try to find others [19], they are thwarted and end up worse off.
Husbands have hidden mistresses [12]. Relationships are shown as 'normal' - there are
no homosexual couples and no age mismatche with the exception of a homosexual couple
depicted in one film [1] set in New York City and produced by Taiwanese and US studios.
Many couples in films are not married, and there are many examples of dating - these
tend to follow western concepts of dating and include western rituals like dinners or red
wine [39]. Losing a boyfriend or a girlfriend in these films is as painful as losing a
spouse.
Women are dominant in relationships, despite the patriarchal nature of Chinese society.
Men are subservient to women; they are expected to take care of them, they do things for
them, and they visit them when they are ill, and they serve as providers. While women
themselves are not helpless - the strong woman is a character archetype - in some
respects they are dependent on men but still in control.
Finally, one of the biggest plot devices in films about relationships is an emphasis on real
estate [72]. This is an accurate reflection of the state of Chinese society, as when a couple
gets married, the husband (or his family) is expected to provide a home for the couple to
start a family. Many of these films have an emphasis on the couple trying to find a home,
or after acquiring a home, the problems they; have with it. This seems to be an
overwhelming concern for young Chinese couples" - and particularly men as in the event
. ..
they cannot afford to purchase one they are in an unfavorable position.
Parents/Family
Many films consider the relationships between families - particularly those between
parents and children. Family units - the nuclear family - are critical in this sample of
films [47]. Characters structure their lives around the formation and sustainment of these
units, and one's primary loyalty is to his or her family once it is formed. If one is not in a
family, their mission is to create one, first with a spouse and then with children [61].
Marriage is incredibly important.
This means that children tend to listen to their parents' guidance, and when they do not,
they get hurt or fail [44]. And failure is unacceptable in front of the family - for children,
parents accept no excuses for failure; they express not just disappointment, but anger. It is
primarily fathers who express anger - the patriarch of the family still holds authority in
both ancient and modern films [17, 58].
Extended family is also important in these relationships. That is to say, parents do not
lose their own parents upon having children. They still have their own parents giving
advice - visiting, counseling, nagging, they still disobey their parents at their own peril
[72]. Sometimes, children even take care of parents. Parents get offers to live with their
children, or if they stay at hospitals, their children visit them and provide help [93].
The primary interactions shown are between parents and children - there are few
interactions between siblings. There are sometimes depictions of single parents, typically
in the case of their partner dying [58]. In these cases, the surviving partner and children
do their best to honor the memory of the deceased. Children never have to deal with the
effects of mistresses - cheating happens among families with no children. When children
go abroad, their parents lament their loss and rejoice upon reunion [1].
This leads to insularity; families are self-contained units, and while there might be
interactions with the broader community, the family is the primary unit of organization
depicted in Chinese film. There are some depictions of work units or military units - but
these are portrayed as being less important than the family organization [9]
Motherland: Loyalty/Organizational Identification
Characters in Chinese films also display serious regard for loyalty. However, the question
is - loyalty to what? Characters display loyalty to country [10], morals [48], leaders [77],
individuals [46], family [43], company [53], and motherland [10]. Primary loyalty seems
to be to one's family, but overriding loyalty is always to one's motherland. China, as a
nation, holds everybody's loyalty no matter what, and if one's family is standing in the
way of China's prosperity they will be pushed aside [20].
Authority is important in protecting the motherland. Characters continually describe
China as being 'chaotic' without a strong authority to protect it - this is prevalent in films
in the historical era; characters desire a strong leader to unify China and impose order on
a state of nature that is violent and barbaric [69]. Characters in Chinese films seek a
'harmonious society' where all Chinese can prosper. Modern Chinese society may have
ills (as depicted in several films, including economic inequality and aimlessness, 24 and
63), but there are no modern protests. Authority figures are morally upstanding when they
are positive characters - the police, for example, are shown as professional and morally
upstanding [25].
When one rebels against authority, it is because the leaders are corrupt. The 'corrupt
leader' archetype is common; corrupt leaders extract rents from local populations while
providing nothing in return, abuse the people under their rule, and violate moral
principles - what those moral principles precisely are is never specified but generally
follows the principles of doing good for the people, following principles of 'justice,' and
remaining humble [48]. Rebellion fails when its cause is unjust and succeeds when it is
just and the leaders are corrupt [6].
Foreigners aid corrupt leaders in extracting resources and wealth from China - the
'corrupt foreigner' is another character type [64]. Shown as foreign, white military
officers planning to build railroads and to take coal and money out of China. These
foreigners are always shown in league with, a corrupt local leader who stands to
personally gain from collusion. Interestingly in films on the Second World War, films
portray the KMT military in a neutral light - they too were trying to defend China.
However, KMT troops are shown as much worse in quality when compared to CCP
troops - they break and run from combat much more quickly [70].
How does one protect authority and" prove loyalty? Loyalty is proved by sacrifice.
Ordinary Chinese sacrifice themselves on a regular basis - their livelihoods, wealth,
family, and lives - to protect China. And this is not just for loyalty to the Chinese nation;
to prove loyalty to whatever organization a character identifies with (family, company,
etc), a character must sacrifice [59].
In sacrificing, one might have to commit violence. But in committing violence, characters
do not do so in the same style as in Leites and Wolfenstein, where there are crimes of
passion and those motivated by internal violence that the character cannot contain. In
Chinese films, all characters are capable of committing violence, but they are adept at
controlling it. That is, they use violence as a tool like any other for specific reasons. It is
violence with purpose [76].
Self Staging
The Wolfenstein and Leites film describe's how characters in the films they survey follow
the archetypes of "Performers and. Onlookers." These characters are performers or
viewers of performances. In Chinese film; this is rare. There are plenty of examples of
onlookers - voyeurism is as important in Chinese films as they are in American films.
However, there are no actual performances in these films.
When there are performances, they are self-staging. Karaoke is a common and important
aspect of Chinese film [7]. Characters perform for themselves. They perform for one
another but not for pay.
--
These categories do not provide for a comprehensive assessment of films - the range of
films covered is quite vast and reductive categories cannot hope to cover every nuance of
film. Film plots range from dark comedies set in the Chinese desert [35], to films about
families coping with the aftermath of earthquakes [62], to searches for crocodiles [95].
There is no reason to assume that films would be any less wide-ranging in subject matter
than what they try to capture - life itself ....
12. Film Summaries
Modern Films
1. The Wedding Banquet (1993)
The Wedding Banquet is unique among these films for being a joint US-Taiwanese
production. It depicts an elaborate scheme enacted by a gay Taiwanese man living in
New York City, along with one of his tenants and his boyfriend in order to fool his
traditional parents that he has met and plans on marrying an Asian woman. Relationship
dynamics here are complex; while the parents are excited that their son has found a wife
(as his father remarks, "I can finally hold"y"grandson"), they realize he is faking the
marriage. At the same time, the woman enters the sham marriage for a green card, but
actually has feelings for him - playing the Leitesian vamp in seducing him and becoming
pregnant with his child. The parent-child relationships here follow closely some of the
Leitesian descriptions of relationships, particularly close relationships between mother
and son. However, the father's relationship (with all) in this film is the opposite, with a
dominant, prominence in the film.
2. Chungking Express (1994)
Chunking Express is a film with two stories, both about police officers. In the first
story, a cop mulls his breakup with a girlfriend and drug dealer encounter; while in the
second story another cop meets a restaurant worker who helps him get over the loss of a
girlfriend. The film explores how the four main characters relate and portrays them as
often being alone. In particular, the relationship of the character in the second story with
the restaurant worker is a developing love that is similar to the 'lovers and loved ones'
theme from Movies. The nice girl in the restaurant is paired with the seediness of a snack
bar - while not criminal in nature (and no violence or crime is portrayed in the film), she
is clearly a good girl in a very shady environment. She is a form of the 'seducer'
referenced in the Wolfenstein and Leites book - approaching a budding with perhaps
even more confidence than the police character, shown by her attempts at infiltrating his
apartment to try to make his life better,
3. In the Heat of the Sun (1994)
In the Heat of the Sun is titled literally; this film is set during a hot summer in
Beijing during the Cultural Revolution. A coming of age story about a teenage boy whose
parents (and indeed any adults) are out of sight, the film explores themes of loyalty,
courage, delinquency, rebellion, and finding love. The cinematography in the film evokes
the idea of memory; in addition to the heat, the colors are hazy and washed out, as if the
narrator (reflecting back on his time as a teenager in Beijing) was (mis)remembering his
childhood. He is an onlooker and voyeur; the first scene of the film shows him peering in
on a group of girls practicing dancing, and he meets and courts his love interest by first
breaking into her home and hiding under her bed while she changes. Like in the Leites
and Wolfenstein volume description of American films, his parents recede into the
background - what interactions they have with him are intermittent and he does not hold
any lessons from them.
4. Eat Drink Man Woman (1994)
Eat Drink Man Woman is a story about love - love between man and woman and
love between family members. Taking a weekly family dinner as a backdrop, the film
explores how three daughters take very divergent paths in achieving happiness in love,
and how their relationship with their father evolves. The representation of family here is
generational - similar to how Leites noted US films portrayed families as not as
continuous passages through life. Unlike American films, where fathers may have little
influence, in this film the daughter's feel serious duty to the happiness of their father, and
unlike American films where the relationship is positive - the relationship here seems to
be negative for the daughters, as their father stifles their personal growth. And finally, in
this film the emphasis is not on the family that one makes - the relationships the
daughters have serve only as ancillary setpieces to the main portrayal of the family that
one comes from, another difference from American films.
5. Good Men, Good Women (1995)
Good Men, Good Women has been billed in the west as one of the masterpieces
of film of the 1990s. It is a complex depiction of the effect of history on modern day life in
Taiwan; the ramifications of the Second World War clearly resonate in 1990s Taiwan, and
the film shows the downward spiral of a young actress training to play the role of a 1940s
revolutionary in contrast with the spiral of the revolutionary herself. The film is a portrait
of sadness in modern Taiwanese society. Of particular interest are the female characters'
relationships with their male counterparts, as well as the film's implications on love and
duty. In both relationships (both old and modern), the female character is a 'nice girl'
involved in crime (in old revolutionary activity and in modern times drug dealing and
other 'gangster' type activity). Like in the American films Leites surveys the women are
portrayed as being inherently good - but in this film they are portrayed as being
controlled by forces out of their control. They might be the 'good-bad girls' referenced in
American film - where good girls are spiced up with a façade of badness. However, they
are probably closer to the women in British films, who are good girls who become
seduced by dark, destructive men.
6. The Emperor's Shadow (1996)
In this historical film set during the imperial era, themes of loyalty, revenge, and
!
duty are explored. The main character sets into a plot (conceived for years) to take
revenge on an evil emperor. Interestingly, it also focuses on the use of culture and music
to control the masses. As the king tries to unify China (through warfare), he remarks that
to win hearts and minds of the people after unification, it will be necessary to introduce a
new anthem to bring cultural and spiritual unity to accompany political unity. In this way
the king seeks legitimacy as an emperor. The emperor is shown as a killer in the sense of
Leites' descriptions of British films - where he has internal impulses to order mass
killings (or even torturous punishments upon' Gao) that he must try to control, although
with little success.
7. Xiao Wu (1997)
Xiao Wu is a pickpocket working in a dusty Chinese provincial city. He falls in
love with a prostitute and, despite warnings from his friends, family, and the police,
continues to steal to fund spending on his girlfriend. She is, in the Leitesian tradition, a
'good girl' who hides behind a veneer of badness - while a prostitute, she cares for and is
cared for by Xiao Wu. He has an overtly negative relationship with his parents; at one
point, his mother gives a ring he had given her as a gift to Xiao Wu's sister - lying about
where it went. But rather than being portrayed; as in the wrong, his mother is innocent.
While Leites notes that in American films children are innocent, Xiao Wu here is clearly
the source of bad impulses, although he does have a quick emotional break from them
after that final straw. Here, the emphasis ,is actually on the family made instead of the
family came from, like in the American films Leites surveys.
8. Devils on the Doorstep (2000)
This film was shot in black and white and set during the Japanese occupation of
rural China. Banned in China, it portrayed Japanese and Chinese interaction in a more
sympathetic light than most other productions, showing a group of Chinese peasants
harboring a Japanese soldier in a scheme that ultimately leads to their end (although
seemingly because of their own fault). Leites and Wolfenstein describe how in American
films, death is not acknowledged; they write "For the most part those who meet a violent
end are unsympathetic, not objects for identification or regret" (p. 233). Here, there is an
effort to portray the peasants sheltering the Japanese as sympathetic; in trying to do
something morally right, they meet their end, something the Japanese themselves realize.
While there are no funerals like in the films described by Leites and Wolfenstein, death is
not meant to be passing - it is an integral part of the fabric of this film, as it tries to give
meaning to the villagers' deaths.
9. Platform (2000)
This film explores inter-group dynamics in Communist China. Portraying a
theatre troupe, it shows how culture and the arts were used to deliver messages in rural
China. Leites and Wolfenstein demonstrate that in American films, the older generation is
shown in the background and not too active; emotional detachment. Here, particularly
for the female characters, parents are shown as incredibly overprotective - and they
emphasize arranged marriages that serve to ensure stability rather than love. Even when
men and women are shown entering relationships without an arranged marriage, and
their dialogue is stilted; they are shown as having trouble understanding how to relate to
each other without formal structures. This film contains two scenes of the troupe dancing
- one toward the beginning of the film, prior to changing socio-cultural-economic norms,
where the characters dance unnaturally, awkwardly. The second scene, after
modernization, shows the characters dancing in a manner one might expect from the
west.
10. Hero (2002)
Hero deals with themes of sacrifice, the need for authority to quash violence, and
the futility of rebellion. In this film Jet Liystars as an assassin who ultimately abandons
his quest to kill the king after realizing the necessity of uniting China under a common
dynasty. There is a side love story in this film; but since the thrust of the film is in the
story around the Qin king, the filmmakers gave light treatment to the love story. However,
there is a murder/suicide scene toward the end of the film - as one woman realizes her
lover has convinced the assassin not to undertake his mission, she becomes furious and
kills him, then commits suicide - similar to suicides in British films described by Leites
and Wolfenstein.
11. For There to be Love (2001)
This film follows four young people as they volunteer their time in Shenzhen. It
portrays in great detail their efforts to do volunteer work in their town - as well as the
benefits that reaps. Rather than a traditional love story focusing on relationships between
man and woman, this film attempts to show how love among people in general can be a
positive force in society. One of the young women finds a mother figure to help care for -
as a recurring theme in Chinese society is proper care for one's parents, this woman
serves as a sort of surrogate daughter to an elderly woman. Leites and Wolfenstein
describe how (p. 119) in the films they survey, heroines frequently lack mothers, and those
who have mothers are not important or agreeable. The opposite is true in For There to be
Love; rather, there is a strong 'mother daughter relationship with mutual benefit. The
daughter receives wisdom and counsel from the mother (here the old woman gives the
young woman advice on dating), while the older woman still feels loved.
12. Cell Phone (2003)
This comedy film looks at how technology is changing China; the main character
keeps in touch with his mistress on his cell phone. The main character takes pleasure in
skirting rules, not caring for social customs, and doing things for face - he takes cell
phone calls at any time of day no matter who he is with. Leites and Wolfenstein show that
the vamp was a foreign import to American films (p. 33), and in this film it seems the
vamp has been imported to China, as the mistress is a clear vamp. However unlike the
foreign vamps described by Leites, she is not a source of comic relief in the film but more
of a source of pain - her seduction techniques play into the naturally unorthodox social
proclivities of the main character and lead him to his ruin. At the end of the film he
divorces from his wife but loses the mistress as well.
13. Three Times (2005)
This film is shot as three vignettes - one set in 1966, one in 1911, and one in 2005
- three love stories all involving the same two actors. This is a Taiwanese film and it
makes use of regional dialects (Hokkien) in addition to Mandarin. The second short is
actually shot as a silent film, complete with dialogue cards. In general, the film is very
sparse on dialogue, which makes it challenging to complete a Leitesian analysis.
However, one aspect of the film that stands out is that in each short, the female character
is a performer - in the first she is a pool hall attendee, in the second a prostitute, and in
the third a singer. In each short, the man is an onlooker - in some fashion a client of the
woman's services. This is very similar to Leites and Wolfenstein's description of how this
relationship works in French and British films (p. 278). However, there are no depictions
of these women with other men, as there are in the British and French examples. In Three
Times, they work personally and only with the men portrayed in the shorts; there is
therefore no chance of jealousy or triangular relationships - here the filmmaker focuses
solely on the relationships between the performer and client.
14. Sunflower (2005)
Sunflower examines family - how 'families stay together regardless of tragedy
(like the Cultural Revolution and earthquakes ;as, portrayed in the film). Once again films
are shown as transmitting information to the countryside. The family changes
significantly from the 1970s to the 1990s; the change in Chinese society is really striking.
Family plays a crucial role in this film; similar to the Leites and Wolfenstein description
of fathers (p. 110), the father in this film is absent for much of it - ineffectual - because of
his purge during the Cultural Revolution. Upon his returned he is a changed man with a
disability and hopes his son can take up the mantle as an artist. There are some
voyeuristic/onlooker themes here as well, as in a pivotal scene, the father finally realizes
that his son still loves him even after his absence by secretly watching his son cry and be
emotional in a grove of trees. While not exactly the same as the voyeurism surrounding
love in the Leites/Wolfenstein work, there is a strong streak of onlooking in the film
(informers, Cultural Revolution, at one point the main character observes a girl skating
..
at an ice rink, etc).
15. The Silent Holy Stones (2005)
This is a pseudo-documentary film: looking into the effects of modernization,
consumerism, and Westernization on Tibetan culture. Produced by the Chinese
government, it shows a young lama. at, a monastery becoming introduced to, and
ultimately consumed by, television, to the effect of blocking out all of the rich cultural
traditions taking part around him as well as his family. There are several father figures in
this film. Including the lama's biological father, there are the lama's mentors at the
monastery. They all try to appease the lama and shape his development in important and
evident ways (primarily by controlling what he'can and cannot do) - this is in contrast to
the style of parenting described in Leites and Wolfenstein. However, in this film the lama
is disrespectful of those desires and seeks out western pleasures in a manner that is
intended to shame the lama and make the viewer wonder question his filial piety.
16. Crazy Stone (2006)
In only a few respects does this dark comedy about rival groups grappling for a
valuable jade pendant give insight into Chinese society. There are a few superficial
details to note: (1) the film pays homage to numerous other films (including western films
like Mission Impossible); (2) characters in this film recite historical proverbs - such as
Sun Tzu's maxim to "Know thy enemy and know thyself"; and (3) comedy here is
slapstick, similar to Hong Kong martial arts films. Of interest are the relationships
!
between men and women in this film - women are decidedly treated as objects. In one
sequence about a cheating wife, it is intimated that women are not capable of cheating
themselves - they are not clever enough - but that evil men around them trick them.
Instead of them cheating with other men, it is those men who cheat them by tricking them.
17. Curse of the Golden Flower (2006)
This film portrays deception schemes among the Chinese imperial family. The
emperor, who has recently returned with one of his generals (a son), returns to his wife,
stepson, and court. There is a concealed triangle here as the empress is having an illicit
affair with her stepson. This is in line with the descriptions of concealed triangles in the
Leites and Wolfenstein study. In this film, the concealed triangle is use as a means of
spurring rebellion; the empress uses her influence in the triangle to force her stepson to
lead a doomed rebellion against his father (her husband), which fails. He commits
suicide rather than cede to his father's punishment, which is to feed his former lover
poison daily until she dies. Finally, with respect, to the theme of authoritarianism - the
film could easily be read as a message that authoritarianism always triumphs over
rebellion, as the emperor's forces easily defeat the rebellion (as the emperor is a superior
practitioner of deception methods). However, there is also the idea that too much
authority can turn subjects brittle and make them snap; while the emperor has won, his
sons all died, he has no heir, and the future stability of his empire is unclear.
18. A Battle of Wits (2006)
This showcases the Chinese appreciation of deception; this film is about conflict
during the Warring States Era and how deception and trickery won the day (often) over
brute force. The main character is a stalwart example of Chinese manhood - calm and
collected, never tempted by women, and ready to loyally see through the task at hand
competently. There is a seducer in the film - a woman who admires Ge Li, the Mohist
fighter who aids the city-states against invading armies, but he is resolute and shakes off
the temptation to focus on fighting. With respect to material Leites and Wolfenstein would
have found interesting, this film contains relatively little as it is action-oriented.
19. The Postmodern Life of My Aunt (2006)
This film examines loneliness, aging, and loss as an experience for the elderly.
Chinese society has changed so quickly that many elderly may feel left behind; in this film
the main character is an old woman trying to adjust to family life and relationships in
new China - she is unsuccessful. The primary source of tension in this film is the woman's
attempt to find new love; feeling lonely, she meets a man at a park and engages in a brief
courtship. Unlike in the American films Leites and Wolfenstein describe (p. 47), here the
woman can clearly love twice and quickly falls in love with the man. Offering a promise
of easy and quick return on investment if she would just give him her life savings, she
ends up losing it all when he turns out to be a scammer.
20. The Legend of the Black Scorpion (2006)
The Legend of the Black Scorpion is a story about deception set in imperial
China. In this film, again we see scheming among an imperial family - involving the
poison of a black scorpion - for power and to take down the throne. This film and others
in the genre are based on Chinese historical epics. The film opens with a concealed
triangle; the emperor takes as his wife a woman whom his son loves. The Empress
(Dowager)'s machinations lead her to the'tithrone - by assassinating the Emperor.
However, in a twist, at the end of the film she finds herself assassinated, with the viewer
left unclear about the identity of the assassin. This film has a very similar plot to Curse of
the Golden Flower.
21. Assembly (2007)
This commercial success was one of the first films to consider the Chinese Civil
War - outside of propaganda. It shows the Huaihai campaign and deals with the major
themes of sacrifice and duty; it does not make a political commentary on the validity of
KMT ideology, but focuses on the experiences of individual soldiers. The film, unlike
American films described by Leites that diminish the importance of death, emphasizes
death - in long action sequences soldier and soldier are killed, and not only are their
deaths shown in great detail, but their effects of their deaths on family and friends as
well. Since this was an action movie that focused on large setpieces rather than personal
interactions, relationship dynamics are in general difficult to observe.
22. The Warlords (2007)
This historical film is set during the warring states era. It considers battling and
rivalry among warlords, who are personalities singularly are less important, but when
taken together represent the chaos and destruction in China prior to unification. In this
film brotherhood (even not between blood brothers) and submission to authority
(especially of wives to husbands) is important. The peasant army in this film is portrayed
as being of strong moral character; violence to them does not come naturally (as in some
traditions described by Leites), but as a necessity to feed families and people they love.
Violence is a tool to destroy other morally 'unsound things - for example, in this film the
selling of daughters. Relationships are portrayed not as vessels for love, but as means to
test one's loyalty. Women are submissive, and in this film women need to be pursued and
captured for them to fall in love. In Leites and Wolfenstein, there is a section titled "Will
It [Love] Ever Take the Place of Baseball?" (p. 89). This film would suggest not - but
instead of baseball, love would never replace brotherhood and the ties that men
(especially in warfare) form with one another.
23. Lust, Caution (2007)
This is one of the few films in modern China to explore sexual themes - control
