and dominance. Striking in this film is the idea of a gift culture; giving gifts to one
another is important to save face and be polite. This film covers patriotic themes - one of
the supporting male actors is shown as a perfect and heroic patriotic young man who can
hold his liquor and is never tempted by women. In the beginning of this film, the women
are shown as vamps - almost as a den of vipers hoping to manipulate and take advantage
of men. Indeed, the central plot of this film istthat a young woman seduces a Japanese
collaborator to set him up in an assassination, plot - at the last moment she warns him
and is executed along with the rest of the group. In a sense she is a classic good-bad girl,
with her sexual willingness satisfying the collaborator but her sweet nature in public
helping him view her as an idealized object.
24. Lost in Beijing (2007)
Lost in Beijing is an example of a film designed to evoke emotions among a
younger generation - it shows how young people in China might feel lost. This film was
banned in China. Without a college degree, those young people are relegated to wage
slavery and little legal or societal help; in this film the main female is character is raped
by her employer and becomes pregnant. The film shows how her boyfriend, her boss, and
herself deal with the situation; she is objectified throughout. In a complex psychological
turn, her employer finds that he likes the idea of having a child, so chooses to (with the
grudging support of her boyfriend) support, her financially (and even payoff her
boyfriend). The Leites and Wolfenstein volume, describes how women might try to take
advantage of men - for example.on page: 37. they describe the home-digger who seeks
marriage. However, when Leites and Wolfenstein describe relationships, they seem to
focus on the idea of love. In this film there is no love - just exploitation.
25. Kidnap (2007)
This film is a detective/crime thriller about a female police detective trying to find
a kidnapping victim. Police are portrayed as being devoted to their jobs, level-headed,
not prone to rash decisions, and with a strong sense of justice. Interestingly, the other
officers she supervises call her "da jie" (big sister) when they ask her things. The heroine
in this film is also a mother - and when her own son is kidnapped she is obviously deeply
concerned but continues to do her job as a professional. Similar to the American films
described in Leites and Wolfenstein, the child's father is missing/distant, and her
relationship with her son is intense feelings and fear of separation just as Leites
describes (p. 135).
26. An Autobiography of a World War II Orphan (2007)
This is a propaganda film (it was produced by a state film studio) about Japanese
activities in China during the Second World War Taking the form of an interview with an
elderly woman orphaned during the war, the film covers Japanese atrocities, lack of
respect or sympathy in Japanese society, and the lack of an official Japanese apology.
This film covered mostly 'factual' details about the Japanese occupation and had little to
do with interpersonal relationships; although the implication is that humans (especially
in warfare) are inherently violent,
27. Ashes of Time (2008)
Ashes of Time is a story about loss, memory, and redefinition of the wuxia genre.
In most martial arts films, heroes are emotionally detached and serve as stoic enforcers
of justice - in this film, they are flawed, emotional characters, who nearly all meet their
ends due to their relationships. In the Leites and Wolfenstein book, there was significant
discussion of violence - here the violence follows American films, where the hero has
motive but is not inherently bad - they have not done anything wrong. However, unlike
American films, there is a great deal of emotion involved in violence, very similar to
British films. Moreover, the way relationships function in this film follows the seducer
paradigm Leites explains; both women and men are seducers, unlike American films
where this paradigm seemed to have gone by the time Wolfenstein and Leites conducted
their analysis.
28. If You are the One (2008)
Many modern Chinese films consider love and what it means - why does one
marry? Who does one marry? How does a marriage last? In this film the main character
suddenly becomes rich and no longer needs: to work; he dates online to find someone to
spend his life (and money) with, finds a woman whom he thinks he is incompatible with
but then realizes would be very compatible. Leites and Wolfenstein describe the 'home-
digger,' an "intensely attractive and emotionally cold girl, suffering from humble
circumstances, who is ready to marry any man who will give her the comforts she
requires" (p. 39). While the heroine here does not seem to come from humble
circumstances, both characters are clearly ready to settle down and to find emotional
comforts; so while Leites and Wolfenstein describe a world in which economic comforts
dominate, in the Chinese world - and in this film - making a home and making a family is
primarily an emotional concern first with financial concerns secondary. And this is
despite the fact that the hero in this film istrich - it seems like even in spit of his wealth
the heroine falls in love with him. Money, falls by the wayside in the face of true love;
whereas in the Leites and Wolfenstein analysis economic concerns are very important.
29. Red Cliff, Part I (2008)
This is the first part to the Red Cliff series, which takes the Romance of the Three
Kingdoms novel from Chinese classical literature and turns it into an action movie. The
film emphasizes loyalty, duty, courage, and deception among the main characters as
primary character traits. Relationships are stylized - the focus is on friendships (and
conflict) between men. There is an example. of the Leitesian 'masculine girl,' as one of the
primary heroines is very skilled at martial arts. The Leites and Wolfenstein descriptions
of 'killers and victims' focus on murders, individual motivations for crime, and human
desires for violence. In Red Cliff, violence is used as Clausewitz envisioned it - as
politics by other means. None of the characters hold grudges against each other or view
their conflict to be personal (despite the fact that their war spans an entire nation).
Rather, they understand that they have differing political viewpoints and serve different
masters, and these facts should not lead them to violence for its own sake, but they should
use violence as a means to exert their political beliefs.
30. Overheard (2009)
Voyeurism - seeing - is the premise of this film. Three police officers comprise the
main characters in this film; assigned to a surveillance detail on insider trading, they
quickly become mixed up in a 'scheme to pump stock prices and profit. They use
voyeurism to spy on stock brokers, each others, criminals, and each others' loved ones -
but in doing so, seem to sacrifice their morality and almost all die; they become
onlookers in the Leitesian sense. A quote on authority rings out - "If you had turned
yourselves in, this would not be a problem." The main mob boss in the film follows the
Leitesian concept of violence - it is not inherent in him ("I am not a natural born
killer"), referenced in French films. Heroes and victims in the film deal with a mix of
impulses but a sort of ironic justice is served: There is not much emphasis on love in this
film, other than a basic treatment that one of the main characters is a providing father for
his sick son.
31. Stubborn Robot/Radish Warrior (2009)
While there is not much discussion about love in this film, the parents and
children theme plays a significant role; In this film, a father loses his entire family and all
of his life savings in a scam - and to wih it all back he tries a scheme to rob his robber.
Unlike the American films Leites surveys, in this film (and in many Chinese films), this
film shows how important family is in Chinese society - they have long term relations (the
son embarks on an alternate scheme ito retrieve the money to stay close to his father),
unlike in American films, and the father has serious influence in this film.
32. A Railway in the Clouds (2009)
This is a film glorifying the efforts of the PLA to construct railways in Tibet. The
PLA is shown as having made repeated expeditions into the wilderness of Tibet to provide
a railway to service the Tibetan population, with heavy loss of life. With sufficient
courage and dedication, the PLA finally succeeded in their most recent attempt -
replicated in this film. Unlike in the American films Leites describes where parents do not
play a large role, the legacy of the hero's father dying in constructing a similar project
weighs heavily on his mind. But like the Leitesian analysis, the father figure is no longer
around. Even when the hero's mother dies in his hometown, he does not return for the
sake of the project - he apologizes to his mother saying "I am sorry I cannot fulfill
loyalty and filial piety at the same time." While his relationship with his parents may
have been important in the past (and their, legacy is clearly important), his current work
is of greater importance.
33. A Place of One's Own (2009)
This is another film about aimlessness in Chinese society; drug use, lack of steady
employment, evictions, and a continued quest to find a place to put down roots. This film
is set in Taiwan but the manner in which the main characters relate is representative of
mainland film. The characters all have overwhelmingly negative relationships with each
other. Interestingly, there is no sign of government presence in this film like there is in
other mainland films. In this film the heroine is highly capable; her boyfriend and the
others in his band are decadent, lazy, and seem to be directionless. Leites and Wolfenstein
describe love in American films are being flexible; in this one it is. The talented singer
eventually leaves her boyfriend as it becomes evident he is going nowhere. There are two
side-stories to this that follow in parallel (with the three stories converging somewhat
toward the end of the film); in both there are traditional familial relationships shown.
While at first glance the primary focus of this film is a preoccupation with housing, it
becomes apparent that the actual focus of this film is how housing affects relationships;
relationships are changed by possessing or not possessing an apartment with serious
effect.
34. The Founding of a Republic (2009)
This film was sponsored by the Chinese government to commemorate the founding
of the PRC. Looking at the military and political machinations necessary for the CCP to
prevail over the Nationalists, this film puits a positive and heroic spin on Communist
actions. Characters are drawn from 'real-life personages and Mao in particular is
portrayed as a loyal and courageous. ledder. In Red Cliff, the leaders are portrayed as
desiring to gain (politically and militarily) but having no personal innate evil that drives
it. In this film, Chiang Kai Shek actively plans for conflict and violence (the implication
being he loves it) while Mao Zedong is portrayed as willing to compromise; even KMT
troops are shown beating crowds of peaceful celebrants in their quest to subdue the
Chinese people. Contrasts between the two men are evident - where Mao speaks from the
heart, Chiang speaks rigidly from notes. Where Mao spends time with his men and the
peasants they protect, Chiang remains cloistered with his senior advisers in a compound.
Mao's willingness to get close with the people overcomes Chiang's formality and
ceremony. Mao in essence serves as a father, figure for the PLA - and since there are no
other real relationships shown, we might view this as the primary relationship in the film,
one that stands in contrast to the.Leitesian tradition of American films where the parents
do not have an impact. Mao, as father of the country, has an incredible impact on the
lives of those in his extended family not just as a titular leader but as a personal and
moral one as well.
35. A Woman, A Gun, and A Noodle Shop (2009)
This surreal film is one of the more inventive to come out in China in recent years.
It has to do with family relationships in a remote part of rural China in the past; themes
of betrayal, love, friendship, and greed are explored, with the characters conniving to
manipulate each other for personal benefit. There is a strong woman in this film who runs
a noodle shop in the desert; she is sleeping with one of the noodle shop's employees and
scheming against her husband. The heroine fits the character type Leites and Wolfenstein
describe as the 'bad girl,' one who uses men to her advantage and one for whom love is
less important than money. She is not a 'good-bad girl,' with redeemable qualities or one
who turns out not to be actually bad, but she is clearly one willing to use others to get her
way.
36. City of Life and Death (2009).
City of Life and Death is about the Rape of Nanking, an episode in 1937 where
Imperial Japanese Army soldiers pillaged and raped Nanking after Chinese troops
withdrew from the city. Portraying the Japanese as savage and ordinary Chinese as
highly sympathetic toward each other's plight (the climax of the film involves a prostitute
sacrificing her own life), the film highlights common bonds among Chinese. There is less
emphasis on individual relationships, as most of these characters neither knew one
another well nor were in good position to make a relationship during a time of war. The
emphasis is on collective action - soldiers standing up against the Japanese together, or
soldiers willing to die for China together, or groups of people banding together to protect
each other. If the morality of a group is right, collective action can lead to heroic things
and great accomplishments; if the morality of a group is wrong, collective action will
lead to rape and pillaging - in this case the example Japanese soldiers set.
37. The Message (2009)
This is another film set during the Second World War and the Japanese
occupation of China. A film about deception and intelligence work, it glorifies resistance
work during the occupation, how clever both sides (resistance and occupiers) behaved,
and honor in not giving up one's secrets. Characters are lauded (or portrayed favorably)
for remaining loyal to a greater cause and for sacrificing themselves (or others) when
necessary. Because so much of this film, has to do with secrecy and the Chinese
resistance, there is relatively little emphasis on relations. In a sense, their bodies/genders
are less important than their minds. "Is something worth more than your life?" The
answer the film gives is yes - when it is a message that deals with a cause bigger than
ones self.
38. Red Cliff, Part II (2009)
This is the conclusion to the Red Cliff series and follows the same themes/trend as
the first film.
39. Sophie's Revenge (2009)
This film was produced in conjunction with a South Korean studio, another
example that the Chinese industry has opened up in recent years. It is a romantic comedy
- they have become incredibly popular - and deals with standard themes in Chinese
romantic films: the meaning of marriage, the role of women in society, companionship,
loneliness. In this film, the relationship between the man and woman is female
dominated! Love is the primary interest; Sophie narrates "Women have to find their inner
self to find true love ... a man is afraid to enter the wrong career, a woman is afraid to
marry the wrong man." In this film nice guys actually get the girl (in contrast to the
tradition in the west). The portrayal of Sophie in the film is reminiscent of Leites and
Wolfenstein's descriptions of vamps (p. 33) in their volume. She would be closer to a
'good-bad girl,' however, as she is not actually bad but only seems that way (she is an
author and the book she is writing is drawings of revenge against her ex-fiance; she is
portrayed as having justifiable concerns).
40. Bodyguards and Assassins (2009)
This historical film turns Sun Yat Sen's visit to Hong Kong in 1905 into an action
thriller - essentially it pits a grassroots group of bodyguards seeking to guard him
against a group of imperial assassins sent to crush his nascent political movement.
Sacrifice, loyalty, and obedience to correct moral authority (rather than corrupt moral
authority) were the primary themes here, and guided how the characters interacted. The
father-son dynamic in the film is very strong; the hero's father strongly disapproves of
him taking any kind of action to protect Sun Yat Sen in order to protect his business
interests, but he eventually comes around. to
tto the morally 'correct' side, despite the
obvious detriments to his business.
41. Memory of Love (2009)
Memory of Love is a dark psychological thriller. It tells the story of a doctor
whose wife gets into a serious cat accident leaving her with amnesia. Her husband treats
her, but in the process discovers that the reason she was in an accident in the first place
was because she was having an affair. The movie shows how they relate to one another
after the revelation (of which she no longer had knowledge) and how they tried to piece
together their relationship. The idea of a concealed triangle - discussed in Leites and
Wolfenstein - is prevalent in this film for obvious reasons. Of note is that there is utterly
no mention of the older generation in the film; one might think that either the doctor or
wife would like to involve parents in the case of a serious accident, but here the younger
generation is left to deal with the mess they have made on their own - without advice
from those who might know better.
42. The Road Home (2009)
This film takes the theme of filial piety to an extreme. An urban dweller's father,
who was a teacher in a rural school, passeslaway, and he returns to help with the funeral.
Throughout the film, he and his mother grapple with the loss of their father and husband
- demonstrating that in Chinese society these bonds extend beyond death. This film
probably demonstrates the Confucianist family structure to the greatest extent of any
film. This story differs greatly from Leites and Wolfenstein's descriptions of the role of
parents. They note, in relation to the role of the hero's father in film, "[i]n most cases, it
would make no difference to the course of events if the father were eliminated from the
story" (p. 110). In this film, where the whole purpose of the hero's journey home was to
honor his father (and to better understand his mother's grief), this would be impossible.
Moreover, the film tells the story of the love between the hero's father and mother, who
met while the father taught in a quiet village. Like in American films Leites and
Wolfenstein describe, the mother falls in love quickly and then cannot fall in love again -
she cannot love twice.
43. Empire of Silver (2009)
This film explores Chinese economics in the late 1800s, showing how Chinese
bankers adapted to political unrest (the Zuthdi Rebellion), changes to paper money, and
grappling with moral issues when confronted with peasants in need. Ultimately a
morality tale about one's duty to honor 'ancestors by staying true to principles, the film
promotes the rights of peasants in standing against corrupt and greedy forces. Family is
crucial to the film - the patriarch of the' banking clan uses his sons to manage operations
and grooms them to take over. The tension between following rigid regulations and doing
what one might consider more morally right is a big concern for the bankers - the root
problem, of course, being that the laws/rules are in conflict with what is morally sound.
The vision of the father in the film follows that described by Leites and Wolfenstein in the
tradition of American films, where the father suffers from violent tendencies - he is not an
ideal-figure but does what he must (hiring guards to beat protesters etc) to protect his
family and business.
44. Together (2009)
Together is about a father and son remaining together despite the odds being
against them. In this film, the son is a violin prodigy who travels to Beijing with his father
to study under a master. The father wants the best for his son, who sees things slightly
different. The two meet various teachers, including ones who try to take advantage of the
boy, but through their relationship manage to stay together and achieve mutual happiness
(not always through success in music). The father figure in this film is closest to what
Leites and Wolfenstein describe as the French father - an ideal father free from sexual
frustration or aggression. He care's for his son and wants only the very best for him.
Leites and Wolfenstein describe how the son may not be as good of a person as the
father; in this film that is true but only because he is young and learning - while he strays
from his father at times, he ultimately comes back and realizes how his father only wants
the best.
45. Accident (2009)
Accident is a standard action movie about a crew of hitmen who specialize in
making deaths look like accidents - veiling, their actions in deception to avoid discovery
by the police (or having their clients discovered). Violence in this film is not something
taken lightly; the way the assassins operate is portrayed as precise and surgical; they
only do as much harm as they need to do so as to make the operation go off without a
hitch. Leites and Wolfenstein describe how in many of the films they surveyed, men kill
because of frustration in love. But more generally, they discuss how American men kill
because they have something to gain and British men kill because of something they have
lost - in how they work for pay, the hitmen' group in Accident are clearly in the first
category. .
46. Crazy Racer (2009)
Crazy Racer is a dark comedy film about a disgraced bicyclist who was
wrongfully accused of doping. After being stripped of his medals, he stumbles across an
opportunity to take revenge on the man who duped him and his mentor. The themes of
loyalty, revenge, and deception permeate the characters' relationships. In particular, the
hero feels strongly for his former coach, who ultimately died in disgrace because of false
accusations. As a father figure, the coach was violent (like American fathers described by
Leites and Wolfenstein), but clearly held the racer's respect. Leites and Wolfenstein
describe (p. 123) how neuroses may be caused by overindulgent parents; here, the racer's
neuroses seem to be caused in part by the coach's Spartan training regimens and utter
lack of emotional empathy (prior to his death).
47. Shanghai (2010)
Shanghai is a spy film set in Shanghai in October 1941, prior to the official
Japanese declaration of the Pacific War. This, film examines espionage and stars John
Cusack in a leading role as an American naval intelligence officer (his voiced is dubbed
in Chinese, along with all the rest of the cast, who apparently acted in English). This
production was filmed in Bangkok after the plot involving opium and prostitution was
frowned upon by local Chinese authorities; however, China was the first country in which
this film was screened, and it has never been released in the United States. Cusack's
character arrives in Shanghai to investigate the death of his friend and partner, another
American agent. Through a complex web of deception, he finds that his friend seduced
the mistress of a Japanese naval intelligence officer, stumbled upon the Pearl Harbor
plot, and Cusack's character races (to no avail) to get the information to the United
States before the attack. The common spy film themes of Japanese corruption/aggression,
foreign indifference (at one point the slain American officer remarks in a flashback to "do
whatever's necessary, and if our superiors don't know, so be it"), and deception schemes
revolving around espionage. In reference to Leitesian themes in the film, while violence is
portrayed as necessary (not coming from within and directed by certain needs), similar to
some of the films in W+L's sample, the director here portrays the Japanese are possessing
internal bloodlust that colors their actions. They are repeatedly shown attacking and
abusing (committing violence) in the most brutal ways; in one scene, they choose to
behead a resistance collaborator instead of any other means of death. While there are
some depictions of love - the Japanese officen's love for his mistress drives the plot - it is
merely a backdrop and there is not enough substance in the depiction to comment on
relevance to W+L's themes.
48. Echoes of the Rainbow (2010):
-
This film is one of the few Chinese films that depict relationships between siblings.
While there are films about the historical era where sibling relationships are relevant,
these often show discord between siblings - they fight with one another for control of the
throne or country. This film shows the relationship between an elementary-school age
child and his older brother that grows stronger as the older brother falls sick with
leukemia and eventually dies. The father in this film is overbearing, hardworking, and
has incredibly high expectations for both of his sons. The Leites and Wolfenstein study
focuses on twins (p. 140), but when they describe how "the more obscure brother is less
confident and adept when thrown into his, double's position" (p. 142), the same is true in
this film - both brothers are students, and the younger brother is far less accomplished
than the older. But unlike how Leites and Wolfenstein go on to describe, they have no
conflict. There is no rivalry or guilt in how the two relate in this film; they appear to have
only love for one another - possibly more love than that their parents feel for them.
49. Confucius (2010)
This is a biopic of Confucius, covering the latter half of his life when he became
elevated to Minister of Law in the Lu court and then his self-imposed exile as an itinerant
scholar. There are no romantic relationships shown here, nor are there any real scenes of
violence, nor are these really any scenes of family shown. This makes this film in large
part unrelatable to Leites and Wolfenstein's study. The film describes broad themes of
loyalty, filial piety, conviction to strong principles, and humility - Confucius is repeatedly
shown in the film as a humble figure unwilling to accept honor and only desiring an
opportunity to teach his philosophy to leaders to that they may better the people. A
recurring strand in the film is the idea that China is a 'messy' and violent place, with a
need for principles like Confucius' to stay together.
50. Wind Blast (2010)
This film is about violence and how it affects individuals. The film revolves around
what Leites would call killers and victims - with the opening scene of the film a down-on-
his-luck small time criminal conducting an assassination in Hong Kong, and then
becoming pursued into the Gobi Desert by a team of assassins and a team of police. The
film follows the British approach (according to Leites) of depicting violence - the
authorities ultimately prevail, violence is at the center of the film, it focuses on the violent
impulses of man (with a "masculine-girl" in the Leitesian tradition serving as an
archtype assassin), and many of the 'bad' characters end up feeling guilty by the end of
the film. As Leites notes, women can use physical force and men can become their pawns
- in this film, the female assassin tries but ultimately fails - stopped by her mentor, a
man.
51. Lost on Journey (2010)
Lost on Journey is a slapstick comedy chronicling the travels of a wealthy CEO
and a milk farm worker (with many references to the melamine scandal). With respect to
character relationships, there are few examples of male-female relationships - with the
exception of the businessman breaking up with his mistress after becoming transformed
by the journey from a cold-blooded profit-monger to someone who cares about people. It
is a moralistic tale - when the pair find a beggar whom the businessman thinks is a
scammer, the farm worker (supposed to be academically idiotic but with a heart of gold),
remarks "I believe people should help each other ... You are so wealthy, you should help
her." Onlooking/viewing is a major part of this film. The businessman and the worker
watch each other get into trouble and only on the cusp of disaster do they end up helping
each other; in one scene, the businessman must even hide in a woman's hotel room -
whose husband believes she is having an affair - and is mistaken for the woman's lover
when he finds it hard to explain that he merely entered the wrong room.
52. Hot Summer Days (2010)
Several love stories interweave in this complex narrative. A lighthearted romantic
comedy, false appearances play a major role. The primary couple conduct the majority of
their courting via SMS messaging, lying to each other about their profession, but
ultimately upon meeting, realize they have known each other all along. In this film, unlike
in Leiteisan analysis, there are no good-bad girls; all girls are good. There are no vamps
or seducers, the women are portrayed as incredibly sweet and innocent. They are also
portrayed as feminine; no masculine-feminine women either. However, in this film also
there are few connections with these characters" families - in the love stories told here,
the emphasis is on the family made, not on the family from which the characters came.
53. Driverless (2010)
This film also has a number of stories that weave together. The film opens in
media res, showing a major car accident, and the rest of the film shows how the seven
characters arrived at the accident and what they did after. There are three major
relationships here - one between two businesspeople (who used to be lovers in university
ten years prior), one between a deaflmute and a taxi driver (with the innocent, lonely girl
voyeuristically spying on the taxi driver in a manner reminiscent of Leites' onlookers),
and a chauffeur and a wealthy businesswoman - where the relationship comes not from
love but from respect. The voyeuristic young girl is an interesting character, taking
photos of characters to express herself. In this film, the director seems to set out to
understand the meaning of love in a changed China - by what standard does one
measure love? What are the things one might do for love? And how/why might one find
love, and in a relationship, what does one look for? These themes are consistent with
some of the themes expressed by Leites but seem to take the further.
54. Go LaLa Go! (2010)
This romantic comedy explores the balance between work and relationships in
professional China. A secretary (LaLa) starts at a US multinational in Beijing, eventually
working her way up the corporate ladder and forming a relationship with the company's
sales director along the way. There is a major love triangle (similar to what Leites
references) in the form of the sales director, Lala, and the HR director, with whom the
sales director had a prior relationship" and who promoted Lala to her role as his
secretary. There is also a great deal on 'önlooking' in this film - but in this film onlooking
leads to love instead of impeding it.
55. Legend of the Fist: The Return of Chen Zhen (2010)
This film is set in 1925 China and is a fairly standard film about the beginning of
Japanese occupation. Overwhelmingly, this'film is about the dangers of foreign conquest
- repeatedly in the film Chinese and China are abused by foreign forces. It is one of the
.....
only films to mention Chinese involvement andthe First World War, and Chinese anger at
not having been rewarded by the allies for their war contribution. This film emulates
those films in the Leites and Wolfensteinstudy; it is set in 1920s Shanghai and features
aspects of that era's culture - nightclubs, gambling, smoking, tuxedos, and vamps. The
"Kiki" character in this film is a good: example of the vamp, and in the Leitesian
categories this would align closely with an American film.
56. If You are the One 2 (2010)
This is the continuation of If You are the One, showing the couple from the first
film in their married life. Continuing to explore the theme of the point of marriage, they
are shown as having significant trouble adjusting to their new lives - the wife even
attempts suicide. Ultimately the viewers' conceptions about love, engendered by the first
film, are confirmed by the success of the relationship. But in confirming that success, the
film poses the question - can love have an expiration date? Does a couple's love for one
another change over time? Is there such a thing as aesthetic fatigue? Is it worth it to stay
together when the spark in a relationship is gone? In this sense this film seems to differ
from many of the Leitesian films by asking deeper questions about the meaning of love -
accepting that there are no universals - and giving both characters agency in the process
of discovering it.
57. Triple Tap (2010)
Triple Tap is a police procedural film - an incredibly complex psychological
thriller with multiple plot layers that are somewhat satisfactorily wrapped up in the end.
Similar in structure and procedure to western crime thrillers, of interest here are the
relationships between characters of authority and their subordinates, motivations behind
criminal acts, appropriate times to bend laws and times to follow them to the letter, and
differences of the Hong Kong judicial system to the mainland. In this film, the (anti) hero
organizes a robbery with the help of a bank manager, whom he kills. He kills for the same
reasons as killers in British films - he has latent violent impulses stemming from
professional failures and takes them out in" competitive shooting - which leads to the
robbery attempt. A final note about the film is his relationship with his supervisor, who
has protected his failures for too long and ultimately must turn against him as his lies
unravel.
58. Detective Dee: Mystery of the Phantom Flame (2010)
In a film dealing with themes of deception, duty, and black magic, this film
portrays magic in a way rarely seen in Chinese film. In this film, a detective originally
prosecuted for treason is brought back to the Court by Empress Wu Zetian to investigate
a series of murders; his intelligence and morality triumph over forces in the court that
would assassinate Wu and plunge China back into chaos. There are a lot of portrayals of
magic in this film, but few portrayals, of relationships that would be of interest to Leites
and Wolfenstein.
59. Ocean Heaven (2010)
Ocean Heaven examines the plight of China's mentally disabled. In Jet Li's first
dramatic role, he plays a handyman at an aquarium - his wife passed away early and his
son is severely mentally disabled, and Jet Li's character develops terminal cancer. The
film examines not just the rights and options available to the disabled, but also the
relationships between father and son toward the end of life. The father in this film is the
'ideal' father-figure, as Leites and Wolfenstein described for fathers in French films. He is
caring, has no real sexual desires, or anger. There is a romantic interest in the film,
spurred by a promoter of love (as Leites and Wolfenstein) would describe - seeing Jet Li's
character to be lonely in his illness, she introduces him to a woman who
60. Sacrifice (2010)
Sacrifice is an historical film about intrigue in the Chinese imperial court. In this
film, a doctor raises an orphaned child as his own son, preparing him for over 20 years
to take revenge on an evil emperor. The son never learns of his true family background
until the end of the film, when he finally avenges his family. Of course, the doctor
sacrifices himself at the end of the film to give his 'son' a chance at a killing blow. The
portrayal of fathers is similar to how Leites and Wolfenstein describe heroes as fathers;
they exercise a great deal of influence on their sons' lives, and their sons are their
"immortality" (p. 125). The doctor wishes for his adopted son to carry on a legacy that
will last after his death, by eliminating the emperor. Of interest in this film is a triangle -
the emperor, finding that he would like a son as well, tries to teach and sway the doctor's
adopted son over to his 'side.'
61. Let the Bullets Fly (2010)
This is a bandit/heist film set in the Chinese 'west' in the early 20th
century. This
film pits a corrupt local leader against a bandit group who has come to profit but reduce
local corruption at the same time. There is serious tension between the needs of the elite
and the needs of the people. The rivalry between the two main characters shows they
respect each others' intellect; but that they are committed to using all tools at their
disposal (including deception techniques) to win, Characters in this film kill each other -
attacking the other side for gain - but always for a purpose, they do not kill out of
passion, but economic gain. Love does not factor at all into decisions about violence in
this film - a contrast to many of the films Leites and Wolfenstein surveyed.
62. Break Up Club (2010)
This film mixes technology and love in a portrait of relationships in modern
China. In this film, a number of couples break, up - and through the intervention of the
Internet, documentary film technology, and text messaging, they try to fall back in love
but realize they cannot - for a woman, not settling for an aimless man is important;
complacency does not seem to be good for anybody. What is interesting is that DV
cameras are central parts of this film, and it is unclear whether in reality the Chinese
government would allow a documentary film company to distribute cameras and produce
a film of this nature. The bad girl, who has so much attention in Leites and Wolfenstein's
volume, is not in this film - only good girls. The film contains ample examples of
onlookers and voyeurism as it is oriented around capturing documentary film. Finally,
loyalty in love is a primary concern in this film.
63. Aftershock (2010)
Aftershock tells a story in two parts - one part in 1976 during the Tangshan
Earthquake (showing the devastation and pain) and then another part showing different
members of the same family living apart, not knowing who each other are, and then
coming back together in the face of the 2008 Sichuan Earthquake. It is an incredibly
emotional movie showcasing the importance of family in Chinese culture. Leites and
Wolfenstein describe sibling bonds but not the brother/sister relationship. Here the
relationship is nonexistent for a long time - for most of their lives the brother and sister
live apart. Upon meeting, they realize how similar they are and the bond seems to be
natural. The older generation, referenced in Leites and Wolfenstein, is an integral part of
this story - parent/child bonds are incredibly strong, and while there may be anger, the
lasting family relationship is enough to weather any problems. This includes, in this film,
a mother abandoning her daughter in earthquake rubble to save her son.
64. Buddha Mountain (2010)
Buddha Mountain is about the dimlessness of Chinese youth, and how they relate
to each other and to those of the older generation. While to some extent the young will
always treat the previous generation as out of touch, it seems to be very evident here. The
girl in this film is a bad girl - as described by Leites and Wolfenstein. She typifies
problems the filmmakers feel face Chinese youth - alcoholism, drug abuse, abusive
girlfriends, general aimlessness, lack of options without entry into university, and
loneliness. Leites and Wolfenstein have a quote that is relevant to the youth themes in this
film: " ... that Spring should vanish with the Rose! That Youth's sweet-scented Manuscript
should close!" (p. 48). Thinking about youth, leaving youth, and dealing with the
transition is a major theme of the film.
65. Shaolin (2011)
Shaolin is set in Henan province, where the famous Shaolin temple (and monks)
has stood for hundreds of years. Set during the early republican era in China, the film
mixes the plotlines of scheming among warlords with foreign raiding of China. There are
no portrayals of romantic relationships in the film; the film's primary themes are
repentance and violence - in what circumstances and how might someone repent, and
what drives men to violence? Violence is central to this film, and this follows the British
tradition as described by Leites and Wolfenstein - violence is an internal destructive
force that compels men to act - but it can be controlled with proper guidance. "Through
Martial Zen, he has released his hatred. Martial arts is one way to enlightenment.
Cooking is another
way
„to
enlightenment .... life is an accumulation of
experiences" [1:09:50].
66. A Big Deal (2011)
This film is about trying to get rich in Dubai. In most Chinese films, characters
are in China only - or nearby in Asia. Very rarely do they go abroad (as they do so often
in western films). In this film friendships fall apart because of the influence of capitalism;
greed for money makes the friends who traveled to Dubai together split apart (but then
come back together). This comedy film has few relationships of interest to Leites and
Wolfenstein but expresses concepts of innate human greed.
67. My Own Swordsman (2011)
This film is a darker comedy set in the historical Chinese west that takes a
.-....
:
satirical look at some elements of modern Chinese society. First - some superficial
comments: (1) the film focuses heavily on, real estate speculation, which has become a
huge issue in many Chinese cities; (2) it also considers stock market speculation; and (3)
it considers some in-jokes for the movie industry, including an aging Hong Kong martial
arts star poking fun at himself (and many HK actors) for having terrible Mandarin. Many
of the archtypes from the Chinese imperial epic film exist here: the corrupt official, the
stalwart upholder of moral values, and the trickster. All the women in this film are
incredibly masculine and aggressive - with both love and with violence. Leites noted that
manipulating women only appeared in comedies - and the main character of the film is
just that, a manipulating woman looking out for her success.
68. Love is Not Blind (2011)
This film is about breakups. It shows a number of bad relationships with
significant others finding each other in compromising relationships; it also adds in the
supervisor-employee relationship into the mix. The message (and the way relationships
work in this film) is that love and marriage is built on loyalty and humility - you need to
find someone who will be with you no matter what happens, EVEN if one falls out of love.
The main character of this film is a wedding planner who finds her boyfriend out
shopping with another woman - in recovering from her loss, she finds that she falls in
love with her best friend and coworker. This film explores the themes of what is the
purposes of finding love - what kinds of people should one seek and what might sustain a
relationship? The relationship she has with her boss is similar to a father-daughter
relationship, particularly one that might have been suggested by Leites in how loving he
is.
69. Jump Ashin! (2011)
Jump Ashin! follows a gymnastics prodigy, Ashin, as he grapples with coming of
age while training for a career in gymnastics. Falling into crime with his sidekick, the
film focuses on finding oneself, relationships with close friends, and what it means to be
redeemed. Ultimately a morality tale, and handling issues like drug addiction, the film
ends on an optimistic tone - but with the optimism grounded on principles of discipline
and redemption. The depiction of family in this film is different from depictions in W+L
volume, as their work indicated films played a lesser role in hero development - while
here the parents play an integral role. He also has a relationship with a teacher that
might be considered a father-son relationship, but which is not biological; this
occasionally happens in Chinese film with plots oriented around teachers and students.
This is a Taiwanese film and set in Taiwan.
70. The Lost Bladesman (2011)
This story comes from the Romance of the Three Kingdoms classic, portrayed in
the Red Cliff films. It shows the, general. Guan Yu crossing a five passes and slaying six
generals to return to his liege Liu Bei. It is, a story about duty, honor, and the feeling that
one's life is never really one's own, but in the control of larger forces. There is a strong
underlying theme that China needs strong authoritative leadership to maintain order and
to reduce chaos. Characters repeat the phrase. "this world is ruled by wolves." Cao Cao,
the pious leader in this film trying to sway Guan Yu away from Liu Bei, is shown as a
champion of the working class - with even a scene depicting him working in the fields.
When Guan Yu must slay generals who are in his way from returning to Liu Bei, he does
so because he is forced to; he does not desire violence. And nor do the men who stand in
his way desire violence. Cao Cao becomes horrified at hearing this news and it is
revealed that it was the emperor - corrupt and trying to prove himself - who ordered him
to surrender or be killed. Finally, Guan Yu makes a great show out of remaining humble
and respectful to those he considers his superiors. This is a general theme in the film -
that people are powerless and fatalistic; that their lives are driven by forces out of their
control - one soldier remarks to Guan Yu right before Guan Yu kill him that "My life isn't
in my hands. You're not different."" When' Guan Yu wants to kill the emperor, he is
persuaded not to by Cao Cao, who argues that such an act would "unleash complete
chaos."
71. The Flowers of War (2011)
Christian Bale stars in this film set during the Rape of Nanking. Focusing on two
groups who found themselves in a cathedral during the Japanese invasion, a group of
schoolgirls and a group of prostitutes, the film describes how the groups - mediated by
the mortician Christian Bale plays - protect each other from the Japanese. Ultimately,
the prostitutes decide to sacrifice themselves to protect the girls, with the mortician
playing a key role. Bale's character, an American, is portrayed in a very positive light,
which is somewhat of an aberration for most Chinese film. Here the Japanese soldiers
are portrayed as being driven by base animal instincts; lust for sex and violence. For
them violence comes from within - like in the American films described by W+L - but
they have no desire or ability to change it. The prostitutes toward the end of the film
approach something like that of mothers, and their sacrifice to save the schoolgirls is
consistent with the idea of mothers sacrificing for children.
tion .
72. The Law of Attraction (2011).
Four vignettes: encounter, romance, betrayal, and rebirth. None of the stories are
related, but they all center around love and how it works. In the fourth story, rebirth, the
woman in the relationship is a' former drug addict just released from prison and
estranged from her parents; together with her boyfriend she puts her life back together,
showing that strong love and true love stays close in times of need. The parents in the
vignettes are all absent; similar to how they are portrayed in the W+L work. Children are
left to deal with their problems on their own as adults. The first vignette - encounter -
has significant elements of voyeurism, as the security guard a the airport uses her
position to gather information on the auditor and try to get to know him better. A hidden
triangle in the form of an affair is shown in vignette three; similar to another film with
this same plot, a couple becomes seriously injured but it is revealed that the woman in the
car was having an affair - and that upon discovering this triangle her husband caused
the accident. And they ultimately fall back in love and she spends tie visiting him in the
hospital after his eventual arrest.
73. Cold War (2012)
Cold War is a Hong Kong police procedural film looking at how to root out
corruption within Hong Kong's police department. The film describes a plot to hijack a
police van and kidnap five HK olice; officer's, as well as the responses the department
takes to get them back and to find the perpetrators. The film has several layers of
.
. 1:
meaning - first is the rivalry between two competing deputy police commissioners, one
from the administrative branch and one from the operations side. The administrative
commissioner "wins" the rivalry, gaining kudos and plaudits for his handling of the
investigation (the operations commissioner's son was the ultimate culprit, wanting to
elevate his father into the commissioner role). In this way the signal is that thinking and
planning win out over action, where the operations commissioner used men to
understand the case, the administrative commissioner used thinking and investigative
tactics. The other level of meaning is the remarks toward the UK's handover of Hong
Kong to China; the administrative commissioner seems to represent the new wave of
leadership coming from China, technocrats, logical, rational, and not susceptible to
corruption. The Leitesian themes here fall under the parents and children realm; the son
organized the scheme to elevate his father, but his father, with his loyalty to the force and
to the city, ultimately disowns him and allows him to be shot and taken away - family ties
do not win out here over ties to country and duty.
74. Back to 1942 (2012)
This historical film looks back at the 1942 famine in Henan province, which killed
upwards of 3 million rural peasants. The film follows the movements of one refugee
family and shows their hardships as they try to migrate from Henan to Shaanxi; in a
parallel track, it shows the corruption and: incompetence of the KMT government in
addressing the famine, and suggests that Chiang Kai-Shek's inability to deal with it
caused China to lose face in the world and contributed to China's snubbing at the Cairo
conference. The importance of family ties is evident in this film; the family unit ties the
Chinese people together throughout hardship. One of the underlying theme of the film is
the underlying corruption and violence of humanity - in the worst of times, the film
argues, you see the worst in people. Repeatedly in the film there are examples of
predation, profiteering, prostitution, and outright murder/abuse that stem from the
famine, that has defeated a corrupt government's efforts to defeat it and has led to
anarchy in Henan. In an interesting comment on leadership, at one point Chiang remarks
"Give the order, and my men would leap into action. I had no burdens," describing his
frustrations at working with his staff. In terms of connections to Leites and Wolfenstein,
there are comments to be made about the family unit - parents and lovers are incredibly
important, but here the love if out of fexpediency (in one scene a man and wife get
married to that when the wife sells herself into slavery, her children would have a father),
and actual romance and love takes a backseat.
75. Lost in Thailand (2012)
Lost in Thailand is a follow up to the 2010 film Lost on Journey, which has the
same general plot. An uptight, driven businessman goes on a trip with a clear objective in
mind, but is both waylaid and aided by à naïve, childlike, sidekick who works a lower
class job but serves as a moral compass and helps ground the businessman back to
reality through homespun wisdom. The businessman, through a series of adventures,
learns how to draw wealth and meaning from his relationships and not just through
business. In this film, the plot largely revolves around a businessman traveling in
Thailand to find his boss to sign a contract; themes of loyalty and companionship are
prevalent, as is the soul-searching about what gives meaning to life that one finds in other
Chinese films. In terms of themes from the W+L study, there are few here and this is a
comedy. However, there are a number of references to current Chinese society that might
point to what filmgoers experience in' their daily lives: weibo, massages, travel tour
companies, franchised companies, and a fascination with other Asian countries.
76. Qin Jia Guo Nian (2012)
A lighthearted film about two families. (of a young married couple) meeting for the
Lunar New Year celebration, this film accurately captures the tension among many
families in modern China. Exploring themes like wealth disparity, male/female gender
roles in a relationship, anxiety over housing, long lost loves, and other themes, this is a
rich trove of material about the state of modern Chinese relationships. Parents play a
huge role in relationships, as do houses and fighting over real estate property. Here, in
the terminology used by W+L, the family you make is just as important as the family from
where you come. A side story in this film is the hero's mother finding out what happened
to her first love, and couples are shown as needing to strike fast and jump on
opportunities to love unless they are willing to lose them.
77. The Great Magician (2011)
This film is a quasi-historical about the relationship between magic and politics in
historical China. Set after the Chinese Revolution, different warlords use magic to raise
armies, support or fight the Qing remnant, and fight each other. There is the theme of
foreign collaboration - as the Qing remnant seeks help from the Japanese to be restored.
In this film we see a few character archetypes - including a masculine girl who is very
capable but gets herself into trouble by enticing the wrong man. In this film greed and
loyalty compete against each other as characters maneuver for control of the country but
try to remain loyal to their own morality and to their friends.
78. Stand by Me (2011)
Stand by Me explores relationships among young adults in China - trying to think
through the interplay between work and relationships. In this film, two couples deal with
the everyday challenges of professional life in China. They deal with timidity in the face
of bosses, desire to emigrate for better opportunities, the problem of finding a job that
will lead to a career, and of finding a career that will lead to a stable life. One interesting
scene in this film is a portrayal of a Chinese business meeting, where all 'discussion' was
read from a script instead of it being an open forum. In this film one might see a twist on
the types of misconstrued situations referenced in the W+L book - because all the
characters speak Chinese, there are constant mistakes of hearing and people think
romantic things are happening that are in reality not.
79. Overheard 2 (2011)
This is a police drama involving greedy capitalism and corruption - themes the
government holds dear. A Hong Kong film, the film is about stock manipulation, with the
"bosses" of scheme making fortunes from market manipulation. Interestingly, the main
antagonist is shown as being very caring to his mother - even caring for her in person at
the nursing home. Despite being a 'bad' person by many standards, even he retains filial
piety - although this is not in the tradition referenced by W+L in their volume. This film
also references the idea of greedy capitalists and what might be a love/hate relationship
with capitalism.
80. Underdog Knight (2011)
The main character of this film was a life-saver in the Chinese Navy but was
injured and suffered from a serious brain injury, leaving him mentally incapacitated but
still physically able. He becomes a crime-fighter abiding by the basic characteristics of a
good person: be a good person for the nation, be a man of good taste, be tough, be noble,
and serve the nation. His girlfriend, characteristic of a 'good person,' stayed with him
despite the injury. There is a somewhat ambiguous message about vigilantism in Chinese
society as Lao San operates in a purely unofficial capacity. Violence here serves a
purpose - unlike the violence described in W+L that seems to be motivated by personal
relations or by love, violence in this film is used for the purpose of upholding the public
morality.
81. The Founding of a Party (2011)
Another propaganda film sponsored by the Chinese government (and full of major
Chinese film stars), this film documents the founding of the Chinese Communist Party. A
laudatory piece establishing the moral and political legitimacy of the party, this film did
not appear to do well at the marketplace and the government ended up distributing large
numbers of free tickets. This film demonstrates the government's desire for the Chinese
public-at-large to feel patriotic about their motherland and leaders - this film legitimizes
the Chinese Communist Party by showing the chaos it had to fight against. Here again,
violence is planned as a means of ensuring goals are met. There are few romantic or
familial relationships of the kind W+L. would. consider, although one might take Mao
Zedong as the father of the nation - in this sense, he is a wise, calm, and humble leader
who takes on no airs and seems to care deeply for the common people.
82. 1911 (2011)
This film about loyalty, duty, and sacrifice follows the events of the 1911 Xinhai
Rebellion, in another historical epic about the founding of China. It starred Jackie Chan
and a cast of young, revolutionary firebrands. One interesting quote from this film was
about who might lead a revolution: "Overseas Chinese are the heart of the revolution."
While the revolutionaries take the fight. to the enemy (strong nations are born of
revolution), Sun Yat Sen takes the case to the US State Department to ask for aid - which
was denied. Something else the film references is evolution - "All the strongest countries
were born of revolution"; to succeed, China must not follow the old ways but move
forward into the future. Violence, again, is used as a political tool as a means to remove
the foreigners preying on China; the corrupt foreigners meet to plan for what to do about
China, and they are told "This is the last time you can gather to feast on China." Loyalty
here is both to individuals (leaders) and to the Chinese 'nation' - not the official
government, and it is supported by the fact that the moral cause they follow is superior to
the moral cause of their leaders and government.
83. Dear Enemy (2011)
Dear Enemy addresses the themes of work/life balance for young professional
Chinese. It adds balanced between relationships and family - while a young woman
should find a husband, the idea is that she should not forget her parents and family. In
this film, filial responsibility takes precedence over both business and relationships, as the
main female character drops nearly everything to go home for her father's 60'n birthday.
This is similar to the W+L depiction of father/daughter relationships, when they note
"tender relations with fathers can be more fully elaborated in the case of daughters" (p.
123); there is no devaluing of their relationship whatsoever. With respect to the actual
content in the film - there are strong deception themes with respect to how the heroine's
firm conducts business. They enact plans to acquire (or prevent the acquisition of) a
mining company. And yet, characters lament how business relationships seem to them to
be so much simpler than romantic relationships:
84. Demon 2 (2011)
This is a Hong Kong film about the effects of drug addiction and trafficking on
families; in essence a morality tale about the dangers of drugs and how they destroy
lives. The characters in this film suffer from breakdowns due to drugs - and unlike in the
W+L book where some attribution may be given to their parents (p. 122), here the
attribution is given to the declining global economy combined with weak minds/morals.
While parents still love children in their times of trial (and children still love their parents
despite having drug problems), they by no means coddle them.
85. White Vengeance (2011)
Another historical epic, this film considers court intrigue and politics at the Feast
of the Red Gate. Involving betrayal, authority, and rebellion, the film covers similar
themes to most other wuxia or historical films. This films describes conflict between
brothers who compete for rule of China - here there is sibling rivalry similar to that
described by W+L (p. 143). But unlike in the W+L study, there is little guilt about it. The
themes of loyalty and rebellion come through again, and violence is planned through
deception (a plot at a feast that all the major characters attend).
86. Eternal Moment (2011)
This film might be called innovative jcinema in China - rather than one story,
Eternal Moment shows three short vignettes about love, with each trying to show the
meaning of an eternal moment. The film tries to understand what gives love meaning and
how to keep it; the first story is about finding, love but becoming bored with it, the second
story is about losing love, and the final story is about finding it again. Voyeurism plays a
major role in the first segment; the man (in the relationship) watches the woman through
a telescope. The second story has an example of divorce, which is relatively rare in
Chinese film. Finally, mistresses are in the third vignette - demonstrating that mistresses
may or may not be hidden from the rest of Chinese society. Themes from the W+L book
do not seem to match much of the content in this film despite a match in topic areas.
87. Love for Life (2011)
The AIDS Villages of Henan have rarely been addressed in public, but this film
appears to confront it head on. Portraying a group of AIDS victims who contracted the
disease from a blood merchant, the film shows how two of them find love in the face of
adversity and discrimination. In an ironic twist, the man who sold the blood then turned
around and made his living selling coffins once the government banned blood trading.
The love-hate relationship with capitalism is shown quite clearly as capitalism may have
led to freedom to do things - but freedom to be harmed as well. The hero and heroine in
the film does not fit any of the character types from the W+L book. Generally, the film
sends a moralistic message about capitalism rather than focusing on character
relationships.
88. A Beautiful Life (2011)
In A Beautiful Life, an alcoholic real estate agent in an abusive relationship
meets an off-duty police officer, beginning a film oriented around the theme of rescue.
There are continual scenes of him coming to her rescue and refusing to sleep with her,
and the film portrays loneliness in China. In a bustling society, it is often impossible to
make meaningful connections. The heroine in this film is frustrated with Chinese gender
norms, asking a male "You think women can only climb onto a bed?" rather than
climbing, for example, the corporate ladder. This film shows karaoke as a means of
relaxation and social bonding, as well as police officers as stalwart supporters of the
moral good and doing what is right. The police officer here rescues the heroine on a
number of occasions, supports her in starting a business, and when he uses his official
car to support her in some capacity, he willingly accepts that he must resign. The twist in
this film is that the police officer develop's a memory-loss disorder - the heroine ends up
taking care of him. He remarks, "I didn't think a girl from Hong Kong could be so loyal."
Like other romantic films, this does not have "a, direct relation to the W+L themes but
follows in a similar vein.
89. Don't Go Breaking My Heart (2011)
This film references alcoholism and how it destroys human productivity, as well as
how it affects relationships. A woman meets an alcoholic architect and helps him recover;
at the same time, she is courted by an important businessman from the building across
the street. Voyeurism plays a role in this film (important to W+L), as the businessman
watches the heroine from his office window. Dating is shown in this film, but western style
as couples go out for coffee. Western themes and references pervade this film; when
characters are upset they seek western liquors (Black Label and red wine) and western
meals. The moral lesson is that shallow and materialistic love will lose out to love borne
out of true emotions and caring. The question here (and with other films) is whether any
of this is truly relatable to the average Chinese middle-class person watching. Buying
plane tickets on a whim and drinking copious amounts of red wine might not be possible
for most Chinese.
polic
90. The Sorcerer and the White Snake (2011)
This film deals with magic and demons - interestingly, with reliance on what
appears to be Chinese mythology. In this film good and evil magic do battle, with good
winning in the end. Various characters take the form of demons battling it out with
humans fighting on the side of good. This film could be categorized with other films in the
historical period in thinking about loyalty and duty. The protagonist of this film is a
woman - a demon - dressed in red and a seductress, a "bad girl" in the W+L tradition.
Whereas the paranormal is common in American films, it is rare in Chinese films and it is
portrayed as not being strange or odd; it seems utterly normal to have demon hunters
around. Order is also an important part of this film, as the goal of the demon hunter is to
eliminate demons that might threaten the stability of an otherwise harmonious society.
:
1
91. Great Wall My Love (2012)
A strong independent professional woman takes a trip to the Great Wall on the
occasion of her father's death. Perhaps most importantly, this film highlights the desire of
many Chinese to return to their "laojid" -' in a nation of migrants, returning to your
hometown is important, and the woman's father wishes her to take him before his death to
find a lost love. Along the way, she is shadowed by a private eye (who also has a lost
love) with whom she falls in love. Another side story this film explores is the tension
between mainland and Taiwan and how people relate. This film clearly demonstrates
filial piety - and the relationship between the heroine and her father closely follows the
warm relationships W+L describe. And how, does one find love? In this film, it is through
shared experiences, which seem to sustain the relationship. The woman in this film drives
the relationship forward (with the private eye) and he does much to take care of her while
they travel.
92. I Do (2012)
A film with a 30 year old professional woman trying to find love, this film
emphasizes western behaviors - drinking Grey Goose at bars, for example. The primary
conflict in this film is between the woman and two men - one who left her and moved to
the United States when they were dating in the past, and a divorcee who she has been
dating. In the end, dedication wins out and she chooses the divorcee over the man who
abandoned her once already. These are theme's not really discussed in the W+L study.
This film is interesting in showing online dating as a legitimate means of meeting people
in China; there is also some discussion of divorce - an acknowledgment that it happens
and seems to be rising. This film also underscores the tension between work and play -
the woman in this film works incredibly hard to the detriment of any relationships in her
life
93. An Inaccurate Memoir (2012)
An Inaccurate Memoir is set in the Chinese west, which is a growing motif in
Chinese film. This movie is about bandits, heists, resisting the Japanese - themes of
ruggedness, masculinity, brotherhood, and trickery shine in this film. The west, when,
portrayed in these films, seems to be lawless and in development - without authority (or
in the face of corrupt authority), the common people rely on themselves. The heroine in
this film is a vamp in the W+L tradition. Ancestor worship is portrayed, with the bandit
group praying to their ancestors to honor them; for a society that in many respects
stamped out organized (or unorganized) religion in the mid-20th century, it is striking to
see it acknowledged in film. Any romantic messages contained in this film are lost in the
revolutionary and independent plot devices.
94. Summer Love (2012)
nemes
Another film with three vignettes, this film is also about love. However, in this case
the vignettes take up a small portion of the film and serve to introduce the three main
characters: one effeminate man obsessed with women (earnest but unsuccessfully so), a
man who can only communicate via' text message, and a man obsessed with powerful
women. They end up on Hainan and try their luck at falling in love; the film is somewhat
of a commentary on modern Chinese society but the content seems too shallow to take
seriously. The character from the first vignette has a younger sister (another example of a
sibling relationship that is hard to find) whom he loves very much. The implication here is
that an aggressive approach is not okay with women - the women these men encounter
are all confident and have no problem being successful on their own; women drive
relationships forward in Chinese film by allowing them to happen - they are the pursued,
not the pursuer.
95. Design of Death (2012)
This film is set in 1940s rural China and includes a number of mystical elements.
With a plague ravaging a rural village; a doctor from the government must travel (in the
Chinese west) to the village. to administer care. While there, he was charged with
dispatching a man who is seemingly impervious to violence. One of the few films set in
the 'modern' era that deals with anything other than reality. This film is another in those
about the Chinese 'west' - that lawless region of China far away from government
control.
96. The Locked Door (2012)
False appearances (in the W+L tradition) play a large role in this film set in
1930s Shanghai. Of particular importance is a scene where the main female character
appears to have been raped by a homeless man, but in reality was assaulted by a group of
young rich men. False appearances ruined her life, and she ends up forming a
relationship with the former homeless man as it is her only recourse. This film's themes,
apart from those about appearances, focus on the dangers of class power in China; when
upper classes gain too much power (such as in the case of these young rich men), they
are able to abuse the poor - who have no choice but to accept their fate.
97. A Simple Life (2012)
This film explores the plight of the' elderly in modern China - a household maid/
servant lives with a man whom she has taken care of since he was a child. The film is a
series of flashbacks of her retiring, moving to a nursing home, and passing away. Along
the way, the viewer is shown how the' man takes care of her, takes her to meals, and
generally treats her better than almost all of the older people are treated in the group
home. This film depicts filial piety better than films about actual mother-son
relationships; the man in this film has a very close relationship with his caretaker. W+L
describe how psychoses among main characters could be traced to "overindulgence by
the father or deprivation by the mother" (p. 122) - the main character here is incredibly
successful, and his caretaker/mother figure indulges him in whatever he wants.
98. Flying Swords of Dragon Gate (2012)
This film is a fairly standard historical drama with corrupt eunuchs, a people's
hero rebellion against the Emperor, "court" Mandarin, and a cat and mouse game
between members of the court for influence and control. There is a strong implication in
this film that women are property - which is different from many Chinese films where
women drive relationships forward. Deception plays a huge role in this film, as does
rebellion, but with respect to W+L themes, they are relatively few.
99. Million Dollar Crocodile (2012)
A massive escaped crocodile that has eaten a massive brick of euros (in cash) is
chased by a number of parties, in this Chinese take on a monster movie. Despite the
superficial premise, there are some interesting points to be made about expatriates and
the Chinese mindset. Of particular interest is the emphasis on cash, the characters in the
movie work with cash because they "can't touch or feel ... money in a bank." This film
shows some of the dangers of capitalism; capitalism has given people freedom to seek out
business opportunities, but these sometimes will hurt the environment.
100. The Resurrection: Painted Skin (2012)
Conflict in relationships and deception is at the core of this film. Relating the
story of a fox demon in her quest to achieve mortality by finding a true love, the film
portrays a general and a princess who reunite after 18 years of separation, with the fox
demon creating the third leg of the triangle: (as referenced in Leites' work). Key to this
film is seduction - both the princess and the demon seek to seduce the general, the
concept of seduction referenced heavily, in the Leites and Wolfenstein work. Here,
seduction is based not on love but on looks, and it is only at the end of the film that the
characters realize looks should be put aside (the general blinds himself). The fox demon
must find a love (and stop seducing, for her life is sustained by seducing men and eating
their hearts) to truly become happy; seduction is a means of survival (physical), but to
survive emotionally she needs actual meaning in love instead of just looks.
EARLIER FILMS
Queen of Sports (1934)
Queen of Sport is an early silent film depicting a young woman
who enters a sports training academy in Shanghai, by way of
her small town laojia in Zhejiang. This film is overtly
moralistic - the filmmakers view physical fitness as extremely
important for the strength of the nation. Interestingly, they
push back against the competitive aspects of sport; when the
young woman beings winning competitions, she falls out of
discipline - she starts going to bars, disregarding her
teachers, and performing poorly. It was only by returning to
the essence of sport - non-competitive and for personal
strength - that she was able to succeed.
"Why China is not strong, the chief reason is our bodies
1
1
:
are weak"
"The essence of sport is to keep everyone's body
developing and to popularize it to the whole society - not
to make a few individual heroes"
Song of the Fishermen (1934)
Song of the Fishermen is another silent leftist film from the
Golden Age of Chinese cinema. Another example of leftist
cinema, this film considers the plight of the poor class - as
compared to the rich. It is notable for the use of Ave Maria -
the operatic theme - in the soundtrack, repeating it again and
again. The use of western musical and media forms to express
essentially Chinese themes is interesting, and continues into the
Communist era.
"Why can't we live a simple life?"
"We are living a hard life in the countryside"
The Goddess (1934)
The Goddess is an example of Chinese silent film - hugely
popular films produced in the 1930s when film was still a new
industry in China. This is an example of leftist film in that
period; films that portrayed the plight of the poor, the
repression of the upper classes and government, and the need
for social change to address these problems. This film is
interesting in having a female character as its lead - and a
female prostitute at that - something that would largely
disappear as the Communist era began.
National Customs (1935) "
National Customs has a classic love triangle - two sisters who
are in love with the same cousin. Both sisters move to Shanghai
for their education. While one focuses on her studies and
excels, the other spends more time wearing fine clothes and
applying makeup. The moral message in this story is that such
extravagance is a waste; that when the nation is in danger,
Chinese must focus on protecting China - to educate
themselves for the good of the nation. This, along with other
leftist cinema of the 1930s, was intended to inspire the Chinese
people to unite and throw out the various foreign powers
occupying China at the time.
"If all the country folk in China emulated the modern city
people and cast off their virtues of frugality and
industriousness, that would be the end of the nation!"
"When the nation is in danger, imminent disaster is
everywhere ... we still have the idea to wear makeup and
jewelry, to be modern romantics"
The Big Road (1935)
The Big Road is an early silent film centered on national
defense. While the "enemy" is never mentioned, the film quite
openly refers to the Japanese invasion of northern China, and
Chinese peasants' attempts to build a road for the nationalist
army to travel to the north to fight the Japanese. One
important theme in this film is the nobility of work - the
laobaixing in this film work hard at building the road, but they
do it without complaint for the good of the country. The
peasantry of future Chinese films follow this example; working
away in distilleries, farms, fishing fleets, the army, etc - all in
the service of China.
"I have no children to sell///All I've got is me and my
vagrant flower-drum life"
Song at Midnight (1937)
Song at Midnight has one of the most complex plots in early
Chinese film - although that does not say much for Chinese
filmmakers themselves, as this film is actually an adaptation of
the French story, Phantom of the Opera. Of course, there is a
Chinese spin - while following the same basic story line, the
filmmakers could not resist inserting some political statements,
casting the major villain in the last act as a landlord who must
be defeated for the "freedom for the public."
"You are youth with hot liver, courage, and hope"
"We should work harder to struggle and break this dark
influence, fight for the freedom of the
public" (shoehorned political speech toward the end of
the film)
Street Angel (1937)
This film is one of the first sound films to come out in China - it
is an example of 1930s leftist film, intending to portray the
social evils present in China society, and arguing that they
needed to be fixed. Particularly, this film focuses on the plight
of the poor, looking at two young women; one, forced into
prostitution, the other sold by her adoptive parents to a local
gangster. While this film touches on many of the same themes
as later Communist-era films, it does so in a far more subtle
way.
"Do you really want a man to take care of domestic
affairs?"
Princess Iron Fan (1941)
This film is the first premiere-length animated film in Chinese
film history. It adapts an episode from Journey to the West - a
classic Chinese novel also portrayed in the 1961 film, Havoc in
Heaven - and employs extensive Rotoscoping. The influence of
Disney techniques and artistic forms is evident in the
animation. The film emphasizes slapstick humor and
deception/cleverness as the primary important themes.
"Scriptures are the principles that link heaven and
earth ... Only he who holds these principles can rid himself
of pain and live a good life, live a true and honest life"
"The reason that we've been defeated is that we haven't
been working together"
Spring in a Small Town (1948)
Spring in a Small Town is one of the most significant films to be
released during the Chinese Civil War prior to the Communist
takeover. This film is a story of love triangle in a small town
ruined by the Sino-Japanese War. An invalid landowner,
attended by his disinterested wife, his loyal servant, and his
younger system, is visited by his old friend - a western-dressed
