doctor who happens to be his wife's former lover. One could
read this film in different ways - one interpretation could be
that the filmmaker was exploring the tension between the old
and the new China. The old China - the invalid - was dying
slowly of a consumptive disease, while the new China - the
doctor - represented a new breed of manliness. The film might
be a broader allegory for the rise of the Communists.
"He became my husband. I serve him. I have to serve him
faithfully. My heart is yours. But I feel sorry for Liyan.
What would have me do?"
Liu Hulan (1950)
Liu Hulan is a propaganda film about a Chinese revolutionary
martyr - a 14 year old girl named Liu Hulan who was
decapitated by Nationalist soldiers after refusing to recant her
Communist ideology in a small town square. The film is a
biopic that spins the Communist party in a favorable light; it
shows landlords collaborating with Japanese oppressors, and
in by now a familiar story, the common people joining together
with the Communist soldiers (the Eighth Route Army) to
throw back the invaders. The culmination of the film, when Liu
Hulan sacrifices herself in front of her neighbors, is meant to
be an inspirational scene designed to unite viewers together in
a common sense of Chinese unity.
"These are all from our sweat and efforts, they should
belong to us"
"All poor people will finally have hope. Today, I joined
the Communist party."
This Life of Mine (1950)
This Life of Mine is a good example of early Communist film;
chronicling the life of a policeman in Beijing during the
revolutions, wars, and political infighting of the Chinese Civil
War. The familiar themes of impotence against western
powers, the decay of the old society, and the CCP's role in
bringing glory back to China are visited here.
"Those big whigs eat freely, buy things freely ... What
have they ever done for the people?"
"Are you Chinese or not? Do you want to be ruled be
foreigners?"
Liu San Jie (1961)
This film relates a folk story about "third sister Liu" from the
1
Guangxi autonomous region. This is a musical film describing
conflict between ordinary peasants and landlords. Like many
films, it denounces landlords as rapacious, oppressive, and
calculating. The peasants rage at the landlords: "The rage in
our hearts cannot be checked." Liu San Jie defeats the
landlords in singing contests and upon capture, disappears by
jumping into a river and swimming away.
"The landlord is proud and arrogant. He raises a knife to
kill me at midnight"
"We have no share of the worldly wealth, but folk songs
belong to we poor"
"You're merely the landlord's dog under the table ... How
many bones can you eat?"
The Red Detachment of Women (1961)
This is a film celebrating the heroism of female members of the
PLA during the fighting against the Nationalists in the early
1930s. One of the main messages of this film is that all Chinese
- men and women alike - suffered from the excesses of the old
society, which must be destroyed to usher in a Communist-led
new society. The familiar themes of sacrifice, duty, and
martyrdom show up here. This film is interesting for
portraying Chinese women - while they are independent, they
are still subordinate to men.
"I will kill the officials ... I will rip off their dog skins ... "
"You think you are the only one with animosity? Every
proletarian feels it in their hearts"
Serfs (1963)
Serfs is a clear propaganda film that pushes forward the
narrative that the Chinese Communist Party is the unifier of
China and liberator of the oppressed. This film is set in Tibet,
where the local feudal customs were still in place ahead of the
PLA invasion. Upon liberation, the serfs throw aside their
chains and are finally free. This film is interesting in its use of
deception - the rebel feudal lords in Tibet utilize deception
techniques to fight the PLA, but the PLA - with its moral
supremacy - has no need for such tactics and wins handily.
"Today I see China gathering together and growing stronger
and wealthier"
The East is Red (1965)
This propaganda film is unique among major Chinese films - it
is a musical. This film tells the party's version of 20th century
Chinese history; it traces the founding of the Chinese
Communist Party, the war against the Japanese, the victory in
the civil war, and the founding of the PRC - along with all the
attendant suffering the Party and the Chinese people
experienced before their liberation. Unity is the major theme of
this film - unity of the Chinese people against reactionaries and
foreign aggressors in building a new China. One of the most
interesting aspects of this film is its seeming relation to Beijing
operatic forms in the dancing and singing.
"The Communist Party is like the sun ... Wherever the
Communist Party is the people are liberated"
""Without the Communist Party, no new China"
The Burning of Yuan Ming Yuan (1983)
This is a propaganda film covering the familiar themes of
China's exploitation by foreign powers, the corruption and
impotence of the late Imperial period, and the heroism of
China's peasants in doing what they could to resist the
"bandits." This film describes the burning of the Imperioal
gardens in Beijing during the Second Opium War. It is unusual
for its depiction of (historically inaccurate) brutality by
western forces in sacking Beijing.
"Her lust for dictatorship still burns like a flame even
after death"
"He was a disgrace to his ancestors ... He put the people's
lives at the hands of the savage enemy troops"
Yellow Earth (1984)
Yellow Earth is a prime example of Fifth Generation
filmmaking. One of the hallmarks of the Fifth Generation is a
move away from traditional narrative forms - with emphasis
on story and dialogue - and moving toward storytelling by
cinematography, form, and color. With a minimalistic color
palette and sweeping scenes of a desolate countryside, Chen
Kaige and Zhang Yimou turn what could be a traditional piece
of Communist propaganda - a story of a CCP cadre visiting a
peasant family in hopes of finding folk songs - into a story
about the ambiguity of human power. The Communists will
come to save us, the peasants sing, as they perform a rain
dance to alleviate a major drought. The main story - a peasant
girl being sold to another family to debts - could be viewed as
an allegory about the changes China faced in the early 1980s;
would China enter the future or remain in its past?
"If a boy gets married it's happiness; if a girl gets
married it's sadness"
Red Sorghum (1987)
This film is one of the seminal achievements of the Fifth
Generation - that generation of directors who graduated from
the Bejing Film Academy in 1982 - and it is the debut of Zhang
Yimou, one of the Fifth Generation's most famous figures. This
is a film that evokes passion, love, emotion, and the desire to
feel - focusing on a portrayal of peasant life at a sorghum
liquor distillery just before (and during) the Sino-Japanese
War. This film shows what directors WANTED to portray, but
were kept from portraying, during the Cultural Revolution.
"A name is as important to a liquor as it is to a human
being"
The Horse Thief (1988)
This film, The Horse Thief, has very little dialogue. Rather, it is
a view of how China's minorities are portrayed by the CCP.
This film is more of an ethnographic look - portraying the
sweeping panoramas of Tibet, and how a small Tibetan village
deals with crime and punishment. The film is ambivalent about
it; it portrays a primitive society practicing traditional values -
neither suggesting that it is bad or that the modern way is
better.
"The good are rewarded with good ... If he's reincarnated,
his life will be good. Yes, the vultures won't touch the
evil"
"Norbu has offended god. He stole the officials' temple
gifts. The government wants strict punishment ... We'll go
according to our rules. The punishment will be the exile.
He has to leave our town. Forever."
Farewell My Concubine (1993)
Farewell My Concubine is an epic that spans a half century of
Chinese history, examining some of the most turbulent
historical episodes of the 20th century. The film follows two
main characters - two opera singers trained from childhood to
practice Beijing Opera. The film is at once an historical epic
portraying the Japanese invasion, the Chinese civil war, the
rise of the Communist Party, and the chaos of the Cultural
Revolution - but at the same time a melodrama exploring the
relationship between the two actors. The film was at first
banned in China - its themes of preserving individualism and
idiosyncrasy in the face of historical forces and fate did not sit
well with Party censors, nor did its portrayal of the Red
Guards' actions during the Cultural Revolution. This is one of
the core works of the Fifth Generation of filmmakers - and was
one of the first Chinese films to attract wide attention outside
of China.
"The Communists still have to have opera ... We're just
waiting to see what their money looks like?"
''Only execution can satisfy the People's anger"
Frozen (1996)
Frozen is a unique film in the Chinese canon - it seems
like more of a reflection of what a small group of artists
in Beijing were feeling in the early 1990s rather than any
type of grand political statement or even a standard film.
It describes the plan of a discontented performance
artist to commit suicide by freezing himself to death -
representing the numbness of life. This film was banned
in China, and it reflects the discontent of many Chinese
artists during the period.
"That's no reason to arrest them, they only
expressed themselves"
"The hygiene of the mind is more important than
keeping China's streets clean"

15. Appendix 2: Some themes in John Weaklands work and mini-lit review
Review - J.H. Weakland's work on China and Chinese film
Weakland is well-known for his contributions to family therapy and psychology, but he
also contributed to the study of China., Weakland's concerns himself with the use of
imagery in Chinese mass media, particularly in film. In work for the US government and
in work in academic journals, Weakland considered major themes in Chinese film in the
1960s, applying psycho-cultural or socio-cultural research methods to think about the
meaning of film in the broader contexts of Chinese society. This review will survey
Weakland's major works in the area of China and in film.
Working with Margaret Mead in the early 1950s, Weakland considered images of family
in Chinese film. When watching films, he emphasized "a striking shift of mood; a
repetition of some feature, object, expression, or detail of structure; emphasis in
characterization or custom; or details of technique, such as camera work in beginning and
ending scenes" (Weakland, 1953a, 292). It is unclear whether at this point he understood
spoken Chinese, or relied on English translations of synopses and conversations with
Chinese-speaking associates.
In seven Cantonese films, he considered two major areas: roles and relations in the family
and time perspectives. With regard to the family, he observed that "although China is a
country where the ideal family is a very large one, a complete family seldom appears, and
an extended family is not shown" (Weakland, 1953a, 292). The core of the films are a
man-woman relationship, and fathers and mothers are not shown, along with siblings or
children. Young men are shown as ineffectual, dependent upon olden men for their
fortunes and is dependent upon them: Young women follow a given role strictly, but
holds the powerful weapon of public announcement of mistreatment (although always
against a situation and not a person) (Weakland, 1953a, 292-293). With respect to time
perspectives, Weakland observes how ' repetition is a prominent feature and the
"importance of destiny and the inescapable weight of circumstances are
stressed" (Weakland, 1953a, 294). He suggests this might mean the Chinese themselves
view destiny as inescapable and impossible to change.
In another piece in the same volume, Weakland applies his understanding of Chinese
family structure to China's role in international affairs. He notes that Chinese Communist
discussion of international affairs is not in pure Marxist terms, it includes family imagery.
He demonstrates how Chinese writing describes international affairs as "two families of
brothers" (Weakland, 1953b, 422), with the Soviet Union and the United States as the
heads of the families. How would China react if its older brother asked it to do
something?
In a 1966 note on Chinese film derived from research funded by the Office of Naval
Research, Weakland describes major themes in Chinese Communist film, which at the
time was strongly controlled by the state" Between 1962-1964 he viewed 17 films
produced by Communist producers, with 11 made in China and 6 in Hong Kong. The
:
films were dated 1956-1962 and represented a number of genres, including adaptations of
Chinese opera, films of historical or ancient China, and some looks at modern China
(Weakland, 1966, 478). He identifies four main stages of Chinese historical development
manifest in the films: (1) The Old Chinese Society and Its Evils; (2) Transitional
Struggles for Social Change; (3) The New Chinese Society and Its Virtues; and (4)
Continuing Social Tasks and Future Goals (Weakland, 1966, 479). The themes portrayed
are oriented around these stages.
These political themes include (1) The Feudalistic Family; (2) The Liberation of Women;
(3) The Liberation of Youth; (4) Reactionary Figures - officials and landlords; (5)
Revolutionary Figures; (6) "Anti-individualism"; (7) Education and Training; (8)
Minority Peoples; and (9) Invaders (Weakland, 1966, 479-481). Weakland relates these
themes together in a broader cultural pattern)-"behavior and social relationships based
on personal initiatives and responsibility, if conceived at all, are evaluated as uncertain
and dangerous in outcome" (Weakland, 1966, 482). Individualism is overtly attacked and
irresponsible; behavior should be ordered according to formal, established, non-personal
rules of social role, guided by authority figures.
In addition to thinking about general themes, Weakland also focuses on specific themes
and how films use imagery to portray them. In a 1968 report for the Office of Naval
Research, Weakland considers 22 feature films produced in China between 1949-1965
and explores images of invasion, resistance to invasion, and Chinese attitudes toward
foreign imperialism (Weakland 1968, i). While Weakland does not specify an explicit
methodological approach, he is generally concerned with non-rational socio-cultural
factors in film that could speak to broader attitudes.
Weakland identifies a number of occurrences and broad themes in these films. First, he
notes that most films regularly center on young women, with commonly oppressed as
helpless victims of invaders. There also seems to be a parallel between the concept of
arranged marriage and the husband being an invader into a woman's life and the broader
idea of China's foreigners serving as intrusive fathers/husbands. In this sense, China
serves as a maternal figure in the eyes of ordinary Chinese. Weakland, however, notes
that as a baseline, it would be valuable to study American attitudes as reflected in film -
similar views could be held there that do not make Chinese attitudes out of the ordinary
(Weakland, 1968, ii and 32). Out of this report, Weakland also published an article in
1971 on the same topic. He reports the same general results and way forward (Weakland,
1971a).
He suggested Hong Kong films as relevant as much as he did mainland films and in 1971
proposed that Hong Kong be used as a, laboratory for the process of social change and
development, particularly when compared to Taiwan and the mainland. Specifically, he
proposes to study film - as "films offer a cultural distillate that otherwise is rare in large
societies, and that has many advantages for study. Films are composed of interrelated
concrete images of individual behavior in social situations of common
concern" (Weakland, 1971b, 239). While they are fiction, social organization and
behavior are rarely based strictly on fact. Films exemplify culturall structured perceptions
and responses and provide commonly shared interpretations of society (Weakland, 1971b,
1
239-240).
Weakland's interest in imagery is not restricted to film. He also considers family imagery
(his great interest) in writing - specifically by Mao Tse-Tung. In one passage, Mao was
concerned with the evolution of Chinese social structure with the end of feudalism - the
crux of his argument is:
..
The invasion of foreign capitalism broke up with [feudal] economy of the
Chinese society. This invasion of foreign capitalist, on the one hand,
destroyed the self-sufficiently natural economy of China, the town
handicraft and the rural home handicraft; whilst on the other hand, it had
promoted the development of the town and rural commodity economy.
Using a content analysis approach, Weakland considers Maos' general form of expression
and its interpretation with respect to his thoughts on family structures in general.
Weakland's interpretation after applying this approach is that the old Chinese society is a
female figure, a conception legendary in nature and scope, which was "invaded" or raped
by foreign capitalism (a male, paternal figure). This led to her gradual death but the birth
of twins - the Chinese proletariat and capitalist classes that formed a new society as the
old died (Weakland, 1958, 393). Weakland's thinking on Chinese family structures and
their role in society extended as far back as at least 1958, and in this article one can see
the origins of the thinking he later applied to film. Weakland followed up his report with
a 1967 report for the Office of Naval Research considering many of the same themes,
emphasizing the relevance of
Weakland's interest was broadly related to Chinese culture. In a 1950 article, he considers
the "organization of sequences of related activity in Chinese culture, the whole sphere of
"purposive" behavior" (Weakland, 1950, 361). His objective was to describe major
patterns in activity organization (relationships among elements of purposive, visible,
behavior) and to relate those toward an overall configuration of society.
Crucially, in Weakland's dealings with Chinese nationals, he observes "in the situations
described above that the preparations carried out or planned were ordinarily undisclosed
by the planner, not revealed to others who were to be involved, until the moment for them
to act arrived" (Weakland, 1951, 363). This echoes some Chinese cultural elements of
deception. He also describes the "extensive use of middlemen" in getting things done
(Weakland, 1951, 364), what in the modern era we would call guanxi. Planning in
secrecy and execution with middlemen are therefore major patterns in Chinese
organization.
Weakland also thought about the Chinese Cultural Revolution. He considers the possible
relationships the movement might have to Chinese culture more generally, considering
Maoist pronouncements on the movement; the"Red Guards, and the education revolution.
Weakland argues "although both the proponents and opponents of the Cultural
Revolution have emphasized its newness and novelty, it regularly displays reliance and
even increased emphasis on traditional cultural patterns (Weakland, 1969, i). Weakland
incorporates drama in his analysis with a long description of changes to stage drama and
.
opera (Weakland, 1969, 34-6). In all discussion of Chinese cultural elements Weakland
includes such analysis of live portrayal of drama.
In what way did the Cultural Revolution reflect traditional Chinese culture even though it
promoted so much that was new? Weakland cites the example of the Red Guards leading
a wide campaign to rename streets; schools, towns, etc. Names and naming has been very
important for an extremely long time in Chinese history; while promoting the new, the
Red Guards invoked the old (Weakland; 1969, 38). Weakland argues the Cultural
Revolution, with this sort of cultural revitalization, could represent the ultimate step in
Chinese revolution, to reconnect the old with the new.
Working with Ruth Bunzel, Weakland produced a report funded by the Office of Naval
Research in 1952 taking an anthropological approach to Chinese Communism and
exploring its similarities and differences with the Soviet model. This report did not utilize
film, although Nathan Leites and Martha Wolfenstein were both consulted in its drafting.
They consider three areas of traditional Chinese culture: "(1) traditional patterns of
thinking; (2) the organization of life experience, with special reference to the perception
of bodily function and family relationships, and (3) counter-tendencies inherent in
traditional Chinese culture or developed in it as a result of contact with the west" (Bunzel
and Weakland, 1952, i). They also note how "Chinese Communism is consciously
modeled on Soviety Communism (Bunzel and Weakland, 1952, x). Chinese Communism
is conditioned, in their view, by traditional Chinese thought blended with Soviet
Communist concepts.
Weakland's work with the Office of Naval Research also included research into film. He
was the PI for a research contracted done on "Chinese Communist Communication and
Values" from the Group Psychology Branch for the Office of Naval Research. He wrote a
series of six technical reports (all of which are referenced here) and submitted several as
academic publications (Weakland, 1970).
Weakland produced several other reports for ONR that have not been publically released;
the Defense Technical Information Center only digitized them upon request for this
report. A 1967 report based on 21 Chinese films considered family relationships. It is
important to note none of these were mainland films - Weakland's sample included six
from Hong Kong made under Chinese influence, 12 other Hong Kong films, and five
Taiwanese films (Weakland, 1967, 1). He emphasizes major themes that can be discerned
through the films, emphasizing the conflict between love and family relationships. He
considers the depicted nature of these conflicts, the sources to which conflicts are
ascribed, and their typical resolutions.
Interestingly, Weakland considers the methodology to be a straightforward - his aim was
to closely observe to discern repetitive main elements, and then compare Communist
Chinese and Taiwanese films.
Weakland reports that in all three groups, conflict had one main focus: children were in
conflict with parents about romantic attachments, but there were differences apart from
this (Weakland, 1967, 4). In the Communist Chinese group, the films either depict the
evils of "Old China" or the benefits of "New China" - Communism rescuing the Chinese
people (Ibid.). In "Old China," when young people come into conflict with parents, they
are always defeated, implying nothing can really change in the old system. This becomes
resolved in new China.
In Taiwanese films, there appears to be an emphasis on fate prescribing certain outcomes
(Ibid.). Occasionally negative outcomes are blamed on the Communists. Additionally,
Taiwanese films do not blame parents for conflict. They propose that the young should
adapt to the status quo, rather than in Chinese. film where the emphasis is on struggle
(Weakland, 1967, 6). Both groups advocate subordination of personal love to wider social
ends, but they differ in what those ends should be.
Weakland also reports the study has implications for understanding social change,
particularly in revolutionary situations. Rather than viewing there to be a dichotomy
between change or continuity, Weakland argues that change and continuity are
interrelated in situations of change and they should be considered hand in hand
(Weakland, 1967, 8).
In an earlier report, Weakland considered 17 films representing Communist Chinese film
output. He looked for what political themes were manifest in film and what cultural
themes are related to the political messages. Weakland identified nine main political
themes: (1) the feudalistic family, (2) the liberation of women, (3) the liberation of youth,
(4) reactionary figures (officials and landlords), (5) revolutionary figures, (6) anti-
individualism, (7) education and training, (8) invaders, (9) national minorities (Weakland,
1966, 1). These themes are interrelated, but Weakland identifies anti-individualism as
being particularly important. Interestingly, anti-individualism and several of the others
are old Chinese themes, not indicative of the Communists forcing new culture upon the
Chinese.
Weakland describes these themes in the context of strategic deterrence, which relies on an
adversary nation's interpretation of action. Being raised in a particular national culture
plays a large role in shaping perception and reality and effects the evaluation of any issue.
These may be implicit or explicit. To predict how a state will react to deterrence
operations, it is important to understand a, nation's way of viewing political events -
knowledge of basic premises of political' life is important (Weakland, 1966, 3-4). In
Weakland's view, "knowledge of more general Chinese Communist cultural system of
conceptions, attitudes, and premises (and something of its relationship with traditional
Chinese cultural patterns) ... is important for a clear view of domestic Chinese Communist
politics" (Weakland, 1966, 4). Films, therefore, present valuable information because
they serve as deliberate interpretation and systematization of content and pattern related
to culture. Weakland additionally argues that film is superior to literature as an object of
analysis. Films are more culturally representative because they are group projects and
intended for wide communication and present general and basic themes (Weakland, 1966,
6).
What kind of work did this research serve? Weakland used cultural analysis to better
understand Chinese Communist views and practices concerning strategy and negotiation.
He relies primarily on Chinese literature, including Sun Tzu and Lo Kuan-Chung, to
generate a sort of Chinese strategic culture (Weakland, 1970, 17-22). Weakland considers
relationships and social interaction were handled in these cultural artifacts to relate to
politico-military strategy. He claims for the Chinese Communists, the point of strategy
was to "enmesh the enemy in such contradictions until he is eventually defeated by his
inability to resolve them, while the Communists aim to win by taking positive strategic
advantage of contradictions" (Weakland, 1970, 36). There is a strong relation to Sun Tzu.
He further argues that because of this, the Chinese take an "adversary" approach to
negotiations. They view negotiation as another type of struggle
Differences from Leites
Weakland drew from the Wolfenstein and Leites volume when doing his own work.
These are the main themes Weakland identified from his study of film:
(1) the feudalistic family, (2) the liberation of women, (3) the liberation of youth, (4)
reactionary figures (officials and landlords), (5) revolutionary figures, (6)
antiindividualism, (7) education and training, (8) invaders, (9) national minorities
(Weakland, 1966, 1).
He also considers how in Chinese film, conflict had one main focus: children were in
conflict with parents about romantic attachments, but there were differences apart from
this (Weakland, 1967, 4). He also notes how there is a dichotomy between films depicting
"Old" and "New" China.
Some of Weakland's work differs slightly from Leites' in that it explicitly seeks a
political motivation to film, though the research is based on the psycho-cultural approach
used in the Wolfenstein and Leites book. Leites, on the other hand, emphasizes broader
themes like Weakland's less, focusing more on different types of characters and
relationships among them. Leites also considers certain plot devices and how they help to
show character interaction. Leites also seeks comparison across different countries where
Weakland considered only China.
Weakland does have significant work thinking about family relationships in film, so there
is some overlap. But where he considers political and strategy factors, Leites sticks to
thinking about relationships and characters. Weakland and Leites therefore emphasize
different aspects of the films. Leites focuses on the foundations in the film - characters
and relationships, while Weakland builds' on such analysis to relate film to larger
questions of theme and cultural strategy ::
